Life of Imam al-Ghazali
beginner
5 min
Name and Lineage of Imam al-Ghazali
Al-Ghazali's full name, kunya, and the controversy over his nickname.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Ghazali's full name, his kunya Abu Hamid, and the scholarly debate over the pronunciation of his nickname 'al-Ghazali'.

Core Idea
Most scholars pronounce 'al-Ghazali' with a shadda, linking it to his father's craft.

Before studying Imam al-Ghazali's theological works, it is helpful to know his background. This lesson explores his full name, his famous kunya, and the disagreement among scholars about the correct pronunciation of his surname. These details are not trivia; they show how a person's name can reflect lineage, profession, and regional origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on his full name.
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Notice the many titles and the chain of names. Pay attention to his kunya 'Abu Hamid' and the remark that he had no son named Hamid.
This highlights that a kunya can be honorific, not necessarily literal.
2
Identify the two opinions on the pronunciation of 'al-Ghazali'.
Look for the two views: with shadda (doubled z) and without (tashdid). Each view has a different reason. List them.
The shadda view derives from his father's profession; the other from a village name.
3
Understand the evidence for each view.
Notice that supporters of the shadda mention attribution to his father as a ghazzal (spinner), while supporters of the light pronunciation attribute it to the village Ghazala.
The passage does not give preference, but notes most scholars favor shadda.
4
Reflect on why this debate matters.
Consider why scholars argue about the correct pronunciation of a name. What does it tell us about the Imam's family and origins?
This shows how names carry historical and biographical significance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سيرة حياة الإمام الغزالي
اسمه ونسبه :
هو حجة الإسلام ، وفخر الأنام ، الإمام الحبر الهمام، زين الدين محمد بن محمد بن محمد بن أحمد الغزالي الطُّوسي الطابراني الشافعي
يكنى بأبي حامد ، وبهذه الكنية اشتهر ، وليس من ولده من اسمه حامد ؛ فالإمام لم يعقب سوى البنات
أما لقبه الغزالي .. ففيه الخلاف المشهور في كتب من ترجم له : أهو بتشديد الزاء أو تخفيفها ؟
فبين مرجّح للتشديد نسبة إلى والده الذي كان يعمل غزالاً ؛ على أن الياء لتأكيد النسبة ،
أو هي عادة أهل بلاده، أو للتمييز بين صاحب الحرفة والمنتسب إليه
وآخر رأى التخفيف ونسبه إلى غزالة كسحابة ؛ قرية من قرى طوس
ولكل قول مناصروه ، وغالبهم على التشديد
وطُوس ناحية بخراسان تشتمل على مدينتين ؛ إحداهما طابران بلد الإمام وإليها نسبته ،
والثانية هي نوقان (1) .
مولده ونشأته :
ولد الإمام بطابران طُوسَ سنة ( ٤٥٠ هـ ) لأب غزال متكسب فقير ، لم يجمع من الدنيا لا النزر اليسير ، فلما دنتْ منه المنية وكان متأسفاً أن فاته التعلم .. دفع بولديه محمد وأحمد
ويلقب الإمام الغزالي بالفارسية بـ ( دانشمند ( ومعناه : العالم الفقيه أو الحكيم ، ذكره بهذا اللقب الإمام ابن العربي المالكي . انظر ( قانون التأويل ) ( ص ۱۱۱ )
The life biography of Imam al-Ghazali. His name and lineage: He is the Proof of Islam, the Pride of Mankind, the Imam, the scholar, the great, Zayn al-Din Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Ghazali al-Tusi al-Tabarani al-Shafi'i. He is known by the kunya Abu Hamid, and by this kunya he became famous, and there is none among his children named Hamid; the Imam only fathered daughters. As for his nickname al-Ghazali, there is the well-known disagreement in the books of those who translated his biography: is it with a shadda on the zay or with a tashdid? Among those who prefer the shadda, it is a relative attribution to his father who worked as a ghazzal (spinner); the ya is for emphasis of the relationship, or it is the custom of his countrymen, or to distinguish between the owner of the craft and the one attributed to it. Others saw it with tashdid (light) and attributed it to Ghazala, like 'sahaba', a village of Tus. Each view has its supporters, and most are on the shadda. Tus is a district in Khurasan containing two cities; one is Tabaran, the city of the Imam, to which he is attributed, and the second is Nuqan. His birth and upbringing: The Imam was born in Tabaran, Tus, in the year 450 AH, to a father who was a poor spinner, who did not gather from the world even a little. When death approached him, and he was regretful that he missed learning, he handed over his two sons Muhammad and Ahmad. He (the Imam) is nicknamed in Persian 'Danishmand', meaning the scholar-jurist or the sage; Imam Ibn al-‘Arabi al-Maliki mentioned him by this nickname. See (Qanun al-Ta’wil) p. 111.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a biography of Imam al-Ghazali. It begins by giving his full name and titles: Proof of Islam, Pride of Mankind, Zayn al-Din Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Ghazali al-Tusi al-Tabarani al-Shafi'i. He is known as Abu Hamid, though he had no son named Hamid; he only had daughters. Then it discusses the controversy over his nickname 'al-Ghazali': whether it is pronounced with a shadda (doubled z) or without. Those who prefer the shadda derive it from his father's profession as a spinner (ghazzal), while others derive it from the village of Ghazala. The author notes that most scholars favor the shadda. Next, it describes Tus as a district in Khurasan with two cities: Tabaran (the Imam's city) and Nuqan. Finally, it covers his birth: born in Tabaran in 450 AH to a poor spinner father who regretted not being able to learn and entrusted his two sons (Muhammad and Ahmad) to others. The passage ends with a note on his Persian nickname 'Danishmand' meaning scholar or sage, cited by Ibn al-‘Arabi al-Maliki in Qanun al-Ta’wil.

Key Terms
حجة الإسلام
الغزالي
كنية
تشديد
تخفيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'al-Ghazali' is always pronounced with a shadda and that the other pronunciation is invalid.
Correction: Both pronunciations have scholarly support, though the shadda is preferred by the majority. The lighter pronunciation (without shadda) is also historically attested.

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason for the shadda pronunciation of 'al-Ghazali'?
A) It is the name of a village
B) It is a title given by Islamic scholars
C) It is attributed to his father who was a spinner
D) It comes from the Persian word for knowledge

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Ghazali's name reflects his lineage, his kunya is honorific, and the correct pronunciation of 'al-Ghazali' (with shadda) is linked to his father's craft.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ghazali's name traces back to his father's humble craft of spinning. Does your identity rely more on worldly achievements or on a deeper connection to your roots and your Creator? How can you embody humility in how you introduce yourself or describe your lineage today?
?
Scholars debated the pronunciation of a single letter in al-Ghazali's name with great precision. Are you as meticulous about the details of your faith as they were? What small matter of deen have you neglected that deserves your attention and care starting now?
?
Most scholars settled on the shadda pronunciation, but the debate itself shows that nothing in Islamic knowledge is trivial. Is there any aspect of Islamic scholarship that you have dismissed as 'minor'? What step can you take today to deepen your respect and engagement with the scholarly tradition?

Life of Imam al-Ghazali
beginner

Birth and Upbringing of Imam al-Ghazali
Al-Ghazali's birthplace, birth year, family, and Persian nickname.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the place and year of Imam al-Ghazali's birth, his family background, and his Persian nickname.

Core Idea
Imam al-Ghazali was born in 450 AH in Tabaran, Tus, to a poor but pious father who entrusted his education before death.

After learning about Imam al-Ghazali's name, we now turn to his early life. His birthplace of Tabaran in Tus and his father's humble profession as a spinner shaped his journey. The passage also reveals his Persian nickname, showing his cultural roots in Khurasan.

Guided Path
1
Locate his birthplace and the cities of Tus.
Read the Arabic describing Tus as a region with two cities. Identify which city is his birthplace (Tabaran) and the other (Nuqan).
This clarifies the geographic origin of the Imam.
2
Learn about his birth year and family.
Note the Hijri year 450 and the description of his father as a poor spinner. Understand the father's regret and his dying wish for his sons to learn.
This shows the humble beginnings of the great scholar.
3
Discover his Persian nickname.
Read the final part about 'Danishmand' (meaning scholar or sage). Note that this nickname was mentioned by Ibn al-‘Arabi al-Maliki.
This links the Imam to his Persian heritage.

The life biography of Imam al-Ghazali. His name and lineage: He is the Proof of Islam, the Pride of Mankind, the Imam, the scholar, the great, Zayn al-Din Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Ghazali al-Tusi al-Tabarani al-Shafi'i. He is known by the kunya Abu Hamid, and by this kunya he became famous, and there is none among his children named Hamid; the Imam only fathered daughters. As for his nickname al-Ghazali, there is the well-known disagreement in the books of those who translated his biography: is it with a shadda on the zay or with a tashdid? Among those who prefer the shadda, it is a relative attribution to his father who worked as a ghazzal (spinner); the ya is for emphasis of the relationship, or it is the custom of his countrymen, or to distinguish between the owner of the craft and the one attributed to it. Tus is a district in Khurasan containing two cities; one is Tabaran, the city of the Imam, to which he is attributed, and the second is Nuqan. His birth and upbringing: The Imam was born in Tabaran, Tus, in the year 450 AH, to a father who was a poor spinner, who did not gather from the world even a little. When death approached him, and he was regretful that he missed learning, he handed over his two sons Muhammad and Ahmad. He (the Imam) is nicknamed in Persian 'Danishmand', meaning the scholar-jurist or the sage; Imam Ibn al-‘Arabi al-Maliki mentioned him by this nickname. See (Qanun al-Ta’wil) p. 111.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What one area of Islamic knowledge have you neglected because you feel you are too busy or not capable? Al-Ghazali's father regretted missing learning on his deathbed. What will you do this week to fill that gap, even if just a few minutes a day?
?

?
Al-Ghazali's father was a poor spinner, yet he prioritised his sons' education. What worldly excuse are you using to avoid seeking knowledge? How can you rearrange your priorities starting tomorrow?

Life of Imam al-Ghazali
beginner
4 min
The Name and Lineage of Imam al-Ghazali
Introduces al-Ghazali's full name, titles, and the debate on his epithet's spelling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the full name, titles, and lineage of Imam al-Ghazali, as well as the scholarly debate over his epithet's spelling and its connection to his father's profession or a village.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's epithet reflects a scholarly debate: is it from his father's trade or a village?

This lesson introduces you to the formal identity of one of Islam's greatest scholars, Imam al-Ghazali. We will explore his full name, his honorary titles, and especially the interesting disagreement about how to spell his famous epithet. Understanding these details helps you appreciate the biographical foundations of his works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the list of names and titles, then the discussion on the spelling of 'al-Ghazali'.
Pay attention to the two opinions.
2
Identify the key elements of his name
List out his full name: Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Ghazali al-Tusi al-Tabirani al-Shafi'i, and his kunyah Abu Hamid.
3
Understand the two opinions on 'al-Ghazali'
One opinion says it's from his father's profession (ghazzal = spinner) with tashdid; the other says it's from a village Ghazalah with takhfif. The majority prefer tashdid.
This is a famous biographical debate.
4
Note the geography of Tus
Tus has two cities: Tabiran (his birthplace) and Nuqan. He is attributed to Tabiran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو حجة الإسلام ، وفخر الأنام ، الإمام الحبر الهمام، زين الدين محمد بن محمد بن محمد بن أحمد الغزالي الطُّوسي الطابراني الشافعي يكنى بأبي حامد ، وبهذه الكنية اشتهر ، وليس من ولده من اسمه حامد ؛ فالإمام لم يعقب سوى البنات أما لقبه الغزالي .. ففيه الخلاف المشهور في كتب من ترجم له : أهو بتشديد الزاء أو بتخفيفها ؟ فبين مرجّح للتشديد نسبة إلى والده الذي كان يعمل غزالاً ؛ على أن الياء لتأكيد النسبة ، أو هي عادة أهل بلاده، أو للتمييز بين صاحب الحرفة والمنتسب إليه وآخر رأى التخفيف ونسبه إلى غزالة كسحابة ؛ قرية من قرى طوس ولكل قول مناصروه ، وغالبهم على التشديد وطُوس ناحية بخراسان تشتمل على مدينتين ؛ إحداهما طابران بلد الإمام وإليها نسبته ، والثانية هي نوقان (1) .
He is the Proof of Islam, the Pride of Mankind, the Imam, the Scholar, the Excellent, Zayn al-Din Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Ghazali al-Tusi al-Tabirani al-Shafi'i. He is known by the kunyah Abu Hamid, and by this kunyah he became famous, and there is none among his children named Hamid; for the Imam only left behind daughters. As for his epithet 'al-Ghazali'... there is the well-known disagreement in the books of those who wrote his biography: is it with tashdid (doubling) of the zay or with its takhfif (lightening)? For some prefer tashdid as a nisbah to his father who worked as a spinner (ghazzal); the ya' is for emphasis of the nisbah, or it is the custom of his countrymen, or to distinguish between the owner of the craft and the one attributed to it. Others held the opinion of takhfif and attributed him to Ghazalah, like a cloud, a village among the villages of Tus. Each opinion has its supporters, and the majority are for tashdid. And Tus is a region in Khurasan comprising two cities: one is Tabiran, the Imam's town, to which he is attributed, and the second is Nuqan.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage begins the biography of Imam al-Ghazali by presenting his full name, titles, and the debate surrounding his epithet. It first lists his names and his kunyah Abu Hamid, noting that he had no son named Hamid. Then it discusses the famous disagreement on the spelling of 'al-Ghazali': is it with a shaddah on the zay (ghazzālī) or without (ghazālī)? Those who prefer tashdid connect it to his father's profession as a spinner (ghazzāl). Those who prefer takhfif connect it to the village of Ghazalah. The majority side with tashdid. Finally, the passage describes Tus in Khurasan, which consists of two cities: Tabiran (his birthplace) and Nuqan.

Key Terms
حجة الإسلام
الغزالي
طوس
طابران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'al-Ghazali' refers to the city of Ghazalah, but the majority connect it to his father's profession.
Correction: The passage states that the majority opinion is tashdid, attributing his epithet to his father's work as a spinner, not primarily to the village.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the origin of the disagreement about the spelling of 'al-Ghazali'?
A) Whether it is a nisbah to his father's profession or to a village
B) Whether he was born in Tus or Ghazalah
C) Whether he had a son named Hamid
D) Whether he was Shafi'i or Hanafi

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Ghazali's full name includes titles like 'Proof of Islam', and his epithet is most commonly linked to his father's profession as a spinner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ghazali's epithet is tied to his father's humble profession as a spinner. Do you ever feel ashamed of your family's background or social status? How does his example challenge you to see honor in lineage through knowledge and piety, not wealth or prestige?
?
The source tells us that al-Ghazali was called 'Abu Hamid' even though he had no son named Hamid. Have you ever made assumptions about someone's family situation based on their name or title? How can you train yourself to avoid such judgments and respect others' dignity?
?
The majority of biographers hold the opinion of tashdid (ghazzālī) over takhfif (ghazālī). In your own learning, do you tend to accept the majority view without verifying its evidence? What would it take for you to become like al-Ghazali – truly investigating a matter rather than blindly following the crowd?

Life of Imam al-Ghazali
beginner
4 min
Birth and Early Upbringing of Imam al-Ghazali
Describes al-Ghazali's birth, his father's poverty and death, and the fulfillment of his will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the humble beginnings of Imam al-Ghazali and how his father's deathbed wish set him on the path to becoming a great scholar.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's poverty and his father's supplication launched him to greatness.

This lesson introduces the early life of Imam al-Ghazali, highlighting his birth in 450 AH in Tus, his father's poverty and regret over lack of education, and the father's deathbed charge to a Sufi friend. The narrative shows how divine providence and a father's prayer led to the rise of one of Islam's greatest thinkers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the descriptive style and the concluding quote from Taj al-Subki.
2
Identify the key events
Identify the birth, the father's poverty and regret, the deathbed will, the Sufi's execution, and the outcome. Note how each part connects.
3
Understand the father's motivation
Consider why the father was regretful and why he entrusted his sons to a Sufi. How did his supplication affect their future?
The father's missed learning drove his desire for his sons' education.
4
Appreciate the result
Reflect on the quote from Taj al-Subki. What does it tell us about the brothers' achievements? Connect it to the father's prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولد الإمام بطابران طُوسَ سنة ( ٤٥٠ هـ ) لأب غزال متكسب فقير ، لم يجمع من الدنيا لا النزر اليسير ، فلما دنتْ منه المنية وكان متأسفاً أن فاته التعلم .. دفع بولديه محمد وأحمد وهما شبلان لصديق له صوفي وأوصاه بتعليمهما وإن نفد من يديه كلُّ ما خلفه فأقبل الصوفي بعد وفاة الأب على تنفيذ الوصية ، فلما نفد المال من يديه وكان من أهل التجريد.. لجأ بهما إلى مدرسة ترعى طلبة العلم قاصداً إعفافهما عن ذل المسألة مع طلب العلم ، ونالتهما دعوة أبيهما ؛ فكان الأمر كما قال التاج السبكي رحمه الله : ( أما أبو حامد .. فكان أفقه أقرانه ، وإمامَ أهل زمانه وفارس میدانه ، کلمته شهد بها الموافقُ والمخالف ، وأقر بحقِّيَّتها المعادي والمحالف وأما أحمد.. فكان واعظاً تنفلق الصمُّ الصخور عند استماع تحذيره ، وترعد فرائص الحاضرين في مجالس تذكيره ((۱)
The Imam was born in Tabiran of Tus in the year (450 AH) to a father who was a spinner, earning a livelihood poor, who had not collected from the world even a little. When death approached him and he was regretful that he had missed learning, he gave his two sons Muhammad and Ahmad, who were two cubs, to a Sufi friend of his and charged him with their education, even if all that he left behind should be exhausted from his hands. So after the father's death, the Sufi set about executing the will. When the wealth was exhausted from his hands and he was among the people of detachment, he took them to a school that cared for students of knowledge, intending to keep them free from the humiliation of begging along with seeking knowledge. And they were reached by their father's supplication; so it was as said by Taj al-Subki, may Allah have mercy on him: 'As for Abu Hamid... he was the most knowledgeable of his peers, the imam of the people of his time and the champion of his field, whose word was witnessed to by both friend and foe, and whose rightfulness was acknowledged by the adversary and the ally. And as for Ahmad... he was a preacher from whose warnings the solid rocks would split, and the livers of those present would tremble in his sessions of admonishment.'
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage covers al-Ghazali's birth in 450 AH in Tabiran, Tus, to a poor father who worked as a spinner. Regretting his own lack of education, on his deathbed he entrusted his two young sons to a Sufi friend, instructing him to use all his inheritance for their education. The friend fulfilled the will until the money ran out, at which point he enrolled the boys in a school for students, to spare them the humiliation of begging while seeking knowledge. The passage then quotes Taj al-Subki praising both brothers: Abu Hamid as the most learned of his time and his brother Ahmad as a powerful preacher.

التاج السبكي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Al-Ghazali came from a wealthy family.
Correction: His father was a poor spinner who left little inheritance.

This passage from al-Ghazali's biography traces his early academic path from his first fiqh lessons in Tus to his transformative encounter with Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni in Nishapur. It also recounts a formative incident involving his first authored work, which shaped his approach to knowledge.

And in the prime of his youth, the Imam studied a portion of fiqh (jurisprudence) with Shaykh Ahmad al-Radhakani, and that was in Tus. Then he traveled to Jurjan after his backbone had become strong, and he met Imam Abu al-Qasim al-Isma'ili, and he wrote from him — on the most apparent view — his first work, which is the book 'al-Ta'liqah'. And there occurred with him on his return an incident that alerted him to memorizing knowledge and mastering it, when some ruffians stole from him the aforementioned Ta'liqah and then returned it to him. And after his return to Tus, he traveled a second time, this time to Nishapur, to meet there the wonder of his age, Imam al-Haramayn 'Abd al-Malik al-Juwayni, and thus began the crafting of al-Ghazali who surpassed the people of his time. So he applied himself to Abu al-Ma'ali, drinking deeply from his knowledge, striving, acquiring, and working hard. Al-Juwayni at that time was the shaykh of the school and of disputation, an imam in the two foundations (usul al-din and usul al-fiqh); no one had seen his like among people. Imam al-Qushayri, author of 'al-Risalah', said about him: 'If Allah were to send a prophet in the era of al-Juwayni, he would be none other than him.' And the Imam, by meeting al-Juwayni, shed his former life in which the students of knowledge in his time would interrupt him, in order to enter...
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage details al-Ghazali's early education. He first studied fiqh with Ahmad al-Radhakani in Tus. Then, after maturing, he traveled to Jurjan and met Abu al-Qasim al-Isma'ili, from whom he wrote his first book, al-Ta'liqah. On his return journey, the manuscript was stolen by bandits but later returned, which taught him the importance of memorizing knowledge. Later, he went to Nishapur to study under the renowned scholar Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni. This marked the beginning of his scholarly excellence. The passage quotes al-Qushayri praising al-Juwayni as being fit to be a prophet if one were sent. Finally, it mentions that meeting al-Juwayni transformed al-Ghazali's life, cutting him off from his previous distractions. (The sentence is incomplete in the source.)

The Orphaned Brothers and the Sufi Guardian
Narrates the father's death and the Sufi's custody of Ghazali and his brother.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ghazali's early orphanhood and the care of a Sufi guardian shaped his path to becoming a great scholar, and the role of his father's supplication.

Core Idea
Orphanhood and a father's prayer set Ghazali on his scholarly path.

This passage from the beginning of al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad gives us a rare glimpse into Imam al-Ghazali's childhood. It tells the story of how he and his brother were orphaned, entrusted to a Sufi, and eventually supported by a school. The narrative highlights the piety and detachment of the guardian, and culminates in the remarkable achievements of both brothers, showing the power of a parent's prayer and sincere intention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation to get the flow of the story.
Notice the emotional tone: the father's will, the guardian's faithfulness, and the eventual success.
2
Identify the key figures
List the main characters: the father, the Sufi guardian, Abu Hamid (Ghazali) and his brother Ahmad. Understand their roles.
The father is not named but his prayer is mentioned.
3
Trace the sequence of events
Follow the narrative: father's death, will execution, money running out, guardian's decision to take them to a school, and the outcome.
The guardian's detachment (tajrid) is key to his decision.
4
Reflect on the outcome
Read Taj al-Subki's praise of the two brothers. Note how Ghazali became a leading scholar and Ahmad a powerful preacher.
The father's supplication is credited for this success.

Consider the lesson for today

They were two cubs for a friend of his who was a Sufi, and he instructed him to teach them even if all that he left behind ran out from his hands. So the Sufi, after the father's death, proceeded to execute the will. When the money ran out from his hands and he was among the people of detachment, he resorted with them to a school that cares for students of knowledge, intending to spare them the humiliation of begging while seeking knowledge. And the father's supplication reached them; so the matter was as Taj al-Subki (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'As for Abu Hamid, he was the most knowledgeable of his peers, the imam of the people of his time and the champion of his field; his word was testified to by both the agreeable and the disagreeable, and his rightfulness was acknowledged by the adversary and the ally. Commentary
This passage is a biographical narrative describing the early orphanhood of Imam al-Ghazali and his brother Ahmad. It begins by mentioning that they were two 'cubs' (shiblan) entrusted to a Sufi friend by their dying father, who ordered the Sufi to teach them even if it consumed all his inheritance. After the father's death, the Sufi faithfully executed the will. When the money was exhausted and the Sufi, being a man of spiritual detachment (tajrid), took the boys to a school that supports students of knowledge, aiming to keep them from begging while they study. The passage then cites Taj al-Subki's praise: Abu Hamid (Ghazali) became the most learned of his peers and the imam of his time, while Ahmad became a powerful preacher. This establishes the blessed outcome of the father's prayer.

Meeting Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni
Ghazali travels to Nishapur to study under Juwayni and undergoes transformation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali's encounter with Juwayni transformed his intellectual character and gave rise to his critical, investigative approach.

Core Idea
Meeting Juwayni unlocked Ghazali's critical mind.

This lesson follows Ghazali's journey to Nishapur to study under the legendary Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni. The meeting was not merely an education — it was a rebirth. Under Juwayni's tutelage, Ghazali shed his earlier self and emerged as a thinker defined by piercing insight and fearless inquiry. Let's explore this turning point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Let the words of the narrator describe Ghazali's departure from Tus and his arrival in Nishapur.
Pay attention to the emotional tone: words like 'drinking deeply' and 'striving' show Ghazali's intensity.
2
Understand Juwayni's stature
Notice how the passage praises Juwayni — 'the marvel of his age,' 'unmatched,' and the hyperbolic quote from al-Qushayri. This sets the stage for why studying under him was transformative.
The quote is not literal; it's a rhetorical exaggeration (hyperbole) to emphasize Juwayni's exceptional virtue.
3
Identify the transformation
Focus on the phrase 'sheds his former life' and 'enter a second life.' What changed in Ghazali? The passage says he developed a 'critical, investigative mind' and 'free, enlightened thought.' This is the core of the lesson: the meeting sparked his intellectual rebirth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد عودته إلى طوس رحل ثانيةً وهذه المرة إلى نيسابور ، ليلقى فيها أعجوبة عصره إمام الحرمين عبد الملك الجويني ، ولتبدأ صناعة الغزالي الذي بذ أهل عصره ؛ فأقبل على أبي المعالي يعبُّ من علمه عبّاً ، ويجد ويحصل ويجتهد ، والجويني يومها شيخُ المذهب. والخلاف ، وإمامٌ في الأصلين ، لم يرَ في الناس مثله ، قال فيه الإمام القشيري صاحب الرسالة » : ( لو بعث الله نبياً في عصر الجويني .. لما كان إلا هو ) ويسلخ الإمام بلقيا الجويني حياته الأولى التي يقاطعه فيها طلبة العلم في عصره ليدخل في ثانية هي سرُّ الغزالي صاحب العقل الناقد المفتش ، والفكر الحر المستنير ، والبصيرة الثاقبة النفاذة
And after his return to Tus, he traveled a second time, and this time to Nishapur, to meet the marvel of his age, Imam al-Haramayn ‘Abd al-Malik al-Juwayni, and to begin the formation of al-Ghazali who surpassed the people of his time. So he devoted himself to Abu al-Ma‘ali, drinking deeply from his knowledge, striving, acquiring, and exerting effort. At that time, al-Juwayni was the shaykh of the school and of disputation, and an imam in the two foundations (usulayn); no one like him was seen among people. Imam al-Qushayri, author of al-Risala, said about him: 'If Allah had sent a prophet in the age of al-Juwayni, he would have been no one but him.' And with the meeting of al-Juwayni, the Imam sheds his former life in which the students of knowledge of his time used to interrupt him, to enter a second life which is the secret of al-Ghazali, the possessor of the critical, investigative mind, the free, enlightened thought, and the piercing, penetrating insight.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage narrates Ghazali's pivotal move to Nishapur to study under the greatest scholar of his era, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni. It describes Ghazali's intense dedication: he eagerly absorbed Juwayni's knowledge, worked hard, and strove diligently. Juwayni is portrayed as the unmatched authority in fiqh (the school), dialectics (al-khilaf), and the two foundational sciences (usul al-din and usul al-fiqh). A strong praise from al-Qushayri is quoted: if a prophet were to be sent in Juwayni's time, it would be him (a rhetorical exaggeration of his virtue). The passage then describes Ghazali's transformation: meeting Juwayni caused him to shed his former life—where students of knowledge used to interrupt him—and enter a second life characterized by a critical, investigative mind, free thought, and penetrating insight. This 'second life' is called the 'secret of al-Ghazali.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Qushayri's statement about Juwayni being like a prophet is meant literally.
Correction: The statement is a rhetorical hyperbole (mubalaghah) used to emphasize Juwayni's exceptional virtue and rank, not a literal claim of prophethood.

Ghazali's Mastery in Logic, Philosophy, and Islamic Sciences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the breadth of Ghazali's intellectual achievements during his time under Imam al-Juwayni.

Core Idea
Ghazali excelled in logic, dialectic, philosophy, and the foundations of law.

In this passage, we learn about the profound intellectual development of Imam al-Ghazali while studying under the great scholar Juwayni. The text highlights his command over multiple disciplines, from logic to jurisprudence, and his ability to produce authoritative works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage lists several fields of knowledge.
2
Identify the fields of mastery
The passage mentions several fields: logic, dialectic, philosophy (hikma and falsafa), foundations (usul), madhhab, and branches (furu'). List them and see how they relate.
Some fields overlap; philosophy includes both hikma and falsafa.
3
Reflect on authorship
The passage states that Ghazali authored works in all these arts that were of utmost perfection. Consider what this says about his intellectual output at a young age.
4
Summarize the portrait
Write a one-sentence summary of Ghazali's intellectual profile as described in this part of the passage.
This connects to the learning objective.

في هذه المرحلة برع الإمام في علوم المنطق والجدل ، وقرأَ الحكمة والفلسفة ، وأجادَ فنون الأصول فضلاً عن المذهب والفروع ، وكان له نصيب من العلم وافر ، ذكاء ينم عن وفهم عجيب ؛ حتى صنف في هذه المرحلة تأليف في كل هذه الفنون وهي في غاية الإتقان ، كما تصدى فيها للرد على أهل البدع والكفر والزندقة وكان يومها مناظراً محجاجاً مُفْحِماً لأهل الزيغ والضلال
In this stage, the Imam excelled in the sciences of logic and dialectic, and read wisdom (philosophy) and philosophy, and mastered the arts of the foundations (usul) in addition to the school (madhhab) and branches (furu‘). Ghazali's refutation of heretics and debating skill.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's active role in refuting heretical groups and his debating prowess.

Core Idea
Ghazali vigorously refuted innovators and heretics, silencing them through debate.

The passage also highlights Ghazali's public role as a defender of Islamic orthodoxy. He did not remain an academic; he engaged directly with those who held deviant beliefs, using his intellectual skills to counter their arguments.

Guided Path
1

These words indicate active confrontation.

These are distinct categories.

Ghazali's method was refutation (الرد) and debate (مناظرة). He was described as 'مُفْحِماً' meaning he silenced his opponents. Reflect on the combative tone.
This shows he was not just writing but engaging directly.
4

Juwayni's Praise: Metaphors for His Students
Juwayni's public praise of Ghazali, Kiya, and Khawafi using striking metaphors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Juwayni publicly praised his student al-Ghazali and two other scholars using vivid metaphors that convey deep respect and distinct qualities.

Core Idea
Juwayni called Ghazali a 'drowning sea,' Kiya a 'tearing lion,' and Khawafi a 'burning fire.'

One aspect of the relationship between teacher and student is shown through Juwayni's open praise. He did not hesitate to acknowledge Ghazali's vast knowledge and the distinct qualities of his other notable students. The metaphors he used were memorable and carried deep meaning about their scholarly strengths.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic praise
Read the Arabic passage where Juwayni describes Ghazali, Kiya, and Khawafi. Notice the powerful imagery: sea, lion, fire.
These metaphors are not random; they highlight specific virtues.
2
Understand each metaphor
Think about what each metaphor implies: a drowning sea suggests overwhelming, vast knowledge; a tearing lion suggests fierce debate; a burning fire suggests sharp critique.
3
Reflect on the context
Consider why Juwayni would use such strong praise. It shows his recognition of their talents and lack of envy, countering later claims of hidden jealousy.
This praise is public and recorded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان الإمام الجويني يعرف للغزالي قدره وفضله ، وأن الله أذن بحفظ علمه إذ حواه صدر ، فقال فيه وفي صاحبيه : ( الغزالي بحر مغرق ، والكيا أسد مخرق ، والخوافي نار تحرق ) . وقد حاول بعض المترجمين الدخول إلى طوايا النفوس واستنطاق مكامنها في حديثهم عن الجويني والغزالي حين قالوا : ( وكان الإمام مع علو درجته لا يصفي نظره إلى الغزالي ؛ ستراً لإنافته عليه في سرعة العبارة وقوة الطبع ، ولا يطيب له تصديه للتصانيف وإن كان منتسباً إليه كما لا يخفى من طباع البشر ، لكنه يظهرُ التبجُحَ به والاعتداد بمكانه ظاهراً خلاف ما يضمره )(۱) ) وكان الإمام أبو المعالي مع علو درجته وفرط ذكائه لا يطيب له تصديه للتصنيف وإن كان في الظاهر مبتهجاً به ((٢) ( ويقال : إن الإمام كان بالأخرة يمتعض منه في الباطن وإن كان يظهر التبجح به في الظاهر ) (۳) وهذا الاستنطاق خارج عن قاعدتهم المشهورة : كلام الأقران يطوى ولا يروى ، ولولا أننا في صدد بحث وتتبع شبه .. لكان الإعراض أولى من الذكر
And Imam al-Juwayni recognized the worth and virtue of al-Ghazali, and that Allah had permitted the preservation of his knowledge when He included it in his breast, so he said about him and his two companions: 'al-Ghazali is a drowning sea, al-Kiya is a tearing lion, and al-Khawafi is a burning fire.' And some biographers attempted to enter into the recesses of souls and elicit their hidden aspects in their talk about al-Juwayni and al-Ghazali when they said: 'And the Imam, despite his high rank, did not look with a pure eye to al-Ghazali, in order to hide his superiority over him in quickness of expression and strength of nature, and he did not approve of his undertaking of compositions, even though he was affiliated with him, as is not hidden from human nature. But he outwardly showed pride in him and esteem for his standing, contrary to what he concealed.' And [it was also said]: 'And Imam Abu al-Ma‘ali, despite his high rank and excessive intelligence, did not approve of his undertaking of composition, even though outwardly he was delighted with it.' And it is said: 'The Imam eventually became displeased with him inwardly, even though he showed pride in him outwardly.' And this elicitation is outside their well-known rule: 'The speech of peers is folded away and not narrated.' And were it not that we are in the context of research and investigation of suspicions, refraining would be more appropriate than mentioning.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents two aspects: first, Juwayni's public praise of Ghazali and two other students, calling Ghazali a 'drowning sea' (vast knowledge), al-Kiya a 'tearing lion' (fierce debater), and al-Khawafi a 'burning fire' (sharp critique). Then it reports attempts by later biographers to uncover hidden resentment from Juwayni towards Ghazali, claiming that Juwayni was internally jealous of Ghazali's quickness and did not approve of his writing books, although he publicly boasted about him. Several reports are quoted. The author of this biography (the editor) then criticizes these attempts, noting that they violate the scholarly principle: 'The speech of peers is folded away and not narrated' — meaning such speculations should be avoided. He adds that only because the context is research and tracking suspicions (likely for a critical study) does he mention them; otherwise, silence would be better. This shows a careful balance between providing information and observing academic propriety.

Key Terms
الغزالي بحر مغرق
الكيا أسد مخرق
الخوافي نار تحرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What metaphor did Juwayni use for al-Ghazali?
A) A tearing lion
B) A drowning sea
C) A burning fire
D) A rising sun

Key Takeaway
Juwayni publicly praised Ghazali as a 'drowning sea,' al-Kiya as a 'tearing lion,' and al-Khawafi as a 'burning fire,' highlighting their unique scholarly strengths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone praises your knowledge or skill, do you feel pride or gratitude to Allah? How does Juwayni's acknowledgment that 'Allah permitted the preservation of his knowledge' challenge your view of your own abilities?
?

?
If you are a teacher or mentor, do you publicly recognize the strengths of your students as Juwayni did? What holds you back from doing so, and what can you do this week to change that?

Hidden Jealousy and the Rule of Peer Speech
Reports of hidden jealousy and the principle of not narrating peer speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the controversy around reports of Juwayni's hidden jealousy towards Ghazali and the principle that 'the speech of peers is folded away and not narrated.'

Core Idea
The rule: 'The speech of peers is folded away, not narrated' — a warning against speculating about scholars' hidden feelings.

While Juwayni's public praise is clear, some biographers attempted to uncover hidden resentment. They claimed that Juwayni was inwardly displeased with Ghazali's quickness and authorship. The author of this biography critiques such attempts, citing the well-known principle that such speculation should be avoided. This lesson examines these reports and the rule.

Guided Path
1
Read the reports of jealousy
Read the Arabic text where biographers claim Juwayni was inwardly jealous of Ghazali. Note the specific claims: he did not look with a pure eye, he did not approve of his composition, etc.
These are reported sayings from unnamed sources.
2
Understand the principle
Learn the rule: 'The speech of peers is folded away and not narrated.' This means we should avoid narrating or speculating about what scholars say about each other in private or what might be hidden.
This rule promotes respect and avoids fitna.
3
Consider the author's stance
The author mentions these reports only because the context is research and investigation of suspicions, otherwise he would have refrained. This shows a careful balance between providing information and observing propriety.

Source Text

And this elicitation is outside their well-known rule: 'The speech of peers is folded away and not narrated.' ?

?

?
Imagine yourself on the Day of Judgment being questioned about every word you spoke regarding others. What would you say about the times you narrated something negative that was 'folded away'? Will you commit to applying this rule starting today, even with those you feel superior to?

Ghazali's Multidisciplinary Mastery
Biographical description of Ghazali's scholarly range and polemical activity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam Ghazali's intellectual training encompassed logic, philosophy, and law, and that he actively defended orthodox Islam through debate and authorship.

Core Idea
Ghazali mastered both rational and legal sciences to defend orthodoxy.

This passage offers a glimpse into the breadth of Imam Ghazali's scholarship. It recounts how he excelled not only in jurisprudence but also in logic, philosophy, and disputation. Understanding this background helps us appreciate the depth of his theological works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note the sequence of sciences mentioned: logic, disputation, wisdom, philosophy, legal theory, jurisprudence, and branches.
Pay attention to the verbs: 'excelled,' 'read,' 'mastered.'
2
Identify Ghazali's fields of expertise
List all the disciplines the passage mentions. Consider why each was important for a theologian.
They cover both rational (logic, philosophy) and transmitted (fiqh, usul) sciences.
3
Understand his role as defender of orthodoxy
The passage says he refuted 'ahl al-bida', al-kufr, al-zandaqah.' Reflect on how his knowledge equipped him for this task.
The term 'mufhim' indicates he silenced opponents through compelling arguments.
4
Connect to his legacy
Think about how these skills are reflected in Ghazali's major works like 'Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din' and 'Tahafut al-Falasifa.'
This passage sets the stage for understanding his method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في هذه المرحلة برع الإمام في علوم المنطق والجدل ، وقرأَ الحكمة والفلسفة ، وأجادَ فنون الأصول فضلاً عن المذهب والفروع ، وكان له نصيب من العلم وافر ، ذكاء ينم عن وفهم عجيب ؛ حتى صنف في هذه المرحلة تأليف في كل هذه الفنون وهي في غاية الإتقان ، كما تصدى فيها للرد على أهل البدع والكفر والزندقة وكان يومها مناظراً محجاجاً مُفْحِماً لأهل الزيغ والضلال
In this stage, the Imam excelled in the sciences of logic (al-mantiq) and disputation (al-jadal), and read wisdom (al-hikmah) and philosophy (al-falsafah), and mastered the arts of legal theory (usul) as well as jurisprudence (al-madhhab) and its branches (al-furu'), and he had an abundant share of knowledge, intelligence that indicated remarkable understanding; so much so that in this stage he authored works in all these arts in the highest perfection, and he also undertook therein the refutation of the people of innovation (ahl al-bida'), disbelief (al-kufr), and heresy (al-zandaqah), and he was at that time a disputant, a debater, silencing the people of deviation (ahl al-zaygh) and misguidance (al-dalal).
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a biographical sketch of Imam Ghazali's scholarly achievements during his early career. It states that he excelled in logic and disputation, studied philosophy and wisdom, and mastered both the principles of jurisprudence and the substantive law. His intelligence was extraordinary. He authored works in all these fields which were of the highest quality. Moreover, he actively refuted heretics, innovators, and unbelievers through debate and disputation, effectively silencing them.

Key Terms

الجدل
الحكمة
الفلسفة
الأصول
المذهب
الفروع
البدع
الكفر
الزندقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali focused only on fiqh and spirituality, ignoring philosophy and logic.
Correction: This passage shows that Ghazali studied and mastered logic, philosophy, and disputation alongside jurisprudence.

Checkpoint
What did Ghazali do in response to heretics and unbelievers during this stage?
A) He avoided them and focused on teaching.
B) He refuted them through disputation and writing.
C) He left Baghdad to avoid conflict.
D) He only studied philosophy.

Key Takeaway
Imam Ghazali's intellectual breadth—spanning logic, philosophy, and law—equipped him to be a formidable defender of orthodox Islam against heretics and innovators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali mastered both rational and legal sciences to defend the truth. What intellectual tools are you equipping yourself with to defend your faith? Are you neglecting the rational sciences that could help you refute modern doubts? Commit to studying one rational science this month.
?
You have just read that Ghazali authored works of 'highest perfection' in logic, philosophy, and law. How many books have you read that arm you against heresy? If you were to face an innovator today, would you have the knowledge to 'silence' them, as Ghazali did? Describe one specific area you will strengthen starting this week.
?
The passage mentions Ghazali refuted 'people of innovation, disbelief, and heresy.' Consider the innovations and doubts prevalent in your time. What are they? And do you have a plan to study them so you can refute them? Your silence might be complicity.

5 min
Juwayni's Praise of Ghazali and the Etiquette of Narrating Peer's Words
Relates Juwayni's praise of Ghazali and reports of hidden jealousy, with a comment on scholarly etiquette.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of scholarly etiquette in handling reports about peer relationships, and how Ghazali's later status transcends his teacher's praise.

Core Idea
True scholars honor their peers, but their private words are to be concealed, not broadcast.

This passage from Ghazali's biography offers a beautiful example of a teacher's recognition while also cautioning against over-analyzing human feelings. Imam al-Juwayni's famous comparison of his students is legendary, but reports of hidden jealousy remind us of the delicate nature of scholarly relationships. In this lesson, we will examine the praise, the rule of 'folding the talk of peers', and the profound distinction between a student shaped by a teacher and one chosen by God.

Guided Path
1
Read Juwayni's praise and the biographers' claims
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the vivid metaphors: sea, lion, fire. Then see how biographers claim Juwayni inwardly disliked Ghazali's success.
The biographers attempt to 'interrogate the hidden places of souls'.
2
Appreciate the rule 'kalam al-aqran yutwa wa la yurwa'
This rule means that talk between peers should be 'folded' and not transmitted. Why? To avoid fitna and to respect the dignity of scholars.
This rule is a guard against gossip and stirring enmity.
3
Understand the defense: even if envy existed, it is natural
The author argues that great souls disdain equals, and if Juwayni felt anything, it is understandable because Ghazali was his student surpassing him. Yet this is speculation.
The author does not confirm the jealousy; he says 'if it exists, it is from this'.
4
Grasp the final distinction between 'product of hand' and 'product of God'
The young Ghazali was Juwayni's product (صنعة اليد), but the later Ghazali of al-Ihya and al-Munqidh was God's product (صنيعة الله). This elevates Ghazali beyond his teacher's circle.
This reflects a belief in divine guidance beyond human training.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان الإمام الجويني يعرف للغزالي قدره وفضله ، وأن الله أذن بحفظ علمه إذ حواه صدر ، فقال فيه وفي صاحبيه : ( الغزالي بحر مغرق ، والكيا أسد مخرق ، والخوافي نار تحرق ) . وقد حاول بعض المترجمين الدخول إلى طوايا النفوس واستنطاق مكامنها في حديثهم عن الجويني والغزالي حين قالوا : ( وكان الإمام مع علو درجته لا يصفي نظره إلى الغزالي ؛ ستراً لإنافته عليه في سرعة العبارة وقوة الطبع ، ولا يطيب له تصديه للتصانيف وإن كان منتسباً إليه كما لا يخفى من طباع البشر ، لكنه يظهرُ التبجُحَ به والاعتداد بمكانه ظاهراً خلاف ما يضمره ) . وكان الإمام أبو المعالي مع علو درجته وفرط ذكائه لا يطيب له تصديه للتصنيف وإن كان في الظاهر مبتهجاً به . ( ويقال : إن الإمام كان بالأخرة يمتعض منه في الباطن وإن كان يظهر التبجح به في الظاهر ) . وهذا الاستنطاق خارج عن قاعدتهم المشهورة : كلام الأقران يطوى ولا يروى ، ولولا أننا في صدد بحث وتتبع شبه .. لكان الإعراض أولى من الذكر . والغزالي كاد يطبع علم الجويني في صدره ثم يزيد عليه ، والنفوس الكبار تأنف من الند فضلاً عن المجلي ، فكيف بمن هو صنعة اليد ؟! فهذا - إن وجد - فهو من هذا ؛ وإلا .. فحسب الإمام الجويني أن الغزالي الشاب البارع الذي بذَ فحول عصره قد كان صنيعته ، أما غزالي « المنقذ » و « الإحياء » . . فهو صنيعة الله وحجته على خلقه !
Imam al-Juwayni recognized Ghazali's worth and virtue, and that God had permitted the preservation of his knowledge by containing it in his chest, so he said concerning him and his two companions: 'al-Ghazali is an overwhelming sea, al-Kiya is a ripping lion, and al-Khawafi is a burning fire.' And some biographers attempted to enter into the recesses of souls and interrogate their hidden places in their talk about al-Juwayni and al-Ghazali, saying: 'The Imam, despite his high rank, did not view al-Ghazali with a clear eye, as a cover for his superiority over him in quickness of expression and strength of nature, and he was not pleased with his undertaking of writing books, even though he was affiliated to him, as is not hidden from the nature of humans, but he outwardly showed boasting of him and taking pride in his status, contrary to what he concealed.' And Imam Abu al-Ma'ali, despite his high rank and extreme intelligence, was not pleased with his undertaking of authorship, even though he outwardly seemed delighted with it. And it is said: The Imam eventually became displeased with him inwardly, though he showed boasting of him outwardly. And this interrogation is outside their well-known rule: 'The talk of peers is folded and not narrated.' Were it not that we are in the context of research and investigation of doubts, it would be better to turn away than to mention it. And al-Ghazali almost imprinted al-Juwayni's knowledge in his chest and then increased upon it, and great souls disdain a peer, let alone one who defeats, so how about one who is the product of one's hand? So this—if it exists—is from this; otherwise, it is enough for Imam al-Juwayni that the brilliant young al-Ghazali, who surpassed the great scholars of his time, was his own product. As for the Ghazali of 'al-Munqidh' and 'al-Ihya'—he is God's product and His proof upon His creation!
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a biographical reflection on the relationship between Imams al-Juwayni and al-Ghazali. It begins by stating that Juwayni recognized Ghazali's merit and famously compared him and two other students to a sea, a lion, and a fire. Then it reports claims by some biographers that Juwayni, despite outward praise, inwardly harbored jealousy because Ghazali surpassed him in eloquence and natural talent, and was not pleased with Ghazali's authorship. The passage quotes three versions of this, and then notes that such probing of inner motives violates the accepted principle that 'the talk of peers is folded and not narrated'—that is, it is improper to transmit potentially divisive statements between colleagues. It then adds that even if there was some envy, it is understandable because great souls dislike equals, much less superiors, and Ghazali was Juwayni's own student. Finally, it distinguishes between the young Ghazali who was Juwayni's product and the later Ghazali of 'al-Munqidh' and 'al-Ihya' who was a direct creation of God.

Key Terms

الجويني
الكيا
الخوافي
كلام الأقران يطوى ولا يروى
صنعة اليد
صنيعة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Juwayni's reported jealousy means he was a bad teacher or that it tarnishes his reputation.
Correction: The passage explains that such reports violate scholarly etiquette, and even if true, it is a natural human reaction. Moreover, it emphasizes Ghazali's later status as God's own creation, not merely a student.

Checkpoint
What is the 'well-known rule' mentioned in the passage regarding the talk of peers?
A) 'The talk of peers is to be recorded and studied.'
B) 'The talk of peers is folded and not narrated.'
C) 'The talk of peers is always truthful.'
D) 'The talk of peers is to be ignored.'

Key Takeaway
Scholarly etiquette demands that the private talk of peers be concealed, and the true measure of a scholar is ultimately divine appointment, not human training alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a peer exceed you in knowledge or skill, does your outward praise ever mask inward resentment? How does Juwayni's reported struggle challenge you to purify your heart tonight?
?
How often do you listen to or share stories that cast doubt on the sincerity of a teacher or scholar? Will you now adopt the rule 'كلام الأقران يطوى ولا يروى' as a personal discipline?
?
Do you secretly attribute your spiritual or academic achievements to your own effort, forgetting that even your ability was given by God? If you, like al-Ghazali, become 'God's product', can you still be proud?

Life of Imam al-Ghazali

Ghazali's Rise After Juwayni
Describes Ghazali's rise and appointment to Baghdad Nizamiyya after Juwayni's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the pivotal role Imam al-Ghazali played after the death of his teacher al-Juwayni, and how his appointment to the Nizamiyya of Baghdad marked the peak of his early career.

Core Idea
Ghazali's appointment to Baghdad Nizamiyya was the turning point that elevated him as the leading scholar of his time.

This lesson places us at a critical juncture in Islamic intellectual history. After the death of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, the community found its champion in his star student, al-Ghazali. The passage recounts how Ghazali's brilliance caught the attention of the vizier Nizam al-Mulk, leading to his appointment at the most prestigious university of the Islamic world. As you read, notice how Ghazali's own words reveal his relentless pursuit of knowledge even while teaching hundreds of students.

Guided Path
1
Read the historical context
Read the passage to understand the sequence of events after Juwayni's death. Notice the year 478 AH and then 483 AH. The community's grief and Ghazali's role as comforter.
The passage uses emotional language: 'عزت نفسها ببقاء أبرز تلامذته'.
2
Identify Ghazali's encounter with Nizam al-Mulk
Focus on Ghazali's visit to the camp near Nishapur, his debating skills, and how he impressed the vizier.
The phrase 'ناظر الفحول فنظرهم' shows his superior argumentation.
3
Understand the significance of the appointment
The appointment to the Nizamiyya of Baghdad was a major honor. Note the distinction between the Nizamiyya of Nishapur (for his teacher) and Baghdad. Also note Ghazali's own account of teaching 300 students while studying philosophy.
The quote from Ghazali gives a personal glimpse into his methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما بعد الإمام الجويني : وفي سنة ( ٤٧٨ هـ ) رُزئت الأمةُ بوفاة إمام الحرمين الجويني ، وعزَّت نفسها ببقاء أبرز تلامذته الغزالي ، فكان وجوده سلوانها ، وكان لسانه وقلمه معقد آمالها ، وأهل الحق يومها أحوج ما يكونون لأنفاس كأنفاس الشيخ والتلميذ في الذب عن الشريعة وصاحبها صلى الله عليه وسلم . ولم تنطو بوفاة الإمام الجويني هذه المرحلة من حياة الإمام الغزالي ، بل إن إنافة الغزالي على المحالف والمخالف كانت في أشد أوجها ، ولا سيما بعد خروجه إلى المعسكر قريباً من نيسابور ؛ ليلقى الوزير الصالح نظام الملك ويغشى مجلسه ؛ إذ مجلسه مجمع أهل العلم وملاذهم ، وفيه وفي أمثاله ناظر الفحول فنظرهم ، وقارع الخصوم فقرعهم وأذلهم ، فكنَّ له نظام الملك الاحترام وعرف أمره ، وولاه التدريس في نظامية بغداد ـ لا نظامية نيسابور التي كان أمرها لشيخه الجويني - وذلك في سنة ( ٤٨٣ هـ ) أي : بعد وفاة الجويني بخمس سنوات ، ونظامية بغداد أبرز جامعة في الرقعة الإسلامية . يتحدث الإمام الغزالي عن هذه الفترة بالذات في سياق الكلام عن طلبه لعلوم الفلاسفة فيقول : ( فشمرت عن ساق الجد في تحصيل ذلك العلم من الكتب بمجرد المطالعة من غير أستاذ ، وأقبلت على ذلك في أوقات فراغي من التصنيف والتدريس في العلوم الشرعية وأنا ممنو بالتدريس والإفادة لثلاث مئة نفس من الطلبة ببغداد ، فأطلعني الله سبحانه وتعالى
After Imam al-Juwayni: In the year 478 AH, the community was afflicted by the death of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, and comforted itself by the survival of his most prominent student al-Ghazali. So his existence was its consolation, and his tongue and pen were the focus of its hopes. The people of truth at that time were in greatest need of breaths like those of the teacher and the student in defending the Sharia and its possessor, peace be upon him. And with the death of Imam al-Juwayni, this stage of Imam al-Ghazali's life did not come to an end; rather, Ghazali's superiority over allies and opponents was at its peak, especially after his departure to the camp near Nishapur to meet the righteous vizier Nizam al-Mulk and attend his council, for his council was a gathering place for scholars and their refuge. In it and others like it, he debated the brilliant scholars and outdebated them, and contended with opponents and defeated them and humiliated them. So Nizam al-Mulk showed him respect and recognized his worth, and appointed him to teach at the Nizamiyya of Baghdad—not the Nizamiyya of Nishapur which was under his teacher al-Juwayni—and that was in the year 483 AH, five years after the death of al-Juwayni. And the Nizamiyya of Baghdad was the most prominent university in the Islamic realm. Imam al-Ghazali speaks about this very period in the context of his pursuit of the sciences of the philosophers, saying: 'I rolled up my sleeves in earnest to acquire that knowledge from books by mere reading without a teacher, and I devoted myself to that in my free time from authorship and teaching in the religious sciences, while I was afflicted with teaching and imparting knowledge to three hundred students in Baghdad, then God Almighty informed me...'
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative describing the period after Juwayni's death. It states that the community grieved Juwayni's loss but found solace in Ghazali, who became the chief defender of the Sharia. Ghazali's influence grew, especially when he visited the vizier Nizam al-Mulk's court near Nishapur, where he debated and defeated scholars and opponents. Nizam al-Mulk was impressed and appointed him to teach at the prestigious Nizamiyya of Baghdad in 483 AH, five years after Juwayni's death. The passage then quotes Ghazali himself, who mentions that during this busy time of teaching three hundred students, he also secretly studied philosophy through books without a teacher.

نظام الملك
نظامية بغداد
نيسابور
الغزالي
الجويني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali stopped teaching after Juwayni's death.
Correction: The passage shows he became even more prominent, teaching at the Nizamiyya and instructing hundreds.

Checkpoint

A) 478 AH
B) 483 AH
C) 488 AH
D) 473 AH

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's appointment to the Baghdad Nizamiyya was the culmination of his early career, solidifying his role as the leading scholar of his time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you lose a spiritual guide or teacher, do you find comfort in continuing their mission, or do you let grief paralyze you? What one action can you take this week to carry forward a legacy that matters to you?
?
Ghazali's tongue and pen became the focus of the community's hopes. If you were stripped of all distractions today, would your words and writings be a source of hope for others? What change can you make starting today?
?
Ghazali defended the Sharia while simultaneously teaching 300 students and studying philosophy. Are you using your time and energy as wisely, or are you making excuses for laziness? What will you sacrifice today to pursue knowledge more earnestly?

Life of Imam al-Ghazali

Ghazali and Juwayni: Student and Divine Gift
The author evaluates Ghazali as both Juwayni's student and a divine gift.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the relationship between Imam al-Juwayni and his student al-Ghazali, and the author's distinction between Ghazali as a disciple and as a divine proof.

Core Idea
Great teachers shape students, but God alone shapes His proofs.

This lesson reflects on the unique bond between Imam al-Juwayni and his most famous student, al-Ghazali. The author acknowledges Juwayni's role in shaping the early Ghazali but makes a striking distinction when it comes to the later Ghazali, the author of 'al-Ihya' and 'al-Munqidh'. This passage reveals how the author views Ghazali as both a product of his teacher and a divine gift.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's flow come through before interpreting.
Notice the shift in description between the first and second parts.
2
Identify the two phases of Ghazali
The passage describes two distinct phases: the brilliant young student who surpassed his peers, and the later Ghazali who authored influential works. Identify the phrases that mark this shift.
Look for the words 'أما غزالي ( المنقذ » و « الإحياء )'.
3
Reflect on the author's tone toward Juwayni
Consider how the author praises Juwayni while still elevating the later Ghazali. Does this diminish Juwayni? The author explicitly says 'حسب الإمام الجويني' — it is enough for Juwayni that he taught the early Ghazali.
This balance shows deep respect for both figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغزالي كاد يطبع علم الجويني في صدره ثم يزيد عليه ، والنفوس الكبار تأنف من الند فضلاً عن المجلي ، فكيف بمن هو صنعة اليد ؟! فهذا - إن وجد - فهو من هذا ؛ وإلا .. فحسب الإمام الجويني أن الغزالي الشاب البارع الذي بذَ فحول عصره قد كان صنيعته ، أما غزالي ( المنقذ » و « الإحياء » . . فهو صنيعة الله وحجته على خلقه !
And al-Ghazali nearly imprinted the knowledge of al-Juwayni in his chest, then added to it. Great souls are averse to a peer, let alone a student, so how about one who is the product of [his] hand?! This one – if he exists – is from this; otherwise, it is sufficient for Imam al-Juwayni that the brilliant young al-Ghazali who surpassed the giants of his time was his disciple. As for the Ghazali of 'al-Munqidh' and 'al-Ihya'... he is the creation of God and His proof upon His creation!
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a biographical reflection by the author on the relationship between Imam al-Juwayni and his student al-Ghazali. The author begins by stating that al-Ghazali nearly absorbed Juwayni's knowledge and then increased upon it. He then notes that great souls disdain having a peer, let alone a student who surpasses them. The author raises the question: how about someone who is the very product of that teacher's hand? He then states that if such a student exists, he is from that teacher; otherwise, it is enough for Juwayni that the brilliant young Ghazali, who surpassed the scholars of his age, was his student. However, he distinguishes the later Ghazali of the works 'al-Munqidh' and 'Ihya' Ulum al-Din' as being directly from God and His proof upon creation, implying a divine transformation beyond Juwayni's influence.

صنعة
حجة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage criticizes Juwayni or suggests Ghazali outgrew him.
Correction: The passage honors Juwayni as the teacher who produced a brilliant student, yet clarifies that the later Ghazali was a direct creation of God, not a product of human teaching.

Checkpoint

A) As the perfect student of Juwayni
B) As God's creation and proof upon His creation
C) As a mere imitator
D) As a philosopher

Key Takeaway
The author honors Imam al-Juwayni as the teacher of the brilliant young Ghazali, but declares the later Ghazali, author of al-Ihya and al-Munqidh, to be a divine creation and proof upon creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed your own growth or success entirely to your teachers or mentors, forgetting that the final transformation comes from Allah? Where in your life have you taken credit for what was actually a divine gift? Commit today to acknowledging Allah's hand in your abilities.
?
The passage says great souls disdain a peer among their students. Does your heart feel threatened when someone you taught surpasses you? Are you able to rejoice in their success as a sign of God's favor, or does it trigger envy? Choose one relationship where you need to practice this humility, and resolve to celebrate their achievements as gifts from Allah.

Ghazali's Rise and Self-Taught Philosophy
Ghazali's career after Juwayni and his independent study of philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali rose to prominence after Juwayni's death and his unique method of studying philosophy independently through reading.

Core Idea
Ghazali's rise and his self-taught mastery of philosophy shaped his legacy.

This passage takes us into a pivotal period of Ghazali's life: after the death of his teacher al-Juwayni, Ghazali becomes a leading scholar, debates opponents, and is appointed to teach at the prestigious Nizamiyya of Baghdad. But even while teaching three hundred students, he secretly studies philosophy by reading books in his spare time, mastering their sciences in less than two years without a teacher. The source reveals both his public achievement and private intellectual quest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Notice the narrative flow from Juwayni's death to Ghazali's activities.
This passage is a mix of third-person biography and a first-person quote from Ghazali himself.
2
Identify the key events after Juwayni's death
List the events: the community's solace in Ghazali, his debates with opponents, meeting Nizam al-Mulk, and his appointment to teach at Nizamiyya Baghdad.
3
Examine Ghazali's description of studying philosophy
Read the direct quote from Ghazali. Note that he studied from books without a teacher, in his free time from teaching, and achieved mastery in less than two years.
This shows his dedication and intellectual independence.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Ghazali chose to study philosophy secretly while publicly teaching religious sciences. How did this shape his later works?
This study later allowed him to critique philosophy in 'The Incoherence of the Philosophers.'

After Imam al-Juwayni: In the year 478 AH, the community suffered the loss of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, and consoled itself with the survival of his most prominent student, al-Ghazali. His existence was its solace, and his tongue and pen were the focus of its hopes. The people of truth at that time were most in need of breaths like those of the teacher and student in defending the Sharia and its possessor. And with the death of Imam al-Juwayni, this stage of Imam al-Ghazali's life did not come to a close. Rather, al-Ghazali's superiority over allies and opponents was at its peak, especially after he went to the camp near Nishapur to meet the righteous vizier Nizam al-Mulk and attend his council; for his council was a gathering place and refuge for scholars. There and in similar gatherings, he debated the giants and silenced them, and contended with opponents and defeated and humiliated them. So Nizam al-Mulk accorded him respect and recognized his worth, and appointed him to teach at the Nizamiyya of Baghdad – not the Nizamiyya of Nishapur which had been assigned to his teacher al-Juwayni – and that was in the year 483 AH, i.e., five years after the death of al-Juwayni. The Nizamiyya of Baghdad was the most prominent university in the Islamic realm. Imam al-Ghazali speaks about this very period in the context of his study of the sciences of the philosophers, saying: 'Then I rolled up my sleeves in earnest to acquire that knowledge from books by mere reading without a teacher. I devoted myself to that in my free time from composing and teaching the religious sciences, while I was burdened with teaching and instructing three hundred students in Baghdad. Then God Almighty informed me through mere reading in these stolen moments of the ultimate of their sciences in less than two years. Glory has come to him, he has attained the heights of honor, and he has been received [with acclaim] so that he was seen to have no peer or equal, and his name has filled ears, engraved minds, and captured hearts.'
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a biographical narrative describing Ghazali's career after the death of his teacher Juwayni. It first states the loss felt by the community and how Ghazali's continued existence was a solace. It then describes Ghazali's rise, his meeting with Nizam al-Mulk, his appointment to the Nizamiyya of Baghdad, and his concurrent study of philosophy. The author then quotes Ghazali's own account from al-Munqidh min al-Dalal, where Ghazali says he studied philosophy through books without a teacher during his free time while teaching three hundred students, and that in less than two years, he gained mastery of their sciences, achieving fame and honor.

Key Terms
نظامية بغداد
العلوم الشرعية
الفلاسفة
مطالعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali studied philosophy secretly with a teacher or through formal instruction.
Correction: Ghazali's own account says he studied philosophy solely by reading books in his spare time without a teacher.

Checkpoint
How did Ghazali study the sciences of the philosophers according to his own account?
A) Under a teacher at the Nizamiyya
B) Through reading books in his free time without a teacher
C) By traveling to philosophers
D) He did not study philosophy

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's mastery of philosophy was achieved through independent reading in his spare time while he was already a prominent teacher of religious sciences.
