The Turn to Sufism: Knowledge and Experience
Ghazali recounts his turn to Sufism and distinguishes between learned knowledge and experiential taste.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's initial turn to Sufism and his discovery that the essence of the Sufi path lies in experiential knowledge (dhawq) rather than mere book learning.

Core Idea
Sufi knowledge is not learned—it is tasted.

In this passage from his spiritual autobiography, al-Ghazali recounts his personal shift from academic pursuits to the path of the Sufis. He describes how he sought knowledge through books but came to realize that the core of Sufism is an inner state that cannot be acquired by study alone. This lesson introduces that crucial insight and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the analogy of health and satiety at the end.
2
Identify Ghazali's starting point
Note that he had finished ‘other sciences’ and then turned to Sufism. Why do you think he did that?
3
Compare knowledge and action
Ghazali says the Sufi path requires both knowledge and action, but knowledge was easier for him. What does this tell you about his initial approach?
4
Understand the analogy
The analogy of health and satiety is key. Reflect on the difference between knowing definitions and actually being healthy or sated. How does this relate to spiritual knowledge?
This is the concept of dhawq (taste).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النزوع إلى طريق القوم : لنصغِ متأملين لكلمات الإمام الرشيقة وهو يحدثنا عن ولادته الجديدة ، وعن أسبابها ودواعيها : ( ثم إني لما فرغت من هذه العلوم .. أقبلت بهمتي على طريق الصوفية ، وعلمت أن طريقهم إنما تتم بعلم وعمل وكان العلم أيسر علي من العمل ؛ فابتدأت بتحصيل علمهم من مطالعة كتبهم ؛ مثل : ( قوت القلوب )) لأبي طالب المكي رحمه الله ، وكتب « الحارث » المحاسبي والمتفرقات المأثورة عن الجنيد والشبلي وأبي يزيد البسطامي قدس الله أرواحهم ... فظهر لي أن أخص خواصهم ما لا يمكن الوصول إليه بالتعلم ، بل بالذوق أنَّ والحال وتبدل الصفات ، وكم من الفرق أن تعلم حدَّ الصحة وحد الشبع وأسبابهما و شروطهما ، وبين أن تكون صحيحاً شبعان ) ! ( وكان قد ظهر عندي أنه لا مطمع لي في السعادة الآخرة إلا بالتقوى ، وكفّ النفس عن الهوى ، وأن رأسَ ذلك كله قطع علاقة القلب عن الدنيا . وأن ذلك لا يتم إلا بالإعراض عن الجاه والمال ، والهرب من الشواغل والعلائق . ثم لاحظت أحوالي ، فإذا أنا منغمس في العلائق ، وقد أحدقت بي من الجوانب ولاحظت أعمالي - وأحسنها التدريس والتعليم - فإذا أنا فيها مقبل على علوم غير مهمة ولا نافعة في طريق الآخرة ) فلم أزل أتفكر فيه - أي : في حاله - مدةً وأنا بعد على مقام الاختيار ، أصمم العزم على الخروج من بغداد ومفارقة تلك الأحوال يوماً ، وأحلُّ العزم يوماً ، وأقدم فيه رجلاً وأؤخر أخرى ، لا تصدق لي رغبة في طلب الآخرة بكرة إلا ويحمل عليها جند الشهوة حملة (۱) المنقذ من الضلال ( ص ٤١ ) .
The inclination towards the way of the [Sufi] people: Let us listen reflectively to the elegant words of the Imam as he tells us about his new birth and its causes and motives: (Then, when I had finished with these sciences, I turned my intention to the way of the Sufis. I knew that their way is completed only by knowledge and action, and knowledge was easier for me than action. So I began by acquiring their knowledge through reading their books, such as 'Qut al-Qulub' by Abu Talib al-Makki, the books of al-Harith al-Muhasibi, and the scattered reports transmitted from al-Junayd, al-Shibli, and Abu Yazid al-Bistami... It became clear to me that their most specific characteristic is what cannot be attained by learning, but by taste (dhawq), state (hal), and transformation of attributes. How great is the difference between knowing the definition of health and satiety and their causes and conditions, and between being healthy and sated!) (And it had become apparent to me that I have no hope of happiness in the Hereafter except through piety and restraining the soul from passion, and that the head of all that is cutting the heart's attachment to this world. And that is not completed except by turning away from status and wealth, and fleeing from distractions and attachments. Then I examined my states, and behold, I was immersed in attachments which had surrounded me from all sides. I looked at my deeds – the best of them being teaching and instruction – and behold, I was engaged in sciences that were not important nor beneficial for the path to the Hereafter.) So I continued to reflect on it – i.e., his state – for a time while still in a position of choice. One day I would resolve to leave Baghdad and abandon those states, and another day I would undo the resolve. I would advance one foot and retreat the other. No sooner would a desire for the Hereafter arise in the morning than the army of passion would launch an attack. (Al-Munqidh min al-Dalal, p. 41)
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the section titled 'The inclination towards the way of the [Sufi] people.' The author introduces Ghazali's own words from his spiritual autobiography. Ghazali describes his turn to Sufism after finishing other sciences. He explains that the Sufi path requires both knowledge and action, and since knowledge was easier, he started reading Sufi books. He realized that the essence of Sufism is not learned but experienced through taste (dhawq) and inner transformation, using the analogy of knowing about health versus being healthy. He then articulates his conviction that happiness in the afterlife requires piety, controlling the soul, and cutting worldly attachments. Examining his own life, he found himself immersed in worldly ties and his teaching to be of little benefit for the afterlife. He describes his inner struggle, fluctuating between resolve to leave Baghdad and hesitation, as worldly desires attacked his spiritual aspirations. The passage ends with a footnote citing al-Munqidh.

Key Terms
طريق الصوفية
الذوق
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali completely abandoned book knowledge for blind mysticism.
Correction: He valued book knowledge as a foundation, but emphasized that the essence of Sufism requires experiential taste beyond learning.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the most specific characteristic of the Sufis that cannot be attained by learning?
A) Knowledge of books
B) Piety and restraining passion
C) Something attained by taste and inner state
D) Cutting worldly attachments

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's turn to Sufism taught him that the core of the path is experiential taste (dhawq), not just intellectual knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have studied many lessons about Islam, but how many of them have changed you from the inside out? Ghazali knew the definitions of health and satiety, but that is not the same as being healthy and sated. In which area of your life are you still only 'knowing the definition'? What is one step you can take today to actually 'taste' that reality?
?
Ghazali humbly admitted that action was harder than knowledge. What is one truth you have learned but never acted upon? Will you continue to let your knowledge remain dry theory, or will you let it transform your state? Write down the first action you will take before the day ends.
?
You have access to countless books and lectures on the spiritual path. But if your heart remains unchanged, what value do they have? Are you content with being a collector of definitions, or do you hunger for the taste? What will you sacrifice this week to move from knowing about God to knowing God?

The Inner Struggle: Detachment from Worldly Attachments
Ghazali explains the necessity of piety and detachment, and recounts his inner conflict about leaving Baghdad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's conviction that happiness in the afterlife requires piety and detachment from the world, and his personal struggle to act on that conviction.

Core Idea
Cutting worldly ties is the path to eternal happiness — but the soul resists.

After recognizing the nature of true Sufi knowledge, Ghazali faced a deeper challenge: his own attachment to status, wealth, and worldly engagements. He realized that without cutting these ties, he could not achieve the happiness he sought. This passage captures his inner turmoil, as he resolved to leave Baghdad only to be held back by his desires.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the emotional intensity of his struggle.
2
Identify the three requirements
Ghazali states that happiness in the Hereafter requires piety (taqwa), restraining the soul (kaff al-nafs), and cutting the heart's attachment to this world. Find these in the text.
He calls cutting attachment the 'head of all that'.
3
Examine his self-assessment
He then examines his own state and finds himself immersed in attachments. He also judges his teaching as 'not important nor beneficial' for the afterlife. Why is this so striking?
He was a famous professor in Baghdad.
4
Witness his internal conflict
His description of wavering between resolve and hesitation shows the depth of the struggle. What does the metaphor 'army of passion' suggest?

Al-Ghazali's Turn to Sufism: From Learning to Experience
Al-Ghazali narrates his turn to Sufism and his realization of experiential knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between theoretical knowledge and direct experiential knowledge in Sufism as described by Imam al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Sufism’s essence cannot be learned from books but must be tasted.

Al-Ghazali describes his personal journey after mastering the sciences. He turned to Sufism, initially studying its books, but soon realized that the core of Sufism is not intellectual but experiential. This lesson explores his distinction between knowing definitions (like health) and actually experiencing the state (being healthy).

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ghazali's autobiographical passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the tone—he is reflecting on his own journey.
2
Identify the two components of the Sufi path
Al-Ghazali says the path is completed by knowledge and action. But he found knowledge easier. Find this in the text.
3
Understand the key insight: taste vs. learning
He says the most specific characteristic is attained by taste/state/change, not learning. Reflect on the analogy of health and satiety.
This is the core idea.
4
Consider the implications for your own learning
Think about why al-Ghazali emphasizes direct experience. How does this apply to religious knowledge?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Let us listen attentively to the elegant words of the Imam as he tells us about his new birth, its causes and motivations: 'Then, when I had finished these sciences, I turned my attention to the way of the Sufis. I realized that their way is only completed by knowledge and action, and knowledge was easier for me than action. So I began to acquire their knowledge by studying their books, such as Qut al-Qulub by Abu Talib al-Makki, and the books of al-Harith al-Muhasibi, and the scattered sayings transmitted from al-Junayd, al-Shibli, and Abu Yazid al-Bistami. Commentary
This passage is a biographical narrative from al-Ghazali's autobiography, quoted by the editor. It describes al-Ghazali's turn to Sufism after completing other sciences. He states that the Sufi path requires both knowledge and action, and he found knowledge easier, so he began by studying their books. He lists key Sufi works: Qut al-Qulub by Abu Talib al-Makki, books of al-Harith al-Muhasibi, and sayings of Junayd, Shibli, and Bayazid. He then makes a crucial point: the most specific characteristic of the Sufis is not attainable by learning but by taste (dhawq) and a change of state and attributes. To illustrate, he contrasts knowing the definition and causes of health and satiety with actually being healthy and full. This emphasizes the experiential nature of Sufi knowledge.

B) studying books

?

?
The difference between knowing about health and being healthy is vast. Have you reduced your deen to a set of concepts and definitions, or do you actively seek to embody its truths? What change of attributes is required in you right now?

Al-Ghazali's Spiritual Crisis and Struggle to Leave Baghdad
Al-Ghazali reflects on his attachments, resolves to leave Baghdad, and experiences a crisis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of al-Ghazali's spiritual crisis, his struggle between worldly attachments and the call of the Hereafter, and how his transformation was misinterpreted by others.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's spiritual transformation was a painful birth of election, not a worldly ambition.

Al-Ghazali's spiritual crisis was not a mere intellectual doubt but a profound existential struggle. Having realized the necessity of cutting worldly attachments for the Hereafter, he found himself entangled in them. This passage captures his inner turmoil, his attempts to resolve, and the eventual overpowering compulsion to change. It also shows how his transformation was misunderstood by those around him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the vivid imagery used by al-Ghazali.
2
Identify the initial realization
Al-Ghazali begins by stating what he understood about the Hereafter: piety, restraining the soul, cutting attachment to the world. Find those elements in the text.
3
Understand the internal conflict
Notice the back-and-forth: one day resolving to leave, the next day hesitating. The 'army of desire' and 'caller of faith' are powerful metaphors. Describe the struggle.
The conflict is between choice (ikhtiyar) and compulsion (idtirar).
4
Observe the outcome and misinterpretation
The crisis culminates in a shift from choice to compulsion, described as 'pangs of birth'. Al-Ghazali becomes unable to speak, his heart troubled. Others misinterpret: evil eye or ambition. Note the contrast.
Al-Ghazali insists they were ignorant of the truth.
5
Reflect on the imagery
Consider how the images (chains, army, caller) convey the intensity of the struggle. How might this apply to spiritual seekers today?
This is a classic example of spiritual autobiography.

وكان قد ظهر عندي أنه لا مطمع لي في السعادة الآخرة إلا بالتقوى ، وكفّ النفس عن الهوى ، وأن رأسَ ذلك كله قطع علاقة القلب عن الدنيا . وأن ذلك لا يتم إلا بالإعراض عن الجاه والمال ، والهرب من الشواغل والعلائق · , ثم لاحظت أحوالي ، فإذا أنا منغمس في العلائق ، وقد أحدقت بي من الجوانب ولاحظت أعمالي - وأحسنها التدريس والتعليم - فإذا أنا فيها مقبل على علوم غير مهمة ولا نافعة في طريق الآخرة ) فلم أزل أتفكر فيه - أي : في حاله - مدةً وأنا بعد على مقام الاختيار ، أصمم العزم على الخروج من بغداد ومفارقة تلك الأحوال يوماً ، وأحلُّ العزم يوماً ، وأقدم فيه رجلاً وأؤخر أخرى ، لا تصدق لي رغبة في طلب الآخرة بكرة إلا ويحمل عليها جند الشهوة حملة فيفترها عشية ، فصارت شهوات الدنيا تجاذبني بسلاسلها إلى المقام ، ومنادي الإيمان ينادي : الرحيل الرحيل ؛ فلم يبق من العمر إلا قليل ، وبين يديك سفر طويل )(١) وقد كان ذلك في سنة ( ٤٨٨ هـ ) وفي ستة أشهر منها هي مخاض الولادة ، حتى ظهرت مخايل الاصطفاء ، وآب هذا الحال من الاختيار إلى الاضطرار ، فانعقد لسان البيان عن الكلام، وسكن الهمُّ الفؤادَ فتعدى الأثر إلى سائر الجوارح ، وتغيَّر كلُّ شيء في الإمام الغزالي ولم يعد على مألوف حاله ؛ فبين قائل : أصابته عين ، وآخر يرى تهممه لمنصب هو أرفع ، وكلهم غائب عن حقيقة الأمر
And it had become clear to me that there is no hope for me in the happiness of the Hereafter except through piety, restraining the soul from passion, and that the head of all that is cutting the heart's attachment to this world. And that cannot be completed except by turning away from status and wealth, and fleeing from distractions and attachments. Then I observed my states, and behold I was immersed in attachments, surrounding me from all sides. And I looked at my works - the best of which were teaching and education - and behold I was engaged in sciences that are not important nor beneficial for the path of the Hereafter. So I continued to think about it - i.e., about my state - for a time while I was still in a state of choice. One day I would firmly resolve to leave Baghdad and abandon those states, and another day I would loosen that resolve, putting one foot forward and drawing the other back. No desire for the Hereafter would become sincere in the morning except that the army of desire would attack it in the evening and weaken it. The desires of the world pulled me with their chains to remain, while the caller of faith called out: 'Depart, depart! For little remains of life, and before you is a long journey.' And that was in the year 488 AH, six months of which were the pangs of birth, until the signs of election appeared. And this state turned from choice to compulsion. The tongue of expression became tied about speech, and worry settled my heart, then the effect spread to all limbs. Everything changed in Imam al-Ghazali and he was no longer as he used to be. Some said: 'He is struck by the evil eye,' while others thought he was aspiring for a higher position. But all were ignorant of the reality of the matter.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage continues al-Ghazali's autobiographical account. He first states the rationale for seeking the Hereafter: piety, restraining the soul, cutting attachment to the world, and avoiding status and wealth. Then he reflects on his own state, finding himself immersed in attachments and his teaching and learning unsuited for the Hereafter. He describes his inner struggle: one day resolving to leave Baghdad, the next day weakening. He uses powerful imagery: the army of desire attacking his desire for the Hereafter in the evening, and the world's desires pulling him back with chains, while the caller of faith urges departure. This crisis culminates in the year 488 AH, described as 'pangs of birth' until signs of election appear. He shifts from choice to compulsion, losing ability to speak, his heart troubled, and his whole being changed. People misinterpreted his state: some said he was affected by the evil eye, others thought he was angling for a higher position. But al-Ghazali stresses they were ignorant of the truth.

التقوى (piety)
قطع علاقة القلب عن الدنيا (cutting heart's attachment to world)
الاختيار (choice)
الاضطرار (compulsion)
مخاض الولادة (pangs of birth)
الاصطفاء (election)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People thought al-Ghazali was struck by the evil eye or seeking a higher position.
Correction: Al-Ghazali emphasizes it was genuine spiritual transformation, a result of compulsion from faith.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's crisis was not a failure but a painful birth of spiritual election, and it was misunderstood by those around him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ghazali realized that his best works, teaching and learning, were not beneficial for the Hereafter. Write down one such activity and decide today how you will begin to detach from it.
?
The 'caller of faith' in your heart is urging you to depart from worldly attachments, but the 'army of desire' pulls you back with chains. Where are you most aware of this tug-of-war in your life right now? What would it cost you to actually listen to the caller and take one concrete step toward leaving what holds you back?
?
People around al-Ghazali misunderstood his transformation, thinking he was struck by an evil eye or seeking promotion. If you were to undergo a radical spiritual change, would you fear being misunderstood by your family or peers? What does that fear reveal about your priorities—are you more attached to their approval than to God's pleasure?

Al-Ghazali's Journey and Seclusion
Al-Ghazali sells his property, travels to Syria and Jerusalem, performs Hajj, and returns to Tus for seclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the transformative journey of Imam al-Ghazali after his crisis, including his travels and prolonged seclusion.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's outward journey mirrored his inward transformation.

This passage narrates a key turning point in al-Ghazali's life after his intellectual and spiritual crisis. It traces his physical movements from Syria to Jerusalem and Mecca, and finally to his seclusion in Tus, showing how his external renunciation reflected a deep internal reorientation. Through this account, we see the practical embodiment of his spiritual ideals.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the overall story. Notice the sequence of locations and actions.
The passage includes a firsthand account from a student who saw al-Ghazali in his later ascetic state.
2
Identify the key phases of his journey
Break down the journey: selling property, stay in Syria, visit to Jerusalem, pilgrimage, return and seclusion. Each phase has a distinct spiritual purpose.
The phrase 'disciplining the soul, striving, and purification' captures the essence of his efforts.
3
Contrast his earlier and later life
Compare the description of his lecture hall with 400 scholars to his later appearance in the wilderness with a patched cloak. This contrast illustrates the depth of his transformation.
The witnesses' report reinforces that his change was both inner and outer.
4
Reflect on the purpose of seclusion
Consider why al-Ghazali chose seclusion for eleven years. The text mentions 'solitude and purification of the heart' as his goals.
Seclusion was not withdrawal from responsibility but a deliberate spiritual discipline.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرحلة إلى الشام والقدس ثم الحج : فباع الأملاك وخرج عن ماله ، ولم يترك إلا يسيراً لكفاف عياله ، ثم دخل الشام وأقام بها نحو سنتين ، لا هم له فيها إلا رياضة النفس ومجاهدتها وتزكيتها ، كان ذلك في منارة مسجدها الجامع بدمشق ثم رحل إلى القدس ، وكان حاله فيها كحاله في دمشق ثم تحركت داعية الحج والاستمداد من بركات مكة والمدينة ، فحج وزار ، ثم ارتحل إلى وطنه نزولاً عند رغبة الأطفال ، فلما عاد طوس .. أثر العزلة حرصاً على الخلوة وتصفية القلب (۲) (۳) إحدى عشرة سنة بقي فيها الإمام على هذه الحال ، وهو مستوحش من الخلق ، مقبل بكله على ربه جل وعز ، من رآه .. لم يكد يعرفه ؛ فقد تغيرت النفس ظاهراً وباطناً ، وثمة لوحة تؤكد لنا هذا المعنى ؛ فقد نقل ابن العماد عن الشيخ علاء الدين علي بن الصيرفي في. كتابه « زاد السالكين » ، أن القاضي أبا بكر بن العربي قال : رأيت الإمام الغزالي في البرية وبيده عكازة وعليه مرقعة وعلى عاتقه ركوة ، وقد كنت رأيته ببغداد يحضر مجلس درسه نحو أربع مئة عمامة من أكابر الناس وأفاضلهم يأخذون عنه (۱) المنقذ من الضلال ( ص ٦٤- ٦٧ ) (۲) الأنس الجليل ( ١/ ٢٩٩ ) (۳) المنقذ من الضلال ( ص ٦٨ ) . ١٦
The journey to Syria, Jerusalem, then Hajj: So he sold his properties and gave up his wealth, leaving only a little for the sufficiency of his family. Then he entered Syria and stayed there for about two years, having no concern except disciplining the soul, striving against it, and purifying it. That was in the minaret of the Grand Mosque of Damascus. Then he traveled to Jerusalem, and his state there was like his state in Damascus. Then the call for Hajj and seeking blessings from Mecca and Medina stirred, so he performed Hajj and visited. Then he departed to his homeland in response to the desire of his children. When he returned to Tus, he chose seclusion, keen on solitude and purification of the heart. For eleven years the Imam remained in this state, estranged from people, turning completely to his Lord. Whoever saw him would hardly recognize him, for his soul had changed outwardly and inwardly. There is a report that confirms this meaning: Ibn al-‘Imad transmitted from Sheikh ‘Ala’ al-Din ‘Ali ibn al-Sayrafi in his book Zad al-Salikin that the judge Abu Bakr ibn al-‘Arabi said: I saw Imam al-Ghazali in the wilderness, with a staff in his hand, wearing a patched cloak, and a waterskin on his shoulder. And I had seen him in Baghdad attending his lesson session with about four hundred turbans of the elite and virtuous people taking from him.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage narrates al-Ghazali's physical and spiritual journey after his crisis. He sold his properties, left only enough for his family, and went to Syria. For two years he focused on disciplining and purifying his soul in the minaret of the Damascus mosque. Then he went to Jerusalem with similar devotion, then performed Hajj. Finally, he returned to Tus at his children's request and chose seclusion for eleven years. During this time he was estranged from people and completely devoted to God. His appearance changed so much that people did not recognize him. To support this, the editor quotes a report from Ibn al-‘Arabī who saw al-Ghazali in the wilderness with a staff, patched cloak, and waterskin, contrasting with his earlier days when four hundred distinguished scholars attended his lessons in Baghdad. This illustrates the profound transformation.

مجاهدة (striving)
تزكية (purification)
العزلة (seclusion)
تصفية القلب (purification of heart)

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's eleven-year seclusion was the culmination of his spiritual quest, symbolizing complete devotion to Allah and a radical transformation from worldly fame to ascetic piety.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Ghazali spent eleven years in seclusion, estranged from people, turning completely to his Lord. Can you honestly say you have ever spent even one full day alone with Allah, without distractions? If not, what is the first step you will take this week to carve out time for solitude and purification of the heart?

Ghazali's Spiritual Crisis and Decision to Leave Teaching
Ghazali narrates his inner struggle and the divine call to leave teaching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual crisis that led Imam al-Ghazali to abandon his teaching position and the transformative process he underwent as described in his own words.

Core Idea
Ghazali's crisis was a divine call, not a worldly ambition.

In this passage from his spiritual autobiography, Imam al-Ghazali recounts the climax of his inner struggle. He describes how the pull of worldly honors clashed with the urgent call of faith, leading to a profound transformation that forced him to leave his prestigious teaching post. This lesson explores that turning point and the reactions of those around him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the narrative flow. Notice the metaphors and the emotional tone.
The passage is highly personal and metaphorical.
2
Identify the conflict
Find the two opposing forces: worldly desires and the caller of faith. Understand how they pull Ghazali in different directions.
The phrase 'chains of the world' and 'caller of faith' are key.
3
Analyze the transformation
Note the change from choice to compulsion and the physical symptoms described. How does Ghazali describe the change in himself?
This is not a gradual change but a sudden turning point.
4
Examine others' reactions
Consider how people misinterpreted Ghazali's condition. What does this reveal about the nature of spiritual experiences?
The text says all were unaware of the true reality.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider the significance of this event in Ghazali's life and in Islamic intellectual history. What does it teach us about the relationship between worldly life and spiritual calling?
This crisis led to his famous work 'The Deliverer from Error.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيفترها عشية ، فصارت شهوات الدنيا تجاذبني بسلاسلها إلى المقام ، ومنادي الإيمان ينادي : الرحيل الرحيل ؛ فلم يبق من العمر إلا قليل ، وبين يديك سفر طويل . وقد كان ذلك في سنة ( ٤٨٨ هـ ) وفي ستة أشهر منها هي مخاض الولادة ، حتى ظهرت مخايل الاصطفاء ، وآب هذا الحال من الاختيار إلى الاضطرار ، فانعقد لسان البيان عن الكلام، وسكن الهمُّ الفؤادَ فتعدى الأثر إلى سائر الجوارح ، وتغيَّر كلُّ شيء في الإمام الغزالي ولم يعد على مألوف حاله ؛ فبين قائل : أصابته عين ، وآخر يرى تهممه لمنصب هو أرفع ، وكلهم غائب عن حقيقة الأمر
He would break them in the evening. The pleasures of this world began to pull me with their chains toward the station, while the caller of faith called: 'Depart, depart! There remains but little of life, and before you is a long journey.' That was in the year 488 AH, and six months of it were the throes of birth, until the signs of election appeared, and this state turned from choice to compulsion. The tongue of speech was tied, anxiety settled in the heart, and the effect extended to all the limbs. Everything changed in Imam al-Ghazali, and he did not return to his usual state. Some said: 'He is smitten by the evil eye,' and others saw him aspiring to a higher position, but all were unaware of the true reality.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage describes the climax of Imam al-Ghazali's spiritual crisis. It begins with a metaphorical statement about breaking worldly attachments in the evening, leading to a conflict between worldly desires and the call of faith. The phrase 'the pleasures of this world began to pull me with their chains' illustrates the powerful attachment to his prestigious position, while 'the caller of faith' represents his inner conviction urging him to leave. He emphasizes the urgency by noting the shortness of remaining life and the long journey ahead. The event occurred in 488 AH, and the six-month period is likened to the pains of childbirth, indicating a transformative process. He then describes the outcome: his speech about knowledge ceased, anxiety filled his heart, and his entire being changed. People speculated about him—some said he was afflicted by jealousy, others thought he was seeking a higher rank—but all missed the true spiritual cause. This passage is a personal narration of a turning point in Ghazali's life, leading to his famous withdrawal from teaching.

Key Terms

منادي الإيمان
الاختيار
الاضطرار
مخايل الاصطفاء

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The text makes clear it was a profound spiritual compulsion, with signs of divine election.

Key Takeaway
This passage shows that Ghazali's decision to leave teaching was not a personal choice but a divine compulsion that changed his entire being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
People around Ghazali misunderstood him. Have you ever been misunderstood by others when you made a decision for spiritual reasons? How did you handle it? What does this passage teach you about dealing with misjudgment?

Ghazali's Journey of Spiritual Transformation
Narrative of Ghazali's ascetic journey.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key stages of Imam al-Ghazali's physical and spiritual journey after his crisis, and how he embodied the principles of self-discipline and purification.

Core Idea
Ghazali's journey from wealth to asceticism mirrors the soul's path to purification.

Imam al-Ghazali's life took a dramatic turn after his intellectual and spiritual crisis. He abandoned his prestigious position and worldly possessions to embark on a journey of self-purification. This passage traces his steps from selling his property to his travels in Syria, Jerusalem, and ultimately to Hajj, before returning to his family.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation to get a sense of Ghazali's actions and the flow of his journey.
Notice the sequence of events: selling, traveling, spiritual practice, pilgrimage, return.
2
Identify the stages
List the main stages: selling possessions, staying in Syria (two years in Damascus), moving to Jerusalem, performing Hajj, and returning home.
Each stage has a purpose—asceticism, purification, seeking blessings, family duty.
3
Reflect on spiritual practices
Focus on the terms 'riyada al-nafs', 'mujahada al-nafs', and 'tazkiya al-nafs'. Consider what these mean in the context of Ghazali's retreat.
These are core Sufi concepts of discipline, struggle, and purification.
4
Consider the return
Ask why Ghazali returned home. How does this balance his spiritual quest with worldly responsibilities?
He returned for his children, showing that renunciation does not mean abandoning duties.
5
Connect to the lesson objective
Summarize how this journey exemplifies the transformation from worldly attachment to spiritual focus, and how it relates to the broader theme of self-purification in Islamic spirituality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرحلة إلى الشام والقدس ثم الحج : فباع الأملاك وخرج عن ماله ، ولم يترك إلا يسيراً لكفاف عياله ، ثم دخل الشام وأقام بها نحو سنتين ، لا هم له فيها إلا رياضة النفس ومجاهدتها وتزكيتها ، كان ذلك في منارة مسجدها الجامع بدمشق ثم رحل إلى القدس ، وكان حاله فيها كحاله في دمشق ثم تحركت داعية الحج والاستمداد من بركات مكة والمدينة ، فحج وزار ، ثم ارتحل إلى وطنه نزولاً عند رغبة الأطفال
The journey to Syria, Jerusalem, then Hajj: So he sold the properties and left his wealth, leaving only a little for the sufficiency of his family. Then he entered Syria and stayed there for about two years, having no concern except self-discipline, struggle, and purification. That was in the minaret of its congregational mosque in Damascus. Then he traveled to Jerusalem, and his state there was like his state in Damascus. Then the motive for Hajj and seeking blessings from Mecca and Medina stirred, so he performed Hajj and visited. Then he returned to his homeland in compliance with the desire of his children.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage narrates the practical steps Ghazali took after his spiritual crisis. He sold his properties and distributed most of his wealth, keeping only enough for his family's basic needs. He then traveled to Syria (al-Sham) and spent about two years there, focusing entirely on disciplining his soul (riyada al-nafs), struggling against its lower desires (mujahada), and purifying it (tazkiya). His location was the minaret of the Umayyad Mosque in Damascus, indicating a life of asceticism and devotion. He then moved to Jerusalem, maintaining the same spiritual practices. Afterwards, the desire to perform Hajj and benefit from the blessings of Mecca and Medina arose, so he completed the pilgrimage and visited the holy sites. Finally, he returned to his hometown at the request of his children. This passage shows Ghazali's total transformation from a world-renowned scholar to a wandering ascetic.

Key Terms
رياضة النفس
مجاهدة النفس
تزكية النفس
الحج
منارة مسجد دمشق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali abandoned all responsibility and left his family without support.
Correction: He left enough provision for his family's sufficiency (كفاف عياله) and later returned to his homeland in compliance with his children's desire, showing a balance between spiritual retreat and familial duty.

Checkpoint
How long did Ghazali stay in Syria and what was his main activity there?
A) One year, teaching students.
B) Two years, disciplining and purifying his soul.
C) Three years, writing books.
D) Six months, visiting holy sites.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's physical journey from wealth to asceticism, through Syria, Jerusalem, and Hajj, was a tangible expression of his inner spiritual struggle and purification, culminating in a balanced return to family responsibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your schedule were cleared of all worldly concerns except self-discipline, struggle, and purification, as Ghazali's was, what would you actually do today? Write down the first step.
?
Ghazali left wealth but still provided for his family and later returned for his children. Are you using your responsibilities as an excuse to avoid spiritual growth, or are you using your spiritual growth as an excuse to ignore your responsibilities? Where is the imbalance in your life?
?
When you perform Hajj or any act of worship, is it a continuation of your spiritual discipline or a one-time event? How can you make every day a journey like Ghazali's from Damascus to Jerusalem to Mecca?

Ghazali's Eleven-Year Retreat and Poetic Reply to Ibn al-Arabi
Ghazali's prolonged retreat and a poignant exchange with a former student.

By the end of this lesson, you will understand the depth of Ghazali's spiritual transformation during his retreat and the symbolic meaning of his poetic response to Ibn al-Arabi.

Ghazali abandoned worldly teaching for divine intimacy.

This lesson narrates a powerful moment from Ghazali's biography. After years of intense teaching in Baghdad, he withdrew completely. When a former student questioned his choice, Ghazali responded with a poem that reveals the inner logic of his retreat. The story illustrates the principle that spiritual attainment can supersede outward scholarly activity.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the contrast between Ghazali's former life and his retreat.
The poetry is a key element—note the shift in tone.

3

This is an allegory, not literal love poetry.

إحدى عشرة سنة بقي فيها الإمام على هذه الحال ، وهو مستوحش من الخلق ، مقبل بكله على ربه جل وعز ، من رآه .. لم يكد يعرفه ؛ فقد تغيرت النفس ظاهراً وباطناً ، وثمة لوحة تؤكد لنا هذا المعنى ؛ فقد نقل ابن العماد عن الشيخ علاء الدين علي بن الصيرفي في. كتابه « زاد السالكين » ، أن القاضي أبا بكر بن العربي قال : رأيت الإمام الغزالي في البرية وبيده عكازة وعليه مرقعة وعلى عاتقه ركوة ، وقد كنت رأيته ببغداد يحضر مجلس درسه نحو أربع مئة عمامة من أكابر الناس وأفاضلهم يأخذون عنه العلم ، قال : فدنوت منه وسلمت عليه وقلت له : يا إمام ؛ أليس تدريس العلم ببغداد خيراً من هذا ؟! قال : فنظر إلي شزراً وقال : لما طلع بدر السعادة في فلك الإرادة ـ أو قال : في سماء الإرادة - وجنحت شمس الوصول في مغارب الأصول : تَرَكْتُ هَوَى لَيْلَى وَسُعْدَى بِمَعْزِلِ وَعُدْتُ إِلَى مَصْحُوبِ أَوَّلِ مَنْزِلِ ونادَتْ بِي الأَشْواقُ مَهْلاً فَهَذِهِ مَنازِلُ مَنْ تَهْوَى رُوَيْدَكَ فَانْزِلِ غَزَلْتُ لَهُمْ غَوْلاً رَقِيقاً فَلَمْ أَجِدْ لِغَزلي نساجاً فَكَشَرْتُ مُغْزَلي
Eleven years the Imam remained in this state, feeling estranged from people, turning entirely to his Lord, Mighty and Majestic. Whoever saw him could hardly recognize him, for his soul had changed outwardly and inwardly. There is a scene that confirms this meaning: Ibn al-Imad narrated from Shaykh Ala al-Din Ali ibn al-Sayrafi in his book 'Zad al-Salikin' that Qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi said: I saw Imam al-Ghazali in the wilderness, with a staff in his hand, wearing a patched cloak, and a water-skin on his shoulder. I had seen him in Baghdad attending his lecture session with about four hundred turbans of the great and virtuous people learning from him. He said: So I approached him and greeted him and said to him: O Imam, is not teaching knowledge in Baghdad better than this? He said: He looked at me angrily and said: When the full moon of happiness rose in the orbit of will—or he said: in the sky of will—and the sun of arrival set in the west of foundations: I abandoned the love of Layla and Su'da aside, and returned to the companion of the first abode. Longings called me: 'Gently, for these are the abodes of the one you love, slowly descend.' I wove for them a delicate yarn, but found no weaver for my yarn, so I twisted my spindle.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage describes Ghazali's lengthy retreat of eleven years, during which he was alienated from people and completely devoted to God. His transformation was so profound that he was unrecognizable. A specific incident illustrates this: Qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi, who had seen Ghazali in Baghdad teaching hundreds of elite students, encountered him in the wilderness wearing a patched cloak and carrying a water-skin. The Qadi questioned whether teaching was not better than this ascetic life. Ghazali responded with poetry, explaining that when true spiritual happiness dawns (symbolized by the full moon and sun), one leaves behind worldly attachments (Layla and Su'da are common names in Arabic love poetry, here representing worldly desires) and returns to the original companion (God). He says he wove a delicate yarn for people but found no weaver (no one to appreciate it), so he twisted his spindle (abandoned the effort). The poetry emphasizes his complete turning away from fame and teaching toward direct communion with the Divine.

استيحاش من الخلق
مرقعة
ركوة
بدر السعادة
فلك الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poetry is simply romantic love poetry.
Correction: Ghazali uses Layla and Su'da as symbols for worldly attachments, and the 'first abode' represents God.

Checkpoint
Why did Ghazali respond with poetry about Layla and Su'da?
A) He was reciting a popular love poem.
B) He used them as symbols for worldly attachments he abandoned.
C) He was complaining about his lost love.
D) He wanted to confuse the qadi.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's retreat exemplifies the priority of spiritual realization over external teaching.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali left behind hundreds of students and a renowned teaching position to be alone with his Lord. What attachment—be it to approval, reputation, or achievement—are you clinging to that might be keeping you from deeper intimacy with Allah? Can you identify one concrete step to let it go starting today?
?
When asked why he abandoned teaching, Ghazali replied with a poem: 'I wove for them a delicate yarn, but found no weaver for my yarn, so I twisted my spindle.' In what area of your life are you expending energy on something that yields no spiritual fruit—perhaps because your motive is mixed with seeking people’s admiration? Will you have the courage to 'twist your spindle' and redirect your efforts solely for God?
?
The passage says whoever saw Ghazali during his retreat could hardly recognize him—his soul had changed outwardly and inwardly. If someone could see your inner state compared to your outer persona, would they perceive a similar transformation? What would need to change for your soul to reflect the reality of your faith rather than the image you project?

Ghazali's Spiritual Training Under Shaykh al-Faramadhi
Ghazali's spiritual training under the ascetic scholar Abu Ali al-Faramadhi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of Shaykh al-Faramadhi in Ghazali's spiritual development and how his guidance influenced Ghazali's later seclusion.

Core Idea
A spiritual mentor can transform intellectual maturity into spiritual depth.

This passage highlights a pivotal relationship in Imam al-Ghazali's life: his training under the ascetic scholar Abu Ali al-Faramadhi. Al-Faramadhi's personal guidance in worship, dhikr, and spiritual struggle prepared Ghazali for his famous seclusion. The lesson explores the divine favor of this encounter and the practices that shaped Ghazali's inner transformation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the narrative flow. Notice the quote from Ibn Ata and the description of al-Faramadhi's role.
The passage is dense; take your time to absorb the key phrases.
2
Identify the key elements
Identify the following: the Ibn Ata quote, the encounter with al-Faramadhi, the specific practices he instructed (worship, supererogatory prayers, dhikr), and the outcome of Ghazali's efforts.
The phrase 'his soul's appetite was not satisfied' indicates his relentless pursuit.
3
Understand the influence on seclusion
Note how the text explicitly states that al-Faramadhi's guidance was among the most important reasons for Ghazali's seclusion, alongside his reading of Sufi books. Reflect on the combination of personal mentorship and personal study.
Seclusion ('uzla) is a key concept in Sufi practice.
4
Reflect on the role of the spiritual guide
Consider the significance of having a spiritual mentor (shaykh tarbiya) in Islamic tradition. How does this passage illustrate the importance of a guide in the spiritual journey?
This connects to the broader theme of 'suhba' (companionship) in Sufism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وملخص هذه المرحلة الجهادية في حياة الإمام في قول ابن عطاء رحمه الله تعالى : ( متى أوحشك من خلقه . . فاعلم أنه يريد أن يفتح لك باب الأنس به . . . ) من الفارمذي إلى النتاج : ومن جملة العنايات الإلهية بالإمام الغزالي أن يصحب في فترة نضوجه الفكري شيخاً زاهداً مربياً هو أبو علي الفارمذي الطوسي ، فاستفتح منه الطريقة ، وامتثل ما كان يشير عليه من القيام بوظائف العبادات والإمعان في النوافل واستدامة الذكر ، والجد والاجتهاد إلى أن جاز تلك العقبات ولم تشبع نهمة نفسه التي كانت تبعد النظر في المأمول . ولقد مات الفارمذي قبل الجويني بعام ، سنة ( ٤٧٧ هـ ) ولعله كان من أهم الأسباب التي حَدَتْ بالإمام لعزلته المشهورة ، إضافة إلى كثرة مطالعته واعتماده على كتب الصوفية كما صرح بذلك في ( المنقذ )
Summary of this jihad-like stage in the life of the Imam is in the saying of Ibn Ata, may God have mercy on him: 'When He makes you feel estranged from His creation... know that He wants to open for you the door of intimacy with Him...' From al-Faramadhi to the outcome: Among the divine favors upon Imam al-Ghazali was that in the period of his intellectual maturity he accompanied an ascetic, nurturing shaykh, Abu Ali al-Faramadhi al-Tusi. He opened the path (tariqa) with him, and followed his instructions regarding performing acts of worship, persisting in supererogatory prayers, maintaining constant dhikr, and striving diligently until he passed those obstacles and his soul's appetite—which had been looking far ahead to what was hoped for—was not satisfied. Al-Faramadhi died a year before al-Juwayni, in 477 AH, and perhaps he was among the most important reasons that led the Imam to his famous seclusion, in addition to his extensive reading and reliance on Sufi books, as he stated in al-Munqidh.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage first summarizes the previous stage of struggle with a quote from Ibn Ata: spiritual estrangement from creation is a sign that God intends to open the door of intimacy with Him. Then it focuses on Ghazali's teacher, Abu Ali al-Faramadhi, an ascetic and spiritual guide. Ghazali met him during his intellectual maturity and learned the Sufi path from him. He followed al-Faramadhi's instructions to engage in obligatory and supererogatory acts of worship, continuous remembrance of God (dhikr), and serious effort until he overcame various spiritual obstacles. The phrase 'his soul's appetite was not satisfied' indicates his relentless pursuit of spiritual knowledge. Al-Faramadhi died in 477 AH, one year before Ghazali's teacher al-Juwayni. The passage suggests that al-Faramadhi's influence was a major cause of Ghazali's later seclusion, along with his study of Sufi books. This highlights the importance of a spiritual mentor in Ghazali's development.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali's seclusion was solely due to his own reading of Sufi books.
Correction: The text explicitly states that al-Faramadhi's personal guidance was among the most important reasons for his seclusion, in addition to his reading.

D) Traveling to seek knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Shaykh al-Faramadhi's personal instruction in worship, dhikr, and spiritual striving was a divine favor that prepared Ghazali for his transformative seclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ghazali's soul remained unsatisfied even after passing obstacles. Examine your own spiritual state: have you grown complacent, or do you feel a holy restlessness driving you to greater devotion?
?
The passage says Ghazali 'emulated what [al-Faramadhi] instructed'. Think of the last piece of sincere spiritual advice you received. Did you act on it, or simply appreciate it? What is one practical instruction from a teacher that you are ignoring today that you need to start implementing?

Yusuf al-Nassaj: The Teacher of Constant Remembrance
Yusuf al-Nassaj's role in Ghazali's spiritual training.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of Yusuf al-Nassaj in Ghazali's spiritual training and how his practice of turning every stop into an act of worship exemplifies constant remembrance of Allah.

Core Idea
Every place is a place for remembering God – Yusuf al-Nassaj's lesson to Ghazali.

This passage introduces another spiritual mentor of Imam Ghazali: Yusuf al-Nassaj. While Ghazali’s famous teacher al-Faramadhi is often highlighted, al-Nassaj also played a crucial role in his development. The anecdote about their travels shows how al-Nassaj turned even mundane moments into opportunities for worship, leaving a lasting impression on Ghazali. Read on to see how a simple practice of two rak'as can transform one's spiritual state.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the chain of narration from al-Zabidi to Ghazali.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the key figures
Who are the people mentioned? Yusuf al-Nassaj, al-Zabidi, Qutb al-Din Muhammad ibn al-Ardabili, and Ghazali. Understand their roles: al-Nassaj is the teacher, the others are transmitters.
The chain shows the careful preservation of this anecdote.
3
Reflect on the anecdote's significance
Ask yourself: Why is this story important? What does Yusuf al-Nassaj's action teach about spirituality? How does it connect to Ghazali's own later teachings?
This practice highlights the importance of continuous dhikr (remembrance of God).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً صحبة شيخه يوسف النساج، وهو ممن اعتنى بتربيته كذلك ، وقد كان له أثر كبير في ذلك ؛ قال الزبيدي رحمه الله : ( ومما وجد بخط الزاهد قطب الدین محمد بن الأردبيلي قال : قال حجة الإسلام : كنت أسافر مع يوسف النساج ، فإذا نزلنا منزلاً صلى ركعتين وقال : نزلنا منزلاً فيه ذكر لله تعالى )

Whenever we stopped at a place, he prayed two rak'as and said: We have stopped at a place in which there is remembrance of God Almighty.'
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This brief passage highlights another spiritual teacher of Ghazali: Yusuf al-Nassaj. The text says he also cared for Ghazali's spiritual upbringing and had a great impact. A specific anecdote is quoted: when they traveled together, whenever they stopped at a place, Yusuf would pray two rak'as and declare that the place is for the remembrance of God. This illustrates the constant mindfulness of God (dhikr) that Yusuf practiced and taught Ghazali. The chain of transmission is given: from al-Zabidi, from the handwriting of Qutb al-Din Muhammad ibn al-Ardabili, who quotes Ghazali directly. This emphasizes the importance of practical spirituality in Ghazali's formation.

Key Takeaway
Yusuf al-Nassaj taught Ghazali that every place is an opportunity to remember God, turning mundane moments into worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Yusuf al-Nassaj did not wait for a mosque or a special moment; he turned every pause into worship. How often do you let ordinary moments slip by without acknowledging the One who brought you there? Choose one stop today and commit to praying two rak'as or saying 'Bismillah' with full presence.
?
If you truly believed that every place is an opportunity to remember God, what habits would you change? Will you let this lesson unsettle you enough to alter your next stop?

Imam al-Ghazali's Anecdote on Leaving Worldly Desires
Anecdote of Imam al-Ghazali meeting someone and reciting poetry about turning to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ghazali's personal experience illustrates the spiritual priority of turning from worldly attachments to intimacy with God.

Core Idea
Leaving worldly attachments is the path to divine intimacy.

This lesson presents an anecdote from the life of Imam al-Ghazali, a towering scholar of Islam, who chose spiritual retreat over teaching in Baghdad. Through poetry and a wise saying, he conveys the essence of the spiritual struggle (jihad) to turn from creation to the Creator. The passage invites us to reflect on the deeper purpose of knowledge and action.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the story of the student's question and the Imam's poetic response. Note the contrast between worldly teaching and spiritual retreat.
Pay attention to the setting: the Imam is in retreat, and the student thinks teaching is better.
2
Understand the poetry
The poem uses metaphors (full moon of happiness, sun of arrival, Layla and Su'da as worldly loves) to express the shift from worldly ambitions to spiritual goals.
The 'first abode' refers to God. The broken spindle symbolizes leaving worldly efforts.
3
Connect to the saying of Ibn 'Ata'
The author summarizes this as the 'jihad stage' and quotes Ibn 'Ata': when God estranges you from people, He intends to open the door of intimacy with Him.
This saying ties the Imam's personal experience to a universal spiritual principle.
4
Reflect on application
Consider how this principle applies to balancing worldly duties and spiritual goals. Is there a need for periodic retreat?
The Imam's action is not a rejection of teaching but a prioritization of spiritual depth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْعِلْمِ ، قَالَ : فَدَنَوْتُ مِنْهُ وَسَلَّمْتُ عَلَيْهِ وَقُلْتُ لَهُ : يَا إِمَامُ ؛ أَلَيْسَ تَدْرِيسُ الْعِلْمِ بِبَغْدَادَ خَيْراً مِنْ هَذَا ؟! قَالَ : فَنَظَرَ إِلَيَّ شَزْراً وَقَالَ : لَمَّا طَلَعَ بَدْرُ السَّعَادَةِ فِي فَلَكِ الْإِرَادَةِ – أَوْ قَالَ : فِي سَمَاءِ الْإِرَادَةِ – وَجَنَحَتْ شَمْسُ الْوُصُولِ فِي مَغَارِبِ الْأُصُولِ : تَرَكْتُ هَوَى لَيْلَى وَسُعْدَى بِمَعْزِلِ وَعُدْتُ إِلَى مَصْحُوبِ أَوَّلِ مَنْزِلِ وَنَادَتْ بِي الْأَشْوَاقُ مَهْلاً فَهَذِهِ مَنَازِلُ مَنْ تَهْوَى رُوَيْدَكَ فَانْزِلِ غَزَلْتُ لَهُمْ غَوْلاً رَقِيقاً فَلَمْ أَجِدْ لِغَزْلِي نَسَّاجاً فَكَشَرْتُ مُغْزَلِي (١) وَمُلَخَّصُ هَذِهِ الْمَرْحَلَةِ الْجِهَادِيَّةِ فِي حَيَاةِ الْإِمَامِ فِي قَوْلِ ابْنِ عَطَاءٍ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى : ( مَتَى أَوْحَشَكَ مِنْ خَلْقِهِ . . فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ يُرِيدُ أَنْ يَفْتَحَ لَكَ بَابَ الْأُنْسِ بِهِ . . . )
Of knowledge, he said: I drew near to him, greeted him, and said to him: O Imam, is not teaching knowledge in Baghdad better than this? He said: Then he looked at me angrily and said: When the full moon of happiness rose in the orbit of will—or he said: in the sky of will—and the sun of arrival set in the wests of origins: I left the passion of Layla and Su'da aside and returned to the companion of the first abode. Desires called to me, gently, for these are the abodes of the one you love, slowly then descend. I spun for them a delicate yarn, but I found no weaver for my yarn, so I broke my spindle. (1) And the summary of this jihad stage in the Imam's life is in the saying of Ibn 'Ata', may Allah have mercy on him: (When He makes you feel estranged from His creation... then know that He wants to open for you the door of intimacy with Him...)
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is an anecdote from the life of Imam al-Ghazali. It begins with a student approaching the Imam and questioning why he is engaged in spiritual retreat rather than teaching in Baghdad, which would seem more beneficial. The Imam responds with a sharp look and then recites a poem. The poem uses metaphors: the full moon of happiness in the orbit of will, the sun of arrival setting in the wests of origins, symbolizing a shift from worldly ambitions to spiritual goals. He leaves the love of Layla and Su'da (representing worldly attachments) and returns to the original companion (God). Desires call him gently, but he is told to descend slowly. He spins a delicate yarn (spiritual teachings) but finds no weaver (no receptive audience), so he breaks his spindle (ceases worldly efforts). The author then summarizes that this stage of struggle (jihad) in the Imam's life is captured by the saying of Ibn 'Ata': when God estranges you from people, He intends to open for you the door of intimacy with Him. This highlights the theme of turning away from creation to focus on the Creator.

Key Terms
الإمام الغزالي (Imam al-Ghazali)
المرحلة الجهادية (jihad stage)
ابن عطاء (Ibn 'Ata')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Imam was criticizing teaching or that spiritual retreat is always superior to worldly duties.
Correction: The Imam's response is not a rejection of teaching, but an affirmation that spiritual retreat can be necessary for inner transformation. He was not criticizing teaching per se, but emphasizing a higher spiritual calling at that moment.

Checkpoint

A) The difficulty of teaching
B) The turning away from worldly attachments to spiritual reality
C) The importance of Baghdad
D) The love of Layla and Su'da

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Ghazali's anecdote teaches that spiritual retreat from worldly desires is a necessary step toward intimacy with God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
{'question': "When you feel estranged from others—unseen, unappreciated, or isolated—do you recognize it as Allah's invitation to intimacy with Him, or do you immediately try to re-enter the circle of creation? What would it cost you to sit alone with Him tonight?"}

Imam al-Ghazali's Teacher: Abu 'Ali al-Farmadhi
Describes al-Ghazali's spiritual teacher Abu 'Ali al-Farmadhi and his influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of spiritual mentorship in al-Ghazali's intellectual and spiritual development, particularly through his teacher Abu 'Ali al-Farmadhi.

Core Idea
A spiritual teacher shaped al-Ghazali's path to knowledge and seclusion.

This lesson introduces a key figure in al-Ghazali's life — his spiritual mentor, Abu 'Ali al-Farmadhi. The passage presents him as a divine favor, guiding al-Ghazali in devotional practices and preparing him for his famous crisis and seclusion. Understanding this relationship helps contextualize al-Ghazali's later works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation. Pay attention to the sequence: divine favor, teacher's role, practices, death, and seclusion.
2
Identify the teacher's role
Notice how al-Farmadhi taught al-Ghazali the 'tariqa' (path) and directed him in obligatory acts, supererogatory worship, continuous dhikr, and effort.
This shows the practical spiritual training al-Ghazali underwent.
3
Connect to al-Ghazali's seclusion
The passage states that al-Farmadhi's death, combined with al-Ghazali's reading of Sufi books, was a major reason for his later famous seclusion.
This corrects the common misconception that the seclusion was only due to philosophical doubt.
4
Reflect on significance
Consider why the author frames this as a 'divine favor' and what it tells us about the importance of spiritual mentorship in Islamic scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِن جُمْلَةِ الْعِنَايَاتِ الْإِلَهِيَّةِ بِالْإِمَامِ الْغَزَالِيِّ أَن يَصْحَبَ فِي فَتْرَةِ نُضُوجِهِ الْفِكْرِيِّ شَيْخاً زَاهِداً مُرَبِّياً هُوَ أَبُو عَلِيٍّ الْفَارْمَذِيُّ الطُّوسِيُّ (٣) ، فَاسْتَفْتَحَ مِنْهُ الطَّرِيقَةَ ، وَامْتَثَلَ مَا كَانَ يُشِيرُ عَلَيْهِ مِنَ الْقِيَامِ بِوَظَائِفِ الْعِبَادَاتِ وَالْإِمْعَانِ فِي النَّوَافِلِ وَاسْتِدَامَةِ الذِّكْرِ ، وَالْجَدِّ وَالِاجْتِهَادِ إِلَى أَن جَازَ تِلْكَ الْعَقَبَاتِ وَلَمْ تَشْبَعْ نَهْمَةُ نَفْسِهِ الَّتِي كَانَتْ تَبْعُدُ النَّظَرَ فِي الْمَأْمُولِ (٣) وَلَقَدْ مَاتَ الْفَارْمَذِيُّ قَبْلَ الْجُوَيْنِيِّ بِعَامٍ ، سَنَةَ ( ٤٧٧ هـ ) وَلَعَلَّهُ كَانَ مِنْ أَهَمِّ الْأَسْبَابِ الَّتِي حَدَتْ بِالْإِمَامِ لِعُزْلَتِهِ الْمَشْهُورَةِ ، إِضَافَةً إِلَى كَثْرَةِ مُطَالَعَتِهِ وَاعْتِمَادِهِ عَلَى كُتُبِ الصُّوفِيَّةِ كَمَا صَرَّحَ بِذَلِكَ فِي ( الْمُنْقِذِ (٤)
And among the divine favors upon Imam al-Ghazali was that he accompanied, in the period of his intellectual maturity, a ascetic, nurturing shaykh, namely Abu 'Ali al-Farmadhi al-Tusi (3). He opened the path (tariqa) through him, and he complied with what he advised him regarding performing the duties of worship, being thorough in supererogatory acts, continuous dhikr, and seriousness and effort until he passed those obstacles, and the appetite of his soul which was far-sighted in what was hoped for was not satiated (3). And al-Farmadhi died one year before al-Juwayni, in the year 477 AH. It may have been among the most important reasons that drove the Imam to his famous seclusion, in addition to his extensive reading and reliance on Sufi books, as he stated in al-Munqidh (4).
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a biographical note about a key spiritual teacher of Imam al-Ghazali: Abu 'Ali al-Farmadhi al-Tusi. The author presents this as a divine favor. The Imam studied under him during his intellectual maturity. Al-Farmadhi taught him the path (tariqa) and instructed him in devotional practices: fulfilling obligatory acts, increasing supererogatory acts, continuous remembrance of God (dhikr), and serious effort. The Imam passed obstacles but his soul's appetite remained unsatisfied, indicating his high ambition. Then the author notes that al-Farmadhi died a year before al-Juwayni (who was al-Ghazali's teacher in kalam). This event, along with extensive reading of Sufi books, is cited as a major reason for al-Ghazali's famous seclusion, as he himself stated in his work al-Munqidh min al-Dalal. The passage emphasizes the role of spiritual mentorship in al-Ghazali's development.

Key Terms
أبو علي الفارمذي الطوسي (Abu 'Ali al-Farmadhi al-Tusi)
الطريقة (tariqa/path)
المنقذ (al-Munqidh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ghazali's seclusion was solely due to philosophical doubt.
Correction: The passage shows that his teacher's death and study of Sufi books were among the most important reasons for his seclusion.

Checkpoint
What was a key reason for Imam al-Ghazali's seclusion according to this passage?
A) A disagreement with al-Juwayni
B) The death of his teacher al-Farmadhi and his study of Sufi books
C) A dream vision
D) The destruction of Baghdad

Key Takeaway
The guidance of a spiritual teacher and the study of Sufi texts were pivotal in al-Ghazali's spiritual journey and eventual seclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Al-Ghazali's soul was 'not satiated' even after passing obstacles. Is your spiritual appetite insatiable, or do you settle for mediocrity? What one supererogatory act can you commit to today?
?
Al-Farmadhi's death was a catalyst for al-Ghazali's seclusion. If a key mentor in your life disappeared, would you be driven deeper into your faith or would you drift? What does your answer reveal about your current dependence?

Life of Imam al-Ghazali
beginner
4 min
Al-Ghazali's Dream and His Teacher Yusuf al-Nassaj
Narrates al-Ghazali's dream of God and his teacher's guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ghazali's spiritual transformation was catalyzed by his teacher Yusuf al-Nassaj and a divine dream.

Core Idea
A single dream transformed al-Ghazali from a denier of saints to a seeker of divine love.

This passage recounts a pivotal moment in Imam al-Ghazali's life. Initially skeptical of the spiritual states of saints, he was transformed through companionship with his teacher Yusuf al-Nassaj and a remarkable dream. The dream, in which God addressed him directly, validated the Sufi path and set him on a new course.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the shift in al-Ghazali's attitude from denial to acceptance.
Al-Ghazali admits he was initially a denier.
2
Identify key elements
Identify the teacher (Yusuf al-Nassaj), the struggle (mujahada), the dream, the test (Is it Satan?), the command (leave desks), and the teacher's response.
3
Reflect on the dream interpretation
Consider why al-Nassaj smiled and said the dream was only the beginning. What does 'These are our tablets in the beginning, we erased them with our feet' mean?
It suggests that direct spiritual experiences are just the start.
4
Consider the role of companionship
Think about how al-Ghazali's teacher played a crucial role. How did al-Nassaj 'polish' him? Why is spiritual companionship important?
Companionship with a guide is central in Sufism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَيْضاً صُحْبَةُ شَيْخِهِ يُوسُفَ النَّسَّاجِ، وَهُوَ مِمَّنِ اعْتَنَى بِتَرْبِيَتِهِ كَذَلِكَ ، وَقَدْ كَانَ لَهُ أَثَرٌ كَبِيرٌ فِي ذَلِكَ ؛ قَالَ الزُّبَيْدِيُّ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ : ( وَمِمَّا وُجِدَ بِخَطِّ الزَّاهِدِ قُطْبِ الدِّينِ مُحَمَّدِ بْنِ الْأَرْدَبِيلِيِّ قَالَ : قَالَ حُجَّةُ الْإِسْلَامِ : كُنْتُ بِدَايَةِ أَمْرِي مُنْكِراً لِأَحْوَالِ الصَّالِحِينَ وَمَقَامَاتِ الْعَارِفِينَ حَتَّى صَحِبْتُ شَيْخِي يُوسُفَ النَّسَّاجَ بِطُوسَ ، فَلَمْ يَزَلْ يَصْقَلُنِي بِالْمُجَاهِدَةِ حَتَّى حَظِيتُ بِالْوَارِدَاتِ ، فَرَأَيْتُ اللَّهَ فِي الْمَنَامِ فَقَالَ لِي : يَا أَبَا حَامِدٍ ؛ قُلْتُ : أَوَ الشَّيْطَانُ يُكَلِّمُنِي ؟ قَالَ : لَا ، بَلْ أَنَا اللَّهُ الْمُحِيطُ بِجِهَاتِكَ السِّتِّ ، ثُمَّ قَالَ : يَا أَبَا حَامِدٍ ؛ ذَرْ مَسَاطِرَكَ وَاصْحَبْ أَقْوَاماً جَعَلْتُهُمْ فِي أَرْضِي مَحَلَّ نَظَرِي ، وَهُمُ الَّذِينَ بَاعُوا الدَّارَيْنِ بِحُبِّي ، فَقُلْتُ : بِعِزَّتِكَ إِلَّا أَذَقْتَنِي بَرْدَ حُسْنِ الظَّنِّ بِهِمْ ، فَقَالَ : قَدْ فَعَلْتُ ، وَالْقَاطِعُ بَيْنَكَ وَبَيْنَهُمْ تَشَاغُلُكَ بِحُبِّ الدُّنْيَا ، فَاخْرُجْ مِنْهَا مُخْتَاراً قَبْلَ أَنْ تَخْرُجَ صَاغِراً ، فَقَدْ أَفَضْتُ عَلَيْكَ أَنْوَاراً مِنْ جِوَارِ قُدْسِي فَفُزْ وَنَلْ ، فَاسْتَيْقَظْتُ فَرِحاً مَسْرُوراً : ، وَجِئْتُ إِلَى شَيْخِي يُوسُفَ النَّسَّاجِ ، فَقَصَصْتُ عَلَيْهِ الْمَنَامَ ، فَتَبَسَّمَ فَقَالَ : يَا أَبَا حَامِدٍ ؛ هَذِهِ أَلْوَاحُنَا فِي الْبِدَايَةِ، مَحَوْنَاهَا بِأَرْجُلِنَا ، بَلْ إِنْ صَحِبْتَنِي .. سَيُكْحِلْ بَصَرَ بَصِيرَتِكَ بِإِثْمِدِ التَّأْيِيدِ حَتَّى تَرَى الْعَرْشَ ... ((١)
And also his companionship with his shaykh Yusuf al-Nassaj, who also took care of his upbringing, and he had a great impact on that. Al-Zabidi, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (Among what was found in the handwriting of the ascetic Qutb al-Din Muhammad ibn al-Ardabili, he said: Hujjat al-Islam [al-Ghazali] said: At the beginning of my affair, I was a denier of the states of the righteous and the stations of the knowers until I accompanied my shaykh Yusuf al-Nassaj in Tus. He continued to polish me with spiritual struggle until I attained divine inflows. Then I saw Allah in a dream, and He said to me: O Abu Hamid! I said: Or is Satan speaking to me? He said: No, I am Allah, the One who encompasses your six directions. Then He said: O Abu Hamid! Leave your desks and accompany people whom I have made in My earth the place of My gaze, and they are those who sold both worlds for My love. I said: By Your might, let me taste the coolness of good opinion of them. He said: I have done so, and what cuts between you and them is your occupation with love of the world, so depart from it willingly before you depart humiliated, for I have poured upon you lights from the neighborhood of My sanctity, so succeed and attain. Then I woke up joyful and happy. And I came to my shaykh Yusuf al-Nassaj and related the dream to him. He smiled and said: O Abu Hamid! These are our tablets in the beginning, we erased them with our feet. Rather, if you accompany me, He will line the vision of your insight with the collyrium of support until you see the Throne... (1)).
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a first-person narrative from Imam al-Ghazali, quoted through al-Zabidi, about his spiritual transformation under Shaykh Yusuf al-Nassaj. Initially, al-Ghazali was a denier of the spiritual states of the righteous and the stations of the knowers. Through companionship with al-Nassaj and spiritual struggle (mujahada), he attained divine inflows (waridat). He then had a dream in which Allah spoke to him. Al-Ghazali tested the vision by asking if it was Satan, and the voice affirmed it was God, who encompasses all directions. God commanded him to leave his academic pursuits (desks) and accompany the saints who had sold both worlds for His love. Al-Ghazali asked for the taste of good opinion of them, which God granted. Then he was told that his attachment to worldly love was a barrier, and he must leave the world willingly. Upon waking, he went to his teacher al-Nassaj, who smiled and interpreted the dream as an initial stage, indicating that this was the beginning of deeper spiritual insight. al-Nassaj said that he would be given spiritual sight to see the Throne. This passage illustrates al-Ghazali's validation of Sufi spirituality through a direct divine encounter.

Key Terms
يوسف النساج (Yusuf al-Nassaj)
الوارادات (divine inflows)
المنام (dream)
المجاهدة (spiritual struggle)
حسن الظن (good opinion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Ghazali was always a Sufi from the start, without any initial skepticism.
Correction: Al-Ghazali explicitly states he was initially a denier of the states of the righteous, showing his transformation was gradual.

Checkpoint
What did the voice in the dream tell al-Ghazali to leave?
A) His family
B) His desks (academic pursuits)
C) His teacher
D) His wealth

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's dream experience and his teacher's guidance confirmed the reality of spiritual states and the necessity of companionship for inner transformation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ghazali was initially a denier of the states of saints until he had a divine encounter. Have you ever dismissed the spiritual experiences of others? What would it take for you to open your heart to the possibility that Allah may grant such states to His sincere servants? Will you ask Him tonight to grant you humility and remove your doubts?
?
In the dream, Allah told al-Ghazali to leave his desks—his academic pursuits—and seek the company of those who sold both worlds for His love. What are your 'desks'—the activities, habits, or attachments that keep you from pursuing Allah with your whole heart? Are you willing to leave them willingly before you are forced to leave them humiliated?
?
Al-Ghazali asked for the taste of good opinion of the saints, and Allah granted it. Do you truly believe that those who are devoted to Allah have a higher station? Or does your heart secretly look down on them, thinking you are better because of your knowledge or status? Ask Allah to give you the coolness of good opinion of His friends—and then act on it by seeking their company.

Life of Imam al-Ghazali
beginner
5 min
The Final Phase of Imam al-Ghazali's Life
Describes al-Ghazali's return to Tus, resumption of teaching, and establishment of a school and khanqah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circumstances of al-Ghazali's return from seclusion, his renewed teaching with purified intentions, and his balanced daily routine in Tus.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's final years exemplify the integration of spiritual purification, teaching, and worship.

This passage takes us to the closing chapter of Imam al-Ghazali's life. After eleven years of seclusion, he returns to public teaching, but transformed. This is not a simple resumption; it is a careful, divinely-guided return to service. The passage shows how he balanced scholarship, Sufi practice, and community.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the narrative flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the Quranic verse at the end.
2
Identify key events
Identify the main events: return to Tus, Fakhral-Mulk's visit, decision to teach, establishment of school and khanqah.
3
Note the emphasis on intention
Pay attention to the phrase describing his teaching as 'with pure and wholesome breaths, unsullied by hypocrisy or pretension to Sufism.'
This highlights his transformed state.
4
Reflect on the Quranic verse
Consider why the passage ends with the verse from Surah An-Nasr. What does it signify about al-Ghazali's state?
It connects his life to the command to glorify and seek forgiveness.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Think about how this final phase integrates different dimensions of Islamic learning: teaching, worship, and spiritual training.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللوحة الأخيرة من حياته : عَادَ الْإِمَامُ أَدْرَاجَهُ إِلَى مَسْقَطِ رَأْسِهِ طُوسَ وَهُوَ يَحْمِلُ بَيْنَ جَنْبَيْهِ قَلْباً سَلِيماً كَانَ قَدْ جَدَّ فِي طَلَبِهِ ، وَنَفْساً رَضِيَّةً لَطَالَمَا جَاهَدَ فِي تَزْكِيَتِهَا وَتَهْذِيبِهَا ، وَكَانَ فَخْرُ الْمَلِكِ قَدْ سَمِعَ بِمَكَانَةِ الْغَزَالِيِّ وَفَضْلِهِ ، فَقَصَدَهُ زَائِراً مُتَبَرِّكاً ، وَمَا زَالَ بِالْإِمَامِ خَاضِعاً رَاجِياً أَنْ يَذْهَبَ بِهِ إِلَى نِيسَابُورَ مُعَلِّماً وَمُدَرِّساً فِي نِظَامِيَّتِهَا ، وَرَأَى أَنَّهُ إِنْ لَمْ يَنزِلْ عِنْدَ رَغْبَتِهِ . . لَلَزِمَتِ الْوَحْشَةُ ، فَعَادَ مِنَ الْخَلْوَةِ إِلَى الْجَلْوَةِ ، بَعْدَ مُشَاوَرَةِ أَرْبَابِ الْقُلُوبِ ، وَتَوَارُدِ إِشَارَاتٍ وَبِشَارَاتٍ تُؤْذِنُ بِالْخُرُوجِ إِلَى هَذَا الْمَقْصُودِ ، وَكَانَتْ مُدَّةُ الْخَلْوَةِ إِحْدَى عَشْرَةَ سَنَةً ، فَعَادَ إِلَى نَشْرِ الْعِلْمِ بَعْدَهَا وَلَكِنْ بِأَنْفَاسٍ طَاهِرَةٍ زَكِيَّةٍ ، لَا يَشُوبُهَا رِيَاءٌ وَلَا تَخْيِيلٌ لِلصُّوفِيَّةِ . ثُمَّ عَادَ إِلَى بَيْتِهِ مُتَّخِذاً فِي جِوَارِهِ مَدْرَسَةً لِطَلَبَةِ الْعِلْمِ وَخَانِقَاه ـ مَرْكَزُ تَرْبِيَةٍ وَتَهْذِيبٍ - وَوَزَّعَ أَوْقَاتِهِ بَيْنَ تِلَاوَةٍ وَذِكْرٍ ، وَمُجَالَسَةٍ لِأَهْلِ الْقُلُوبِ ، وَقُعُودٍ لِلتَّدْرِيسِ ، وَانْكَبَّ لِدِرَاسَةٍ وَرِوَايَةِ الْحَدِيثِ (٢) ، وَهُوَ يَتَنَسَّمُ قَوْلَهُ سُبْحَانَهُ : ( فَسَبِّحْ بِحَمْدِ رَبِّكَ وَاسْتَغْفِرْهُ إِنَّهُ كَانَ تَوَّاباً )
The final scene of his life: The Imam returned back to his birthplace Tus, carrying in his sides a sound heart that he had been earnest in seeking, and a content soul that he had long struggled to purify and refine. Fakhral-Mulk had heard of al-Ghazali's status and merit, so he visited him seeking blessing. He kept humbly asking the Imam to go with him to Nishapur as a teacher in its Nizamiyya. The Imam saw that if he did not comply with his wish, estrangement would follow, so he returned from seclusion to public appearance, after consulting the masters of hearts, and receiving indications and glad tidings permitting the exit for this purpose. The period of seclusion was eleven years. He returned to spreading knowledge after that, but with pure and wholesome breaths, unsullied by hypocrisy or pretension to Sufism. Then he returned to his home, taking beside it a school for students and a khanqah—a center of training and refinement. He distributed his time between recitation and dhikr, sitting with the people of hearts, teaching, and devoted himself to studying and narrating hadith (2). And he was breathing the saying of Him, exalted: (So glorify the praise of your Lord and seek His forgiveness, indeed He is ever accepting of repentance.)
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is the final biographical section, titled 'The Final Scene of His Life.' It describes al-Ghazali's return to his birthplace Tus after his long seclusion. He is described as having a sound heart and a purified soul. Then Fakhral-Mulk, a ruler, visited him and requested him to teach in the Nizamiyya of Nishapur. Al-Ghazali initially hesitated but after consulting spiritual guides and receiving signs, he agreed to return to public teaching. The seclusion had lasted eleven years. He returned to spreading knowledge but with pure intentions, free from hypocrisy or pretension to being a Sufi (i.e., he did not flaunt his spirituality). Back in Tus, he established a school for students and a khanqah (Sufi lodge) for spiritual training. He divided his time among Quran recitation, dhikr, sitting with spiritual people, teaching, and studying hadith. The passage ends with the Quranic verse (Surah An-Nasr: 3) which commands glorification of God and seeking forgiveness, indicating his state of repentance and devotion. This summarizes al-Ghazali's final years: a balanced life of worship, teaching, and spiritual guidance.

Key Terms
الخلوة (seclusion)
الجلوة (public appearance)
فخر الملك (Fakhral-Mulk)
مدرسة (school)
خانقاه (khanqah)
الصوفية (Sufism)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that al-Ghazali completely abandoned teaching after his spiritual crisis, but this passage shows he returned to teaching after seclusion, but with a transformed heart.
Correction: Al-Ghazali did not renounce teaching; rather, he resumed it after eleven years of seclusion, with purified intentions, and established a school and khanqah.

Checkpoint
How long did Imam al-Ghazali's seclusion last before he returned to teaching?
A) 5 years
B) 10 years
C) 11 years
D) 15 years

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's final years demonstrate that spiritual retreat can lead to a more sincere and balanced engagement with teaching and worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ghazali spent eleven years in seclusion to purify his heart. What current attachment or distraction in your life would you need to set aside for a significant period to attain sincerity in your worship and learning? What is one concrete step you can take this week to begin that detachment?
?
He returned to teaching with 'pure and wholesome breaths, unsullied by hypocrisy or pretension.' In which area of your religious practice do you most fear hypocrisy (riya)? What specific action will you take today to ensure your intention is solely for Allah, even if no one notices?
?
Al-Ghazali balanced recitation, dhikr, teaching, and hadith study. Which of these four elements is most neglected in your daily life? Commit to adding one of them to your schedule this week, and write down when and how you will do it.

Life of Imam al-Ghazali
beginner
