Ghazali's Dream and Spiritual Transformation
Ghazali narrates his initial denial, spiritual training, and transformative dream.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the transformative power of spiritual companionship (suhba) and divine unveiling (kashf) as exemplified in Imam al-Ghazali's own journey.

Core Idea
God’s command in a dream: abandon scholarship and accompany the righteous.

This passage is a first-person account by Imam al-Ghazali of a pivotal spiritual experience that transformed his life. It illustrates the role of spiritual struggle under a master and the reality of divine communication through dreams. As you read, notice how Ghazali's initial skepticism gave way to certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the full translation. Let the narrative flow before analyzing details.
The Arabic is rich in spiritual terminology; the translation will clarify.
2
Identify the key characters and their roles
Notice Ghazali's transformation from a denier to a seeker, his shaykh Yusuf al-Nassaj as a spiritual polisher, and the divine voice in the dream.
3
Trace the progression of the story
Follow the sequence: Ghazali's initial denial, his spiritual training (mujahada), the dream, his doubt, God's command, his request, waking, and the shaykh's response.
Each stage marks a step in Ghazali's transformation.
4
Reflect on key symbols and commands
Consider what 'abandon your pens' means (leaving scholarly pursuits) and who 'the people who sold both abodes' are (those who renounce worldly and otherworldly rewards for God's love).
These symbols point to a radical reorientation of priorities.
5
Examine the shaykh's interpretation
Shaykh Yusuf says 'These are our tablets in the beginning, we erased them with our feet.' This suggests that the dream's visions are initial spiritual states that must be transcended through further training.
The shaykh's response deepens the lesson: spiritual progress is continuous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بداية أمري منكراً لأحوال الصالحين ومقامات العارفين حتى صحبت شيخي يوسف النساج بطوس ، فلم يزل يصقلني بالمجاهدة حتى حظيت بالواردات ، فرأيت الله في المنام فقال لي : يا أبا حامد ؛ قلت : أَو الشيطانُ يكلمني ؟ قال : لا ، بل أنا الله المحيط بجهاتك الست ، ثم قال : يا أبا حامد ؛ ذر مساطرك واصحب أقواماً جعلتهم في أرضي محل نظري ، وهم الذين باعوا الدارين بحبي ، فقلت : بعزتك إلا أذقتني برد حسن الظن بهم ، فقال : قد فعلت ، والقاطع بينك وبينهم تشاغلك بحب الدنيا ، فاخرج منها مختاراً قبل أن تخرج صاغراً ، فقد أفضت عليك أنواراً من جوار قدسي ففز ونل ، فاستيقظت فرحاً مسروراً : ، وجئت إلى شيخي يوسف النساج ، فقصصت عليه المنام ، فتبسم فقال : يا أبا حامد ؛ هذه ألواحنا في البداية، محوناها بأرجلنا ، بل إن صحبتني .. سيكحل بصر بصيرتك بإثمد التأييد حتى ترى العرش ... ((١)
The beginning of my affair was that I was denier of the states of the righteous and the stations of the knowers until I accompanied my shaykh Yusuf al-Nassaj in Tus. He kept polishing me through spiritual struggle until I attained divine inflows. I saw God in a dream and He said to me: 'O Abu Hamid!' I said: 'Or is Satan speaking to me?' He said: 'No, but I am God who encompasses your six directions.' Then He said: 'O Abu Hamid! Abandon your pens and accompany a people whom I have made in My earth the object of My gaze, and they are those who sold both abodes for My love.' I said: 'By Your might, let me taste the coolness of thinking well of them.' He said: 'I have done so. What separates you from them is your preoccupation with love of this world. So leave it voluntarily before you leave it humiliated. I have poured upon you lights from the vicinity of My holiness, so succeed and attain.' I woke up happy and joyful. I came to my shaykh Yusuf al-Nassaj and related the dream to him. He smiled and said: 'O Abu Hamid! These are our tablets in the beginning, we erased them with our feet. Rather, if you accompany me, the vision of your insight will be kohled with the antimony of divine support until you see the Throne...' (1)
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a first-person autobiographical narration recounting a pivotal spiritual experience. Ghazali describes his initial state as a denier of the spiritual states of the righteous and the stations of gnostics. He then met his shaykh Yusuf al-Nassaj in Tus, who trained him through spiritual struggle (mujahada) until he received divine inflows (waridat). In a dream, he saw God, who addressed him. Ghazali doubted whether it was Satan, but God reassured him. God commanded him to abandon his scholarly tools (pens) and to accompany a special people who had sold both this world and the next for God's love. Ghazali asked to taste the coolness of having a good opinion of them, and God granted it, then warned that his love of this world was the barrier. God urged him to leave the world voluntarily. Ghazali woke up joyful and recounted the dream to his shaykh, who explained that the dream's tablets were like those they had erased in early spiritual training, promising further spiritual vision with his guidance.

Key Terms
المجاهدة
الواردات
حسن الظن
إثمد التأييد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali literally saw God's essence in the dream.
Correction: The dream is a spiritual unveiling (kashf) appropriate to the dream state, not a literal vision of God's essence, which is impossible in this world.

Checkpoint
What did Ghazali initially think when he heard a voice in his dream?
A) It was an angel
B) It was Satan
C) It was his own self
D) It was a friend

Key Takeaway
Spiritual transformation requires both personal struggle (mujahada) and the guidance of a realized shaykh, and can culminate in divine unveiling even in a dream.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed the spiritual experiences of others as fantasy or exaggeration, as Ghazali initially did? What would it take for you to genuinely consider that there are people who have sold both worlds for God's love?
?
Ghazali was told to abandon his pens and accompany the saints. What is your equivalent of 'pens'—what intellectual or material attachments are you holding onto that prevent you from seeking the company of the righteous?
?
God said to Ghazali: 'What separates you from them is your preoccupation with love of this world.' If you heard those words spoken directly to you tonight in a dream, what would change tomorrow?

Ghazali's Return to Teaching After Seclusion
Ghazali's return to teaching and establishment of a school and khanqah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali's return to public teaching was a deliberate act of purification and devotion, not a contradiction of his earlier withdrawal.

Core Idea
Ghazali's return to teaching was guided by spiritual counsel, not worldly ambition.

In this passage, we follow Ghazali after eleven years of seclusion. He returns to Tus, receives a request from Fakhr al-Mulk, and after consulting spiritual masters, he resumes teaching with renewed sincerity. This lesson explores the conditions and intentions behind his comeback.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the narrative of Ghazali's return.
2
Identify key details
Note why Ghazali left seclusion: the request from Fakhr al-Mulk and the spiritual consultation.
The text mentions 'بعد مشاورة أرباب القلوب'
3
Understand the transformation
Contrast his earlier seclusion with his new public role, emphasizing his purified intentions.
The text says 'بأنفاس طاهرة زكية'
4
Reflect on his daily routine
Notice how he structured his time around worship, teaching, and companionship with spiritual people.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللوحة الأخيرة من حياته : عاد الإمام أدراجه إلى مسقط رأسه طوس وهو يحمل بين جنبيه قلباً سليماً كان قد جد في طلبه ، ونفساً رضية لطالما جاهد في تزكيتها وتهذيبها ، وكان فخر الملك قد سمع بمكانة الغزالي وفضله ، فقصده زائراً متبركاً ، وما زال بالإمام خاضعاً راجياً أن يذهب به إلى نیسابور معلماً ومدرساً في نظاميتها ، ورأى أنه إن لم ينزل عند رغبته . . للزمت الوحشة ، فعاد من الخلوة إلى الجلوة ، بعد مشاورة أرباب القلوب ، وتوارد إشارات وبشارات تؤذن بالخروج إلى هذا المقصود ، وكانت مدة الخلوة إحدى عشرة سنة ، فعاد إلى نشر العلم بعدها ولكن بأنفاس طاهرة زكية ، لا يشوبها رياء ولا تخييل للصوفية . ثم عاد إلى بيته متخذاً في جواره مدرسة لطلبة العلم وخانقاه ـ مركز تربية وتهذيب - ووزع أوقاته بين تلاوة وذكر ، ومجالسة لأهل القلوب ، وقعود للتدريس ، وانكب لدراسة ورواية الحديث (۲) ، وهو يتنسم قوله سبحانه : ( فَسَبِّحْ بِحَمْدِ رَبِّكَ وَاسْتَغْفِرَةً إِنَّهُ كَانَ تَوَّابَا ) . قال التاج السبكي : ( قال أبو الفرج بن الجوزي في كتاب ( الثبات عند الممات » : قال أحمد أخو الإمام الغزالي : لما كان يوم الإثنين وقت الصبح .. توضأ أخي أبو حامد وصلّى ، وقال : عليَّ بالكفن ؛ فأخذه وقبله ووضعه على عينيه وقال : سمعاً وطاعة للدخول على الملك ، ثم مدَّ رجليه واستقبل القبلة ، ومات قبل الإسفار قدَّسَ الله روحه ((۱) وكان ذلك يوم الإثنين الرابع عشر من جمادى الآخرة سنة ( ٥٠٥هـ ) ودفن بالطابران في طوس مسقط رأسه ، رحمه الله ورضي عنه وأجزل مثوبته وجزاءه . ومما ينسب له من الشعر في هذا المقام : قُل لإخوان رَأَوْنى مياً فَبَكَوْني وَرَثُوا لِي حَزَنَا لَيْسَ ذاكَ الْمَبْتُ والله أنا أَتَظُنُّونَ بِا ـا فِي الصُّورِ وَهَذا جَسَدِي كانَ بَيْنِي وَقَمِيصِي زَمَنَا أَنَا عُصْفُورٌ وَهَذَا قَفَصِي طِرْتُ مِنْهُ وبَقِي مُرْتَهَنا أَحْمَدُ اللهَ الَّذِي خَلَّصَنِ وَبَنَى لِي فِي الْمَعالي سَكَنا كُنْتُ قَبْلَ اليَومِ مَيْتاً بَيْنَكُـ فَحَينتُ وَخَلَعْ ت وَخَلَعْتُ الْكَفَنا
The last panel of his life: The Imam returned to his birthplace Tus, carrying in his chest a sound heart which he had been earnest in seeking, and a contented soul which he had long struggled to purify and refine. Fakhr al-Mulk had heard of Ghazali's stature and virtue, so he visited him seeking blessings. He kept humbly pleading with the Imam, hoping that he would go with him to Nishapur as a teacher and instructor in its Nizamiyya. The Imam saw that if he did not accede to his wish, estrangement would occur, so he returned from seclusion to public life, after consulting the people of hearts, and after successive indications and glad tidings that suggested going out for this purpose. The period of seclusion was eleven years. He returned to spreading knowledge after that, but with pure and virtuous breaths, untainted by ostentation or pretense for the Sufis. Then he returned to his home, taking adjacent to it a school for students and a khanqah (a center for training and purification), and he divided his time between recitation and remembrance, sitting with the people of hearts, teaching, and dedicating himself to studying and narrating hadith (2), inhaling the words of the Exalted: 'So exalt [Allah] with praise of your Lord and ask forgiveness of Him. Indeed, He is ever accepting of repentance.' Al-Taj al-Subki said: Abu al-Faraj ibn al-Jawzi said in the book 'Al-Thabat 'Inda al-Mamat': Ahmad, the brother of Imam Ghazali, said: On Monday at dawn, my brother Abu Hamid performed ablution and prayed. He said: 'Bring me the shroud.' He took it, kissed it, placed it on his eyes, and said: 'Hearing and obeying for entry to the King.' Then he stretched his legs, faced the qibla, and died before daybreak – may God sanctify his soul. (1) That was on Monday, the 14th of Jumada al-Akhira, year 505 AH. He was buried in al-Tabiran in Tus, his birthplace. May God have mercy on him, be pleased with him, and multiply his reward. Among the poetry attributed to him on this occasion: 'Tell brothers who saw me dead and wept for me, grieving for me – that is not what is meant, by God. Do you think...?'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of the final period of Ghazali's life. It begins with his return to Tus after his seclusion (khalwa). He had a sound heart and contented soul. Fakhr al-Mulk, a statesman, visited him and asked him to teach in Nishapur. After consulting spiritual masters and receiving positive indications, Ghazali left seclusion after eleven years. He resumed teaching with pure intentions, free from hypocrisy. He built a school and khanqah, dividing his time among Qur'an recitation, dhikr, companionship with spiritual people, teaching, and hadith study. The narrative then recounts his death through the report of his brother: on Monday dawn, he performed wudu, prayed, asked for his shroud, kissed it, and placed it on his eyes, saying he was obeying the call to enter the presence of the King (God). He then stretched out, faced the qibla, and died before sunrise. The date is given as 14 Jumada al-Akhira 505 AH, and he was buried in Tus. A poem attributed to him is cited, which metaphorically describes death as a bird leaving its cage, expressing joy at liberation from the body.

Key Terms
الخلوة
الجلوة
الخانقاه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali's return to teaching shows he abandoned his earlier asceticism and love for seclusion.
Correction: His return was based on spiritual consultation and performed with purified intentions, as the text says 'with pure and virtuous breaths, untainted by ostentation'.

Checkpoint
How long was Ghazali's period of seclusion before his return to teaching?
A) Five years
B) Ten years
C) Eleven years
D) Twelve years

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's return to public life was a spiritually guided move, where he balanced teaching and devotion without losing sincerity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you return to a public role or responsibility after a period of reflection, do you ensure your motives are purified from ostentation and worldly ambition? Who are the 'people of hearts' you consult before making major decisions?
?
Ghazali's seclusion lasted eleven years. What 'seclusions'—periods of solitude, reflection, or spiritual focus—have you intentionally taken to purify your heart, and how have they prepared you for the public duties Allah places before you?
?
The verse 'So exalt [Allah] with praise of your Lord and ask forgiveness of Him' (Surah An-Nasr) concludes Ghazali's story. In what specific areas of your life do you need to combine gratitude and repentance as you re-engage with the world?

Ghazali's Death and Joyful Poem
Ghazali's death scene and his poem about liberation from the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the details of Ghazali's death as narrated by his brother and the spiritual meaning of his final poem.

Core Idea
Ghazali faced death with joy, seeing it as liberation of the soul from its bodily cage.

This lesson presents the account of Ghazali's final moments and a poem attributed to him. The description of his death shows his readiness and serenity, while the poem uses the metaphor of a bird escaping its cage to express the soul's freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read the death narrative
Read the Arabic account from Taj al-Subki and notice the sequence of actions: wudu, prayer, requesting shroud, kissing it, facing qibla, and dying.
The phrase 'سمعاً وطاعة للدخول على الملك' shows his submission.
2
Interpret the poem
Read the poem line by line. Identify the central metaphor: a bird and a cage. Note the contrast between the body (cage) and the soul (bird).
The poem explicitly says 'that is not what is meant' - the weeping brothers misunderstand.
3
Connect to Ghazali's spiritual state
Reflect on how Ghazali's death reflects his life's journey: seeking a sound heart and contented soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine your own death, does it fill you with the same eager anticipation as it did Ghazali, or does it sting with regret? What exactly in your life today would make you hesitate to say 'سمعاً وطاعة للدخول على الملك' (Hearing and obeying for entry to the King)?
?
Ghazali kissed his shroud as a beloved gift. What would it take for you to welcome the call of death as a friend rather than an enemy? Which attachments to this world are you clutching that make the thought of leaving it painful?
?
The poem says: 'I was dead among you, then I came to life.' In what ways are you spiritually dead right now—going through the motions of life but unaware of your soul's true purpose? What needs to 'die' in you today—what pride, what distraction—so that you can truly live for Allah?

Testimonies to Imam Ghazali's Stature
A compilation of four scholars' praises of Ghazali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how prominent scholars praised Imam Ghazali and what these titles reveal about his scholarly rank.

Core Idea
Scholars unanimously recognized Ghazali as a towering authority in Islamic sciences.

Imam Ghazali's legacy is attested by the glowing testimonies of his teachers and contemporaries. This lesson presents four such praises, each highlighting a different facet of his genius. As you read, consider how these statements collectively establish Ghazali's unique position in Islamic intellectual history.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall sense. Notice the names of the scholars and their specific praises.
The passage is a list of quotes, each from a different scholar.
2
Identify each scholar and their praise
List the four scholars mentioned and what each said about Ghazali. For example, Imam al-Haramayn called him a 'vast sea'.
This will help you remember the distinct attributes highlighted.
3
Reflect on the titles given
Consider the significance of titles like 'Proof of Islam' and 'Spiritual Master of the Community'. What do they imply about Ghazali's role?
These titles are not mere flattery; they indicate recognized authority.
4

Ask yourself: why does the author compile these statements? What purpose does it serve for the reader of Ghazali's works?
The quotes establish Ghazali's credibility before presenting his arguments.

ثناء أهل العلم على الإمام الغزالي : وهذه جملة من شهادات العلماء بقدر وعلم الإمام وفضله : قال فيه شيخه إمام الحرمين : ( الغزالي بحر مغرق ) . وقال عبد الغافر الفارسي : ( حجة الإسلام والمسلمين ، إمام أئمة الدين ، مَنْ لم تر العيون مثله لساناً وبياناً ونطقاً وخاطراً وذكاء وطبعاً ) . وقال الحافظ ابن عساكر : ( كان إماماً في علم الفقه مذهباً وخلافاً ، وفي أصول الديانات ) . وقال ابن النجار : ( أبو حامد إمام الفقهاء على الإطلاق ، ورباني الأمة باتفاق ،
Praise of scholars for Imam Ghazali: These are a number of the testimonies of scholars concerning the stature, knowledge, and virtue of the Imam. His teacher, Imam al-Haramayn, said of him: 'Al-Ghazali is a vast sea.' Abd al-Ghafir al-Farisi said: 'The proof of Islam and the Muslims, the Imam of the imams of the religion, one whose like the eyes have not seen in speech, expression, articulation, thought, intelligence, and nature.' Commentary
This passage is a collection of encomiums from various scholars affirming Ghazali's outstanding qualities. It begins by stating that these are testimonies to his rank and knowledge. Four scholars are quoted: 1) His teacher Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) called him a 'vast sea' (bahr mughriq), indicating his immense depth of knowledge. 2) Abd al-Ghafir al-Farisi described him as 'the proof of Islam and the Muslims' (hujjat al-Islam wa al-muslimin), and enumerated his unmatched eloquence, intellect, and character. 3) Ibn Asakir praised his expertise in both the substantive law (madhhab) and legal disagreement (khilaf) of fiqh, as well as in the principles of religions (usul al-diniyyat). 4) Ibn al-Najjar declared him the absolute imam of jurists and the spiritual master (rabbani) of the community by consensus. The passage is cut off but clearly continues to list more scholars.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The title 'Proof of Islam' (Hujjat al-Islam) implies Ghazali was considered infallible.
Correction: The title is a recognition of his supreme rank in defending Islamic doctrine, not a claim of infallibility.

Imam al-Ghazali's Final Moments and Death
Narrates the final morning and death of Imam al-Ghazali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ghazali faced death with calm preparation and spiritual readiness, as narrated by his brother.

Core Idea
A saint’s death is a peaceful homecoming, not an end.

This passage gives us a rare glimpse into the final hour of one of Islam's greatest scholars. Through the eyes of his brother, we see how Imam al-Ghazali calmly prepared for death, treating it as an audience with the King. His actions teach us about the ideal Muslim's approach to the end of life.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative of his last morning
Read the Arabic passage describing Monday at dawn. Notice the sequence of actions: ablution, prayer, requesting the shroud, kissing it, placing it on his eyes, and his words.
The words 'Hearing and obeying to enter upon the King' show his eager submission.
2
Reflect on his actions with the shroud
He kissed the shroud and placed it on his eyes. This shows reverence and acceptance of death as a gift.
3
Note the posture: facing the qibla
He stretched his legs and faced the qibla, the direction of prayer. This is a sunna for the dying.
4
Consider the timing and place
He died before dawn on Monday, 14 Jumada al-Akhira 505 AH, in Tus. The early hour symbolizes spiritual light.
5
Contemplate the legacy of this death
This account is preserved to show how a scholar of his rank transitions from this world to the next with grace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال التاج السبكي : ( قال أبو الفرج بن الجوزي في كتاب ( الثبات عند الممات » : قال أحمد أخو الإمام الغزالي : لما كان يوم الإثنين وقت الصبح .. توضأ أخي أبو حامد وصلّى ، وقال : عليَّ بالكفن ؛ فأخذه وقبله ووضعه على عينيه وقال : سمعاً وطاعة للدخول على الملك ، ثم مدَّ رجليه واستقبل القبلة ، ومات قبل الإسفار قدَّسَ الله روحه ((١) وكان ذلك يوم الإثنين الرابع عشر من جمادى الآخرة سنة ( ٥٠٥هـ ) ودفن بالطابران في طوس مسقط رأسه ، رحمه الله ورضي عنه وأجزل مثوبته وجزاءه ومما ينسب له من الشعر في هذا المقام : · قُل لإخوان رَأَوْنى مياً فَبَكَوْني وَرَثُوا لِي حَزَنَا لَيْسَ ذاكَ الْمَبْتُ وَالله أنا أَتَظُنُّونَ بِا ـا فِي الصُّورِ وَهَذا جَسَدِي كانَ بَيْنِي وَقَمِيصِي زَمَنَا أَنَا عُصْفُورٌ وَهَذَا قَفَصِي طِرْتُ مِنْهُ وبَقِي مُرْتَهَنا أَحْمَدُ اللهَ الَّذِي خَلَّصَنِ وَبَنَى لِي فِي الْمَعالي سَكَنا كُنْتُ قَبْلَ اليَومِ مَيْتاً بَيْنَكُـ فَحَينتُ وَخَلَعْتُ الْكَفَنا
Al-Taj al-Subki said: Abu al-Faraj ibn al-Jawzi said in the book 'al-Thabat ‘inda al-Mamat': Ahmad, the brother of Imam al-Ghazali, said: When it was Monday at the time of dawn, my brother Abu Hamid performed ablution and prayed, and said: Bring me the shroud. So he took it, kissed it, placed it on his eyes and said: Hearing and obeying to enter upon the King. Then he stretched his legs and faced the qibla, and died before daybreak—may Allah sanctify his soul. That was Monday, the fourteenth of Jumada al-Akhira in the year 505 AH. He was buried in al-Tabaran in Tus, his birthplace. May Allah have mercy on him and be pleased with him and multiply his reward and recompense. Among the poetry attributed to him on this occasion: Say to brothers who saw me dead and wept for me, grieving for me with a sorrow that is not that—by Allah, do you think [؟] I am in the grave? This is my body; there was between me and my shirt a time. I am a bird and this is my cage; I flew from it and it remains as a pledge. I praise Allah who delivered me and built for me a dwelling in the lofty stations. Before today I was dead among you, so I came to life and removed the shroud.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of Imam al-Ghazali's death, narrated by al-Taj al-Subki via Ibn al-Jawzi, quoting the Imam's brother Ahmad. It begins by describing the events of the Monday morning when al-Ghazali performed ablution and prayer, then requested his shroud, which he kissed and placed on his eyes, saying 'Hearing and obeying to enter upon the King'—indicating his readiness to meet Allah. He then lay down facing the qibla and died before dawn. The date is given as the 14th of Jumada al-Akhira 505 AH, and his burial place is al-Tabaran in Tus. The text then attributes a poem to him on this occasion. The poem uses metaphorical language: the body is a shirt or cage, and the soul is a bird that has flown away. He addresses those who weep for him, telling them not to think he is truly dead; rather, he has been delivered from this worldly prison and now dwells in lofty stations. The final line states that he was 'dead' before today (i.e., in ignorance or heedlessness) and has now come to life and removed the shroud. The poem emphasizes the spiritual perspective on death—liberation of the soul.

Key Terms
الكفن
القبلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

?

The Deathbed Poem: Soul's Liberation
Presents a poem attributed to Imam al-Ghazali on his deathbed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual metaphor in Imam al-Ghazali's poem: the soul as a bird freed from the cage of the body.

Core Idea
Death is not loss; it is the soul flying free from its cage.

Imam al-Ghazali's poem on his deathbed uses vivid imagery to convey the joy of the soul leaving this world. He addresses those who weep for him, telling them not to mistake the body for his true self. The poem is a powerful reminder that for the believer, death is liberation.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem in Arabic
Read the lines of the poem. Notice the repetition and the metaphors. Some words may be unclear due to manuscript variations.
The line 'بِا ـا فِي الصُّورِ' is uncertain, but the general meaning is clear.
2
Identify the central metaphor
He compares his body to a shirt (قميص) and a cage (قفص), and his soul to a bird (عصفور) that has flown away.
3
Understand the message to the mourners
He tells them not to think he is in the grave; his body is just what remains. He is with Allah.
4
Reflect on the final line
'I was dead among you, then I came to life and removed the shroud.' This suggests that true life begins after death.
5
Consider the spiritual lesson
The poem transforms the sorrow of death into hope and gratitude. It shows the perspective of a saint who sees death as deliverance.

Scholars' Testimonies on al-Ghazali's Virtue
Lists praises of Imam al-Ghazali by various scholars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the testimonies of major scholars about Imam al-Ghazali's knowledge and spiritual rank, demonstrating his unanimous recognition as a leading authority in both outward and inward sciences.

Core Idea
The consensus of scholars across disciplines places Imam al-Ghazali as the unparalleled Proof of Islam.

This passage gathers the praises of some of the most prominent scholars from different fields—jurisprudence, theology, Sufism, and history—all affirming Imam al-Ghazali's extraordinary knowledge and spiritual station. As you read each testimonial, notice how they highlight different aspects: his sea-like depth, his role as 'Hujjat al-Islam', his siddiqiyya, and his mastery of both rational and transmitted sciences. The passage aims to establish his authority in the eyes of the Muslim scholarly community.

Guided Path
1
Understand the purpose of the collection
Read the introductory sentence to see that these are 'jumpalat shahadat al-ulama' (a collection of testimonies). This sets the context that the chapter aims to legitimize al-Ghazali's authority.
The author is building a case for al-Ghazali's credibility before presenting his theological arguments.
2
Examine each testimonial in order
Go through each scholar's statement. Notice the diverse backgrounds: teachers, historians, jurists, Sufis. Each highlights a different dimension of al-Ghazali's excellence.
Pay attention to specific phrases like 'bahr mughriq', 'hujjat al-Islam', 'rabbani al-ummah', 'siddiqiyya al-uzma'.
3
Reflect on the concluding statement
The author ends by saying these and more fall short of his true virtue. This underscores the superlative nature of al-Ghazali's rank.
This conclusion serves to elevate al-Ghazali beyond any limited praise.

Praises of the scholars concerning Imam al-Ghazali: This is a collection of testimonies of the scholars regarding the worth, knowledge, and virtue of the Imam. His teacher Imam al-Haramayn said: 'al-Ghazali is an overwhelming sea.' 'Abd al-Ghafir al-Farisi said: 'The proof of Islam and the Muslims, the imam of the imams of the religion, one whose like eyes have not seen in tongue, expression, speech, mind, intelligence, and natural disposition.' Al-Hafiz Ibn 'Asakir said: 'He was an imam in the science of jurisprudence—both in the school and in differences of opinion—and in the foundations of religions.' Ibn al-Najjar said: 'Abu Hamid is the imam of the jurists absolutely, and the rabbani (divinely learned) of the nation by consensus, and the mujtahid of his time, and the eye of his era. He excelled in the school, principles, differences, debate, and logic, and he studied wisdom and philosophy, understood their speech, and undertook to refute them. He was extremely intelligent, strong in perception, possessing penetrating insight and deep delving into meanings.' When the arif Abu al-'Abbas al-Mursi was asked about him, he said: 'I testify that he has the highest siddiqiyya (veracity).' Abu al-Hasan al-Shadhili said about him: 'If you have a need with Allah, then seek intercession through Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali.' Al-Hafiz al-Dhahabi wrote about him saying: 'The shaykh, the imam, the sea, the proof of Islam, the wonder of the age...' Al-Taj al-Subki said about him: 'The proof of Islam, the highway of the religion by which one reaches the abode of peace, the collector of the various sciences, the outstanding one in transmitted and intellectual sciences.' And many more things that fall short of expressing the reality of his virtue and the elevation of his rank.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a collection of testimonies from renowned scholars praising Imam al-Ghazali's knowledge, virtue, and spiritual rank. It begins by stating that this is a summary of scholarly testimonies. The first is from his teacher, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, who called him 'an overwhelming sea'—a metaphor for his vast knowledge. Then 'Abd al-Ghafir al-Farisi gives a detailed description, calling him 'the proof of Islam and the Muslims' and praising his unmatched eloquence, intelligence, and natural gifts. Ibn 'Asakir highlights his mastery in jurisprudence—both within the Shafi'i school and in comparative fiqh—and in 'usul al-diyanat' (principles of religions). Ibn al-Najjar provides a lengthy tribute, noting his absolute imamate in fiqh, his consensus as the 'rabbani' of the nation, his rank as mujtahid, his excellence in multiple disciplines including logic and philosophy, and his refutation of the philosophers. Then two Sufi masters testify: Abu al-'Abbas al-Mursi grants him the highest degree of 'siddiqiyya' (truthfulness and veracity), and Abu al-Hasan al-Shadhili recommends seeking intercession through him in times of need—an indication of his spiritual station. Al-Dhahabi calls him 'the wonder of the age', and al-Taj al-Subki calls him 'the highway of the religion' and the one who collected all sciences. The passage concludes that these and many other praises still fall short of his true virtue. The overall purpose is to establish al-Ghazali's unparalleled authority in both outward ('ilm) and inward (spiritual) sciences.

بحر مغرق
صديقية
رباني الأمة

Correction: This passage shows that scholars from various fields—teachers, jurists, historians, Sufis—all unanimously testify to his vast knowledge and high spiritual rank.

Praise of Imam Ghazali by Four Great Scholars
A collection of four scholars' testimonies praising Imam Ghazali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the high scholarly and spiritual stature of Imam Ghazali as attested by four eminent figures from different disciplines.

Core Idea
Four scholars from different fields all affirm Imam Ghazali's unparalleled rank.

Imam Ghazali is widely recognized as a reviver of the Islamic sciences. This passage collects testimonies from a Sufi master, a hadith scholar, a legal historian, and a theologian. Each praises him from their own perspective, revealing the breadth of his excellence.

ومجتهد زمانه ، وعين أوانه ، برع في المذهب والأصول والخلاف والجدل والمنطق ، وقرأ الحكمة والفلسفة ، وفهم كلامهم وتصدى للرد عليهم ، وكان شديد الذكاء ، قوي الإدراك ، ذا فطنة ثاقبة وغوص في المعاني ) . ولما سئل عنه العارف أبو العباس المرسي . . قال : ( أنا أشهد له بالصديقية العظمى ) . وقال فيه العارف أبو الحسن الشاذلي : ( إذا عرضت لكم إلى الله حاجة . . فتوسلوا إليه بالإمام أبي حامد الغزالي ) وترجم له الحافظ الذهبي فقال : ( الشيخ الإمام البحر ، حجة الإسلام ، أعجوبة الزمان . . . . ) . وقال فيه التاج السبكي : ( حجة الإسلام، ومحجة الدين التي يتوصل بها إلى دار السلام ، جامع أشتات العلوم ، والمبرز في المنقول منها والمفهوم ) . وغيرها الكثير مما يقصر عن بيان حقيقة فضله ، وسمو مكانته
And the mujtahid of his time, the peer of his era, excelled in the school (madhhab), principles (usul), disagreements (khilaf), debate (jadal), and logic (mantiq). He studied philosophy and wisdom (al-hikma wa al-falsafa), understood their speech, and undertook to refute them. He had extreme intelligence, strong perception, penetrating insight, and depth in meanings. When the gnostic Abu al-Abbas al-Mursi was asked about him, he said: 'I bear witness to him as having the greatest veracity (al-sidqiyya al-uzma).' And the gnostic Abu al-Hasan al-Shadhili said: 'If a need for God arises for you, seek intercession (tawassul) to Him through Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali.' Al-Hafiz al-Dhahabi translated his biography and said: 'The shaykh, the imam, the sea, the Proof of Islam (Hujjat al-Islam), the wonder of the age.' And Taj al-Subki said: 'The Proof of Islam, the path of religion by which one reaches the abode of peace (dar al-salam), the gatherer of varied sciences, the foremost in transmitted (manqul) and conceptual (mafhum) sciences.' And many other praises that fall short of expressing the reality of his virtue and the height of his station.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a biographical eulogy for Imam al-Ghazali. It begins by describing his expertise in various Islamic sciences: he was a mujtahid, excelling in jurisprudence (madhhab), principles of jurisprudence (usul), legal disagreements (khilaf), debate (jadal), and logic (mantiq). He also studied philosophy and wisdom, understood the arguments of the philosophers, and set out to refute them. His intellectual qualities are then highlighted: extreme intelligence, strong perception, penetrating insight, and depth in understanding meanings. Next, notable scholars testify to his stature. First, the gnostic Abu al-Abbas al-Mursi states he bears witness to Ghazali having the 'greatest veracity' (al-sidqiyya al-uzma), a high rank of truthfulness. Second, Abu al-Hasan al-Shadhili advises seeking intercession through Ghazali when one has a need from God. Third, the hadith scholar al-Dhahabi calls him 'the shaykh, the imam, the sea, the Proof of Islam, the wonder of the age.' Fourth, Taj al-Subki elaborates: he is the Proof of Islam, the path of religion leading to Paradise, the gatherer of all sciences, and the most excellent in both transmitted and intellectual sciences. The passage concludes that all these praises still fall short of conveying the full reality of his virtue and lofty station.

المنقول والمفهوم

Correction: The praises come from multiple authoritative scholars across different disciplines, confirming Ghazali's extraordinary rank. They reflect the high regard of his contemporaries and later scholars.

Nizam al-Mulk's Strategic Patronage and Reforms
Describes Nizam al-Mulk's role in countering sectarian strife through patronage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nizam al-Mulk's deliberate strategy of patronizing scholars and building schools shaped the religious and political landscape of his era and set the stage for Imam al-Ghazali's work.

Core Idea
Nizam al-Mulk's reforms were a deliberate, not accidental, response to the challenges of his time.

This passage introduces us to Nizam al-Mulk, the Seljuk vizier whose political and religious reforms countered the sectarian chaos of the fifth Islamic century. By understanding his strategic patronage of Sunni scholars and construction of Nizamiyya schools, we see how he stabilized the state and nurtured the environment that produced scholars like Imam al-Ghazali. Let's explore how his actions were far from random.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

2
Identify his key actions

3
Analyze the author's argument
The author argues Nizam al-Mulk's actions were deliberate, based on his awareness of the era's problems. Find the sentence that makes this claim and reflect on its significance.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Connect to Imam al-Ghazali
The passage ends by hinting at Ghazali's connection to Nizam al-Mulk. Think about why this context is important for understanding Ghazali's own work.

Nizam al-Mulk and political and social reform: The Seljuqs had a very wholesome effect in the land of Khurasan by appointing Nizam al-Mulk as vizier, upon whom the books of histories concur in praise. And Nishapur is a piece of Ghazali's era that gathered the scattered images of this age, so much so that some researchers liked to call the fifth century the 'Age of Khurasan'. Nizam al-Mulk (408-485 AH) was born, lived, ruled, and died in this century, and he was more than a vizier. Whoever reads his biography and knows him – the status and love of Qiwam al-Din (Nizam al-Mulk) settles in his heart. He was a brilliant politician who knew how to repel opponents and consolidate the pillars of the state, and a scholar of hadith, literature, and language; scholars, Quran-readers, and jurists frequented his assembly. He had a clear inclination towards Sufis and people of spiritual hearts, and fine etiquette with the Sharia and its rulings. Among those who attended his assembly were Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni and Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri, author of al-Risala. And we will see for Imam al-Ghazali a close connection with this extraordinary personality. But why speak about Nizam al-Mulk? We do not exaggerate if we say: Nizam al-Mulk had an almost complete awareness of the currents of his age and the details of its events, and this awareness enabled him to handle its difficulties and escape its most prominent trials. So his bringing close of scholars – especially the heads of Ahl al-Sunna – was not merely a coincidence or a customary habit, and his building of the Nizamiyya schools attributed to him in the most prominent cities of the caliphate such as Baghdad, Nishapur, Tus, and Isfahan was not random; these particular cities had their problems and disturbances.

This section introduces the role of Nizam al-Mulk, the Seljuk vizier. It praises his political and religious reforms. He patronized Sunni scholars, especially the likes of al-Juwayni and al-Qushayri, and built Nizamiyya schools in key cities to combat sectarian chaos. The author argues that his actions were deliberate and strategic, not accidental: he understood his era and used education and patronage to stabilize the state and counter heresies. This background is important for understanding Ghazali's connection to Nizam al-Mulk.

Three Observations on Criticisms of Imam al-Ghazali
The author lists three common flaws in criticisms of Imam al-Ghazali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three common methodological flaws in criticisms of Imam al-Ghazali as identified by the author.

Core Idea
Criticisms of Ghazali often suffer from vagueness, selective targeting, and uncritical acceptance.

The author presents three keen observations about the nature of criticisms leveled against Imam al-Ghazali. These observations serve as a methodological guide for researchers, highlighting recurring problems such as vague generalizations, singling out the Imam for accusations shared by others, and blind acceptance of claims from authoritative names. Understanding these flaws helps one approach the study of Ghazali with greater discernment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get a sense of the three observations.
Notice the repeated phrases which may be scribal, but don't let them distract.
2
Identify each observation
Break down the passage to label each of the three observations clearly. The author uses 'الأولى', 'الثانية', 'الثالثة'.
3
Understand the critique
For each observation, think about why the author considers it a flaw in the criticisms. What is the underlying methodological problem?
The first observation critiques vagueness; the second notes selective targeting; the third criticizes blind acceptance.
4
Reflect on the methodological lesson
Consider how these observations can guide your own study of theological debates. How can you avoid these pitfalls?

ملاحظات ثلاث حول هذه الانتقادات : وأول منغصة تواجه الباحث في دراسة هذه الانتقادات : هي تلك العمومية الممجوجة في طرح المشكلة ، ترى كلمة كبيرة جداً تسبح في بحر من الاحتمالات ، يلقيها قائلها دون لجام ، تدلُّ أول ما تدل على حيرة صاحبها في فهم منهج وفكر الإمام في فهم منهج وفكر الإمام ، وكأنه أراد الانتقاد وكفى !! والثانية : أن ترى الإمام يقاطع الجلة علماء من عصره وسابقيه في عين التهمة الموجهة إليه ، إلا أن المنتقد ينأى بنفسه عنهم ، ويصوب نصاله للإمام فحسب وأنت تحار وتقول : ولكن لم ؟! والثالثة : هي وقوفُ كثير من المتأخرين المتأخرين مع هذه الأحكام العجلة على شخص الإمام مبهورين ببريق الأسماء الكبيرة التي فاهت بها ، ناسين أو متناسين مهمة البحث والتحقق عن مصداقية هذه التهمات المكالة ، أو على الأقل عن دواعيها وأسبابها .
Three observations regarding these criticisms: The first annoyance that faces the researcher in studying these criticisms is that excessive generality in presenting the problem—you see a very large word swimming in a sea of possibilities, uttered by its speaker without restraint, indicating first of all the confusion of its owner in understanding the methodology and thought of the Imam in understanding the methodology and thought of the Imam, as if he wanted to criticize, and that's enough!! The second: that you see the Imam sharing with the dignitaries of scholars of his time and before him in the very accusation directed at him, except that the critic distances himself from them, and directs his arrows only at the Imam, and you are perplexed and say: But why?! The third: is the reliance of many later scholars later scholars upon these hasty judgments about the person of the Imam, dazzled by the glitter of the great names that uttered them, forgetting or pretending to forget the task of investigation and verification of the credibility of these accusations, or at least of their motives and causes.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage enumerates three observations that the author makes about the nature of criticisms leveled against Imam al-Ghazali. It is a classification of common errors in such criticisms. The first observation is that many criticisms are presented with excessive generality—they are vague and sweeping, lacking specificity, which indicates the critic's own confusion about the Imam's methodology. The second observation is that the Imam is often singled out for accusations that apply equally to other prominent scholars of his time or earlier, yet critics ignore those others and focus solely on Ghazali. The third observation is that later scholars often accept these criticisms uncritically, being impressed by the fame of the early critics, without undertaking the necessary verification of the accusations. These three points serve as a methodological critique of how Ghazali has been treated.

Three Scholars' Criticisms of Ghazali's Hadith Knowledge
Ghazali presents three scholars' critiques of his hadith knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific hadith-related criticisms leveled against Ghazali by three classical scholars and their implications for his methodology.

Core Idea
Ghazali was accused by three scholars of lacking hadith credentials and filling al-Ihya' with fabricated hadith.

In defending his methodology, Ghazali acknowledges that he will address one major accusation — that he was weak in hadith. He presents direct quotations from three prominent scholars who criticized his hadith expertise. These criticisms center on his lack of isnad, his inclusion of fabricated hadith in al-Ihya', and the severity of fabrications. Understanding these criticisms is key to evaluating Ghazali's approach to hadith in his theological works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for context
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Ghazali's framing and the three critiques.
Notice the preamble: Ghazali says he will only discuss one major accusation.
2
Identify each scholar and their criticism
List Ibn al-Najjar, Ibn al-Jawzi, and al-Turtushi. For each, write down the precise charge they make against Ghazali.
Ibn al-Najjar focuses on lack of isnad; Ibn al-Jawzi on fabricated hadith; al-Turtushi on severity.
3
Analyze the metaphor 'night gatherer'

It suggests collecting indiscriminately without verification.
4
Consider the author's purpose
Ghazali presents these criticisms to respond to them. Think about why he includes them and how they relate to his overall defense.
He is setting up the accusation he will address in detail.

وليس الغرض في هذه الوجازة تفصيل القول في كل انتقادة عابثة ، بل هي لماعات تضيء الجوانب المغفلة التي كان يجب البحث فيها ، وتبرز المشكلة في صورتها الموضوعية وبكل إنصاف ، خلا مسألة واحدة ، وجب تفصيل القول فيها ؛ لأنها من كبريات التهمات . ألصقهن بحياة الإمام وفكره ومنهجه ، ولها علاقة وطيدة بـ «الإقتصاد» هي وهي وكلامه ومنطقه علما الرواية والدراية ومكان الإمام منهما : ، فلسفته قال الحافظ ابن النجار : ( ولم يكن له إسناد ، ولا طلب شيئاً من الحديث ، ولم أرَ له إلا حديثاً واحداً ((۱) ) وقال ابن الجوزي : ( وذكر في كتاب « الإحياء » من الأحاديث الموضوعة وما لا يصح ذلك قلة غير قليل ، وسبب معرفته بالنقل ، فليته عرض تلك الأحاديث على من يعرف وإنما نقل نقل حاطب ليل ((٢) ) وقال أبو بكر الطرطوشي : ( شحن الغزالي كتابه ( الإحياء ) بالكذب على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فلا أعلم كتاباً على بسطة الأرض أكثر كذباً على رسول الله منه ((٣) . (۱) طبقات الشافعية الكبرى ( ٦ / ٢٠٠ ) (۲) المنتظم ( ١٠/ ١١٥ ) (۳) تاريخ الإسلام ( ٣٥ / ١٢٤ ) .
And the purpose in this brevity is not to detail the discussion of every frivolous criticism, but rather they are flashes that illuminate the overlooked aspects that should have been investigated, and present the problem in its objective form and with complete fairness, except for one issue, which requires detailed discussion because it is among the major accusations, the most closely related to the life of the Imam, his thought, and his methodology, and it has a strong connection to 'al-Iqtisad', namely: and it is and his speech and his logic, the two sciences of narration and knowledge and the Imam's position concerning them: , his philosophy. Al-Hafiz Ibn al-Najjar said: 'He had no chain of transmission, nor did he seek anything of hadith, and I have not seen for him except one hadith.' And Ibn al-Jawzi said: 'He mentioned in the book al-Ihya from fabricated hadith and what is not authentic, that is not a small amount, and the reason for his knowledge is through transmission; if only he had shown those hadith to someone who knows, but he transmitted like a night gatherer.' And Abu Bakr al-Turtushi said: 'Ghazali filled his book al-Ihya with lies against the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and I do not know a book on the face of the earth that contains more lies against the Messenger of Allah than it.' (1) Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyya al-Kubra (6/200) (2) al-Muntazam (10/115) (3) Tarikh al-Islam (35/124).
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage begins with a statement of purpose: the author says that the aim is not to address every petty criticism, but to highlight one major accusation that is closely tied to Ghazali's life and to his book al-Iqtisad. The text then seems to list the specific subject: 'his speech and logic, the two sciences of narration and knowledge and the Imam's position concerning them: his philosophy.' The phrase 'his philosophy' (falsafatuhu) appears but is somewhat unclear in context. Then the author presents three direct quotes from classical scholars who criticized Ghazali's hadith expertise. First, al-Hafiz Ibn al-Najjar states that Ghazali had no chain of transmission (isnad) and only one hadith attributed to him. Second, Ibn al-Jawzi accuses Ghazali of including fabricated and weak hadith in his book Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din, and that he transmitted uncritically like a night gatherer (i.e., collecting anything without discernment). Third, Abu Bakr al-Turtushi makes an even stronger claim that al-Ihya' contains more lies against the Prophet than any other book on earth. These quotations form the core of the criticism that Ghazali lacked proper hadith training.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These criticisms prove Ghazali completely ignorant of hadith.
Correction: The criticisms reflect a specific methodological difference; Ghazali prioritized fiqh and kalam over hadith transmission.

Checkpoint
Which scholar accused Ghazali of having no chain of transmission (isnad) and only one hadith?
A) Ibn al-Jawzi
B) Ibn al-Najjar
C) al-Turtushi

Key Takeaway
Three classical scholars accused Ghazali of having no hadith credentials and filling al-Ihya' with fabricated hadith, a major challenge to his methodology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a scholar known for his intellectual depth, do you assume he is also reliable in every field of Islamic knowledge? How has this lesson exposed the risk of such an assumption—and what does it demand of you when you seek knowledge today?
?
Ibn al-Jawzi compared Ghazali's transmission to a 'night gatherer' who picks up anything without discernment. In your own pursuit of Islamic knowledge, where do you risk being a 'night gatherer'—taking information without verifying its source? What specific step will you take tonight to safeguard your deen from weak or fabricated narrations?
?
The three critics were not attacking a layman but one of Islam's greatest minds. If even Ghazali could be accused of spreading fabricated hadith, what does that say about your own responsibility when you share Islamic content online or teach someone a hadith without checking its authenticity? Will you repent from that habit and commit to only speaking with knowledge?

Life of Imam al-Ghazali
intermediate

Criticism of Ghazali's Hadith Knowledge and Its Refutation
Editorial defense of Ghazali's hadith knowledge with criticisms and refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the criticism against Imam al-Ghazali's hadith knowledge and the evidence used to defend him.

Core Idea
Even great scholars can be misunderstood; investigation reveals the full picture.

This editorial passage addresses one of the most persistent criticisms of Imam al-Ghazali: his alleged weakness in hadith. Several prominent scholars accused him of lacking isnad and using fabricated hadith in his works. The passage presents these criticisms, then provides evidence that Ghazali did engage with hadith, especially later in life. It teaches us to evaluate accusations carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the criticisms
Identify the accusations from Ibn al-Najjar, Ibn al-Jawzi, al-Turtushi, and al-Dhahabi. Notice how each critic frames the deficiency.
These are strong claims from respected scholars.
2
Consider Ghazali's own admission
Read Ghazali's statement: 'Know that my stock in the science of hadith is weak.' This is often used to support the criticisms.
Does this admission prove the critics right or require context?
3
Examine the refutation
Understand al-Zabidi's explanation that the weakness refers to Ghazali's early life, and the evidence from Ibn Asakir about Ghazali's later turn to hadith.
This shifts the timeline and shows growth.
4
Assess the evidence of narration
Note that Ghazali heard Sahih al-Bukhari from Abu Sahl al-Hafsi and recited it to Abu al-Fityan al-Rawasi. Also, later scholars narrated from him via chains.
This directly refutes the claim that he had no isnad.
5
Conclude the lesson
The editor concludes that denying narration is false, and that Ghazali's admission is misunderstood. Reflect on how this episode illustrates the need for thorough investigation.
Consider the broader lesson about evaluating scholarly accusations.

وهو يومها ابن الخمسين ـ ثابت بلا مراء ؛ إذ روى ابن عساكر عن عبد الغافر الفارسي - وهو صاحب ترجمة الإمام التي تتناقلها الكتب - قوله : ( وكانت خاتمة أمره إقباله على حديث المصطفى صلى الله عليه وسلم ومجالسة أهله ومطالعة الصحيحين للبخاري ومسلم اللذين هما حجة الإسلام ، ولو عاش . . لسبق في ذلك الفن بيسير من الأيام يستفرغه في تحصيله ، ولا شك أنه الحديث في الأيام الماضية ) (٣) سمع وقد ثبت سماعه لـ « صحيح البخاري » رواية من أبي سهل الحفصي (٤) ، كما قرأ الصحيح على أبي الفتيان الرواسي بطوس ، وقد روى عن الغزالي بسنده لبعض أحاديث الصحيحين كل من التاج السبكي والحافظ الزبيدي (٥) (٦) القارىء الكريم فنفي أصل الرواية باطل كما يرى لقد أنصف الإمام حين قال في كلمته المتواضعة الدالة على جميم أدبه : ( وأعلم أن بضاعتي في علم الحديث مزجاة ( ولكن يسيء البعض في فهم هذه الكلمة إذ يحسب الإمام
A bridle? It first indicates the confusion of its owner in understanding the methodology and thought of the Imam, as if he intended criticism, and that's enough!! The second: that you see the Imam interrupting the leading scholars of his time and predecessors in the very accusation directed at him, but the critic distances himself from them and aims his arrows only at the Imam, and you wonder and say: But why?! The third: is the standing of many later scholars with these hasty judgments about the Imam, dazzled by the glitter of the big names that uttered them, forgetting or pretending to forget the task of researching and verifying the credibility of these fabricated accusations, or at least their motives and causes. The purpose of this brevity is not to detail every frivolous criticism, but rather flashes that illuminate the neglected aspects that should have been investigated, and present the problem objectively and fairly, except for one issue which must be detailed because it is among the greatest accusations, most closely related to the Imam's life, thought, and methodology, and has a strong connection to al-Iqtisad; it is? and his speech and logic, the sciences of transmission and knowledge, and the Imam's position regarding them: his philosophy. Al-Hafiz Ibn al-Najjar said: 'He had no chain of transmission, nor did he seek any hadith, and I have seen only one hadith from him that is authentic.' Ibn al-Jawzi said: 'He mentioned in the book al-Ihya fabricated hadiths and what not, much of it, and the reason is his lack of knowledge of transmission. If only he had presented those hadiths to someone who knows, but he transmitted like a night gatherer.' Abu Bakr al-Turtushi said: 'al-Ghazali filled his book al-Ihya with lies against the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. I do not know a book on the face of the earth with more lies against the Messenger of Allah than it.' Al-Hafiz al-Dhahabi said: 'He had weak stock in traditions despite his vast knowledge and his great rank and majesty.' Imam al-Ghazali himself sums it up and spares people the trouble of talking, saying: 'Know that my stock in the science of hadith is weak.' As for Ibn al-Najjar, may Allah have mercy on him, his statement is not accepted unless it was as al-Hafiz al-Zabidi said: 'It seems he refers to his early period, for his focus at that time was on acquiring the various sciences.' His seeking of hadith as a narrator in the later part of his life—when he was fifty—is established without doubt. Ibn Asakir narrated from Abd al-Ghafir al-Farsi, who is the author of the biography of the Imam that books transmit, saying: 'The end of his affair was his turning to the hadith of the Chosen One, peace be upon him, and sitting with its people and studying the two Sahihs of Bukhari and Muslim, which are the proof of Islam. Had he lived... he would have surpassed in that field with a few days he would have devoted to its acquisition, and there is no doubt that he heard hadith in the past days.' He (the editor said): He has confirmed hearing of Sahih al-Bukhari in narration from Abu Sahl al-Hafsi, and he recited the Sahih to Abu al-Fityan al-Rawasi in Tus. Both Taj al-Subki and al-Hafiz al-Zabidi narrated from al-Ghazali with his chain some hadiths of the two Sahihs. O noble reader, the denial of narration in principle is false, as you see. The Imam was fair when he said in his humble statement indicating his beautiful manners: 'Know that my stock in the science of hadith is weak,' but some misunderstand this word, as they suppose the Imam...
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is an editorial defense of Imam al-Ghazali against criticisms regarding his knowledge of hadith. The editor begins by describing three types of critics: those who are confused about the Imam's methodology, those who single him out for accusations while ignoring other scholars, and those who blindly follow the accusations of famous names without investigating. Then the editor narrows down to one major accusation: Ghazali's alleged weakness in hadith. Several scholars are quoted: Ibn al-Najjar says he had no isnad and only one authentic hadith; Ibn al-Jawzi claims he used fabricated hadith in Ihya due to ignorance; al-Turtushi calls Ihya the most lying book; al-Dhahabi says his stock in traditions was weak. Ghazali himself is quoted admitting his stock in hadith is weak. The editor then refutes these criticisms by citing al-Zabidi's explanation that this refers to Ghazali's early life, when he focused on other sciences. Evidence is provided: in his later years, Ghazali sought hadith, heard Sahih al-Bukhari from Abu Sahl al-Hafsi, recited it to Abu al-Fityan al-Rawasi, and transmitted hadith through chains recorded by al-Subki and al-Zabidi. The editor concludes that denying his narration is false, and that his admission of weakness is misunderstood.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali never engaged with hadith and had no knowledge of it.
Correction: The passage shows he studied and narrated hadith later in life, and his admission of weakness refers to his early period.

Checkpoint

C) He memorized the entire Sahihayn.
D) He criticized the hadith scholars of his time.

Key Takeaway
Despite early focus on other sciences, al-Ghazali engaged with hadith in his later years, refuting claims of complete ignorance.

Life of Imam al-Ghazali

Defense of al-Ghazali's Hadith Methodology in Ihya
Author defends Ghazali's hadith methodology in Ihya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the defense of al-Ghazali against criticism regarding weak hadiths in Ihya, and the concept of narration by meaning.

Core Idea
Ghazali's hadiths in Ihya are not fabricated but based on transmission by meaning.

This passage defends Imam al-Ghazali against critics who questioned the authenticity of nearly a thousand hadiths in his masterpiece, Ihya Ulum al-Din. The author argues that these hadiths, while not always traceable, are not fabrications. Instead, they likely arise from al-Ghazali's practice of narrating by meaning—a legitimate method for scholars of his caliber. The defense also highlights the historical context of widespread innovation, which occupied al-Ghazali's efforts and excused him from specializing in hadith transmission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice how the author structures his defense by first acknowledging criticism, then offering counterarguments.
Pay attention to the footnote by Taj al-Subki at the end.
2
Identify the three main defenses
The author presents three key defenses: (1) most hadiths do not contradict established principles, (2) they may be based on narration by meaning, and (3) Ghazali's efforts were focused on combating innovation, not on hadith transmission.
These are not explicitly numbered but can be extracted from the flow.
3
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that Ghazali fabricated hadiths. How does the author correct this? Think about the role of ijtihad and context in evaluating a scholar's work.

وأحاديثُ الإحياء التي تتبعها التاج السبكي ولم يجد لها أصلاً تقارب الألف ، والتاج متأدب عارف بقدر الإمام متتبع لحديثه غيرةً على سنة الحبيب المصطفى صلى الله عليه وسلم ، ولكن المتأمل في تلك الأحاديث سيجد أن جلَّها - إن لم تكن كلها ـ منطو تحت لا يخالف الأصول الثابتة ، ولا أريد أن أقول : هي ليس بمكذوبة ، بل هي على قواعد الصنعة الحديثية كذلك بلا شك ، ولكنها قد تكون ناشئة عن رواية بالمعنى يقول بها الإمام الغزالي باجتهاده (۱) أصل شرعي ولو عرف الطرطوشي رحمه الله تعالى حجم الثلمة التي أحدثها المبتدعة في عصر الإمام ، وعرف الجهد الجبار الذي بذله الإمام لرأب الصدع.. لكن للغزالي الحب والاحترام، وحمد مولاه أن لم يشغل الغزالي نفسَهُ بالرواية يومئذ ومثله في الرواية كثير ، ولعله قال : كبرت كلمة خرجت من فيَّ لم تصاحبها النصفة من الحظوظ أما الإمام ابن الجوزي .. فهو صاحب رواية ودراية ، وكم قلب الحال فصحح التالف وأتلف الصحيح وكلنا يحفظ للإمام مكانته وجلالته، فهل سرى الداء الغزالي للحافظ ابن الجوزي ؟! أم هي طبيعة البشر ؟ على كل الأحوال كفتنا كلمة الغزالي المتواضعة عتاب الأئمة عليه ، ناهيك أن الإمام لم يصنف كتاباً متخصصاً في علم الرواية تحديداً ليكون مرجعاً ، ومآباً ، فهل بعد ذلك من شين؟! (۱) قال التاج السبكي رحمه الله : ( وأما ما عاب به ( الإحياء ) من توهنة بعض الأحاديث . . فالغزالي معروف بأنه لم تكن له في الحديث يد باسطة ، وعامة ما في ( الإحياء ) من الأخبار والآثار مبدد في كتب من سبقه من الصوفية ، ولم يسند الرجل لحديث واحد ، وقد اعتنى بتخريج أحاديث ( الإحياء » بعض أصحابنا ، فلم يشدّ عنه إلا اليسير ) . ( طبقات الشافعية الكبرى » ( ٢٤٩/٦ ) .
The hadiths of the Ihya that al-Taj al-Subki followed and did not find a source for approach a thousand. Al-Taj is an educated person, knowing the Imam's status, following his hadith out of zeal for the Sunnah of the Chosen Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace. But the one who contemplates those hadiths will find that most of them—if not all—are subsumed under not contradicting the established principles. I do not mean to say they are not fabricated; rather, they are according to the rules of hadith craftsmanship unquestionably, but they may arise from narration by meaning that Imam al-Ghazali practiced according to his ijtihad. (1) A legitimate principle. If al-Turtushi, may God have mercy on him, had known the size of the breach that the innovators caused in the Imam's era, and had known the tremendous effort exerted by the Imam to repair the rift... But for al-Ghazali there is love and respect, and he praised his Lord that al-Ghazali did not occupy himself with narration at that time—there were many like him in narration—and perhaps he said: "Great is the word that came out of my mouth that was not accompanied by fairness from desires." As for Imam Ibn al-Jawzi, he is a man of narration and insight. How often did he turn the situation upside down, correcting the corrupt and corrupting the correct. We all preserve the Imam's status and majesty. Did the disease of al-Ghazali spread to al-Hafiz Ibn al-Jawzi? Or is it human nature? In any case, al-Ghazali's humble statement is sufficient: the criticism of the imams against him. Not to mention that the Imam did not compose a specialized book in the science of narration specifically to be a reference and a resort. Is there any disgrace after that? (1) Al-Taj al-Subki, may God have mercy on him, said: "As for the criticism against al-Ihya regarding the weakness of some hadiths... al-Ghazali is known for not having an extended hand in hadith. Most of the reports and traditions in al-Ihya are scattered in books by Sufis before him. He did not attribute a single hadith to its source. Some of our companions have taken care to extract the hadiths of al-Ihya, and nothing beyond a little escaped them." (Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyya al-Kubra, 6/249).
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defends al-Ghazali against criticism regarding weak hadiths in Ihya. The author begins by noting that Taj al-Subki, despite his respect for Ghazali, found nearly a thousand hadiths without sources. However, the author argues that upon examination, most of these hadiths do not contradict established principles. They may not be fabricated according to hadith methodology; rather, they might arise from Ghazali's practice of narrating by meaning, based on his ijtihad. The author then mentions al-Turtushi, who criticized the wastage of effort, but suggests he would have understood the magnitude of the innovation crisis Ghazali faced. He praises Ghazali for not engaging in hadith narration at that time, as there were many narrators. Then he quotes Ibn al-Jawzi, a scholar of both narration and insight, who sometimes corrected errors but also made mistakes. The author concludes with Ghazali's humble statement that the criticism of scholars is sufficient, and notes that Ghazali did not write a specialized work on hadith narration, so the criticism is unwarranted. Finally, the footnote by Taj al-Subki confirms that Ghazali was not a hadith specialist but his material was already present in earlier Sufi works.

Key Terms
الحديث (hadith)
الإحياء (Ihya)
رواية بالمعنى (narration by meaning)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali fabricated hadiths in Ihya.
Correction: The author argues that the hadiths may be based on narration by meaning and do not contradict established principles, and that Ghazali did not claim to be a hadith specialist.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why are the hadiths in Ihya not necessarily fabricated?
A) They all have sound chains.
B) They were invented by later Sufis.
C) They may be based on narration by meaning and do not contradict established principles.
D) Ghazali admitted to fabricating them.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's use of hadith in Ihya is defended by the notion of narration by meaning and the context of combating innovation, not by claiming all hadiths are rigorously authentic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a scholarly work that you respect, and you find a flaw in it, does your first reaction resemble al-Turtushi and Ibn al-Jawzi (quick to criticize) or al-Subki and the author (seeking to understand the context)? Which is more difficult for your ego, and why?
?
The author praises al-Ghazali's humble acceptance of criticism. When someone points out a mistake in your understanding or practice of Islam, how do you respond? Do you have the same humility to say 'perhaps they are right and I am wrong'?
?
Al-Ghazali did not compose a specialized hadith book, yet his work Ihya remains influential. Have you ever undervalued someone's contribution because they were not specialized in a particular area? How can you apply this lesson to appreciate sincere efforts even if they are not perfect by the strictest standards?

Refuting the Accusation of Neglecting Jihad
Author refutes accusation by describing political context and quoting Ghazali on jihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali's biographer refutes the charge that he was lethargic about jihad by highlighting the political turmoil of his era and citing his own writings on the virtue of fighting.

Core Idea
Ghazali's asceticism and his magnum opus 'Ihya' prove his dedication, not laziness.

This passage comes from a defense of Imam al-Ghazali against critics who claimed he was indifferent to jihad. The author sets the stage by describing the fractured political landscape of Ghazali's time, then poses a series of rhetorical questions about the very works that shaped great leaders. Finally, he quotes Ghazali's own words from the Ihya to show that true ascetics are the most eager fighters. You'll see how context and text together refute the accusation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the political context
Read the first part describing the weakened caliphate, internal strife, and fall of cities. Note that the author says the problem was not numbers but moral decay.
This sets the stage for why Ghazali's efforts were intellectual rather than military.
2
Ponder the rhetorical questions
The author asks: what laziness could produce 'Ihya' and other works? Then describes Ghazali's ascetic life. Reflect on the implication that such productivity and sacrifice contradict laziness.
3
Analyze Ghazali's own words on jihad
Read the quotation from Ihya. Notice how Ghazali distinguishes between ascetics who rush to fight and hypocrites who flee. Identify the key contrast.
This direct evidence is the core refutation.
4
Connect back to the accusation
Consider how both the context and the quote together undermine the claim of neglect. The author shows that Ghazali was actively promoting jihad through his writings and by example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتهامه بالخمول والتثاقل عن الجهاد : لم تكن الأوضاع السياسية أحسن حالاً من الأوضاع الفكرية ، فعصر الإمام الغزالي لا يعرف فيه للخلافة هيبة، ناهيك عن وجود خلافة في مصر وأخرى من الأندلس غير خلافة بغداد الاسمية . وقراءة العصر توضح لنا مقدار الأدواء المتفشية التي صنعت عوامل الهزيمة وعلى رأسها التشرذم والتفرق والنخر من الداخل ، ومع هذا كله ومع سقوط أبرز المدن الإسلامية كالقدس وطرابلس وكثير من حواضر الساحل الشامي .. لم تكن المشكلة مشكلة عَدَدٍ وعُدَد ؛ لذلك لم تكن صيحةُ النفير العام الذي يفرض على كل فرد حمل السلاح .. معلنة من قبل الخليفة ، ولكن .. كيف كان حال الناس إذن ؟ إن لوحة العصر تؤكد وجود تطاحنات داخلية مهلكة ، وصراعات حول كراسي السلطة واغتيالات جبانة ، وأحقاد مشتعلة ، وفساد أخلاقي ، وابتعاد عن المنهج السليم ولنا أن نسأل : أي خمول هو هذا الذي يصنع « إحياء علوم الدين ) ؟ أربعون كتاباً تصنع الرجال وتقض مضاجع الأوهام، وغيره من الكتب والرسائل التي نبرتها يد الإمام لإصلاح الأمة وتهيئة الجيل النوري والصلاحي ثم أي خمول هو هاذا الذي عاشه الإمام مع عكاز ومرقعة وركوة وشظف عيش يتمنى المعترض لو حلّ فيه الموت فرراً من قسوته وطعناته ؟! ثم ما هو رأي وموقف الإمام من الجهاد ؟ ينقل الأستاذ أسعد الخطيب نصوصاً متفرقة عن الإمام الغزالي في عدة من كتبه تبرهن على نقاوة وطهارة الإمام من هذه التهمة السخيفة ؛ منها : ( فالحاصل : أن الزهد عبارة عن الرغبة عن البقاء في الدنيا . . . لذلك : لما كتب عليهم القتال - أي : المنافقين - (وَقَالُوا رَبَّنَا لِمَ كَنَبَتَ عَلَيْنَا الْفِنَالَ لَوْلَا أَخَرْلَنَا إِلَى أَجَلٍ قَرِيبٍ) فقال تعالى : قُل مَنَعُ الدُّنْيَا قَلِيلٌ ) أي : لستم تريدون البقاء إلا لمتاع الدنيا ، أما الزاهدون المحبون الله تعالى .. فقاتلوا في سبيل الله كأنهم بنيان مرصوص، وانتظروا إحدى الحسنيين ، وكانوا إذا . دعوا إلى القتال .. يستنشقون رائحة الجنة يستنشقون رائحة الجنة ، ويبادرون إليه مبادرة الظمآن إلى الماء البارد ... وهكذا كان حال الصادقين في الإيمان ، الذين تركوا تمتع عشرين سنة مثلاً أو ثلاثين سنة بتمتع الأبد ، واستبشروا ببيعهم الذي بايعوا به أما المنافقون .. ففروا من الزحف ؛ خوفاً من الموت ، فإيثارهم البقاء على الشهادة استبدال الذي هو أدنى بالذي هو خير )(١) (۱) إحياء علوم الدين ( ٢٢٧/٤ ) بتصرف ، وانظر ( البطولة والفداء عند الصوفية : ( ص ١٦٩ ) .
Accusation of laziness and reluctance for jihad: The political situation was not better than the intellectual situation. The era of Imam al-Ghazali knew no reverence for the caliphate, not to mention the existence of a caliphate in Egypt and another in al-Andalus besides the nominal caliphate of Baghdad. Reading the era clarifies for us the extent of the rampant ailments that created the factors of defeat, foremost among them fragmentation, division, and internal decay. With all this, and with the fall of the most prominent Islamic cities like Jerusalem, Tripoli, and many of the Syrian coast towns... the problem was not a problem of numbers and equipment; therefore, the call for general mobilization that obliges every individual to bear arms was not announced by the caliph. But how was the condition of the people then? The tableau of the era confirms the existence of destructive internal conflicts, struggles over chairs of power, cowardly assassinations, blazing hatreds, moral corruption, and deviation from the sound methodology. We may ask: What laziness is this that produces 'Ihya Ulum al-Din'? Forty books that shape men and disturb the resting places of illusions, and other books and epistles that the hand of the Imam crafted to reform the nation and prepare the generation of Nur al-Din and Saladin. Then what laziness is this that the Imam lived, with a staff, a patched cloak, a waterskin, and a harshness of life that the critic would wish death to come upon him to escape its severity and blows?! Then what is the Imam's opinion and position on jihad? Professor As'ad al-Khatib transmits scattered texts from Imam al-Ghazali in several of his books that demonstrate the Imam's purity and innocence from this foolish accusation. Among them: 'The result is that asceticism is the desire for not remaining in this world... Therefore, when fighting was prescribed for them—i.e., the hypocrites—they said: "Our Lord, why have You prescribed fighting for us? If only You would delay us for a short term." So the Exalted said: "Say: The enjoyment of this world is little." That is, you only want to remain for the enjoyment of this world. As for the ascetics who love God... they fought in the path of God as if they were a well-built structure, and they awaited one of the two good things, and when they were called to fight, they inhaled the fragrance of Paradise, and they rushed to it as the thirsty rush to cold water. Thus was the state of the truthful in faith, who left the enjoyment of twenty or thirty years, for example, for the enjoyment of eternity, and they rejoiced in their transaction which they made. As for the hypocrites, they fled from the battle out of fear of death, so their preference for remaining over martyrdom is exchanging that which is lower for that which is better.' (1) (1) Ihya Ulum al-Din, 4/227, adapted; see also al-Batala wa al-Fida' ind al-Sufiyya, p. 169.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage refutes the accusation that Ghazali was sluggish and avoided jihad. The author first sets the political context: the situation was dire, with loss of Jerusalem and other cities, but the problem was not numbers or equipment; rather, internal strife and moral decay. Then he asks rhetorically: what laziness could produce a work like Ihya? Ghazali's forty books shaped men and dispelled illusions. Then he describes Ghazali's own ascetic life with a staff, patched cloak, and water-skin, a life so harsh that critics would prefer death. Finally, the author cites Ghazali's own words on jihad from Ihya, where he explains that true ascetics are not those who avoid fighting but those who fight eagerly for the sake of God, desiring martyrdom. Ghazali contrasts the believers who rush to battle with hypocrites who flee due to love of this world. This shows that Ghazali exalted jihad and did not neglect it.

Key Terms
الجهاد (jihad)
الزهد (asceticism)
إحياء علوم الدين (Ihya Ulum al-Din)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali was a pacifist who neglected jihad
Correction: Ghazali's writings in Ihya show that he praised ascetics who fight eagerly for God, and his own life of asceticism and scholarship produced works that inspired jihad leaders.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that Ghazali neglected jihad?
A) By showing Ghazali's military records.
B) By quoting Ghazali's writings that exalt jihad and describing his ascetic lifestyle.
C) By noting that Ghazali led armies.
D) By blaming the political situation on Ghazali.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's own words in Ihya demonstrate that true ascetics eagerly fight for God, directly refuting any accusation of neglecting jihad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely never been accused of neglecting jihad. But what areas of your life are you using as an excuse for spiritual laziness while you distract yourself with busy work? How are you like those who, as Ghazali says, prefer remaining in this world over the hereafter? Commit to one change today.
?
The author says the problem was not numbers and equipment but internal decay. What internal decay in your heart—pride, envy, love of comfort—is making you weak in your own jihad against your nafs? Name it and make a plan to uproot it.
?
Ghazali's asceticism was not passive but produced works that shaped generations. What are you producing with your life? Are you living comfortably while the Ummah suffers, or are you sacrificing comfort for something lasting?
